Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
Possibility vs. Actuality: The Refutation of Sophistry
Distinguishing possibility from actuality to refute sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why deeming the existence of unseen mountains possible is not sophistry, based on the distinction between possibility and actuality.

Core Idea
Possibility does not entail actuality, nor does it contradict certainty of non-existence.

The author begins by addressing a common objection: if we deny the existence of unseen mountains, aren't we falling into sophistry? He clarifies that merely deeming something possible does not mean affirming its existence. This sets the foundation for a rational approach to unseen matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic text from the beginning up to 'فمجرد تجويزها لا يكون سفسطة'. Understand the author's claim that possibility is not sophistry.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the author distinguishes between possibility (jawāz) and actual occurrence (wuqūʿ). He argues that possibility does not imply actuality.
Also note that possibility does not oppose certainty of non-existence.
3
Apply to the mountain example
The author applies this to the case of unseen mountains: we can deem their existence possible while being certain they do not exist. This is not contradictory.
This resolves the sophistry objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوعها ولا سفسطة هاهنا فكذا الحال في الجبال الشاهقة التي لا نراها فإنا نجوز وجودها ونجزم بعدمها وذلك لأن الجواز لا يستلزم الوقوع ولا ينافي الجزم بعدمها فمجرد تجويزها لا يكون سفسطة ( ثم) نقول : ( إن كان مأخذ الجزم بعدم الجبل) المذكور ( ما ذكرتم ) من وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها ( لوجب أن لا نجزم به إلا بعد العلم بهذا واللازم باطل لأنه يجزم به من يجزم به من لا يخطر بباله هذه المسألة، ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون ذلك الجزم ( نظرياً) مع اتفاق الكل على كونه ضرورياً، وأما ثانياً : فهو القياس الاستثنائي وهي أن اللازم باطل مستنداً بلزوم مثله في جملة العاديات مع أنه لا سفسطة فيه قطعاً لكن فيه بحث لجواز أن يريد المستدل بالتجويز أن يكون محتملاً عندنا كما في الظن، وحينئذ لا يرد النقض بالعاديات إذ لا يجوز نقائضها تجويز الطرف الآخر في الظن، بل الجواب حينئذ منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا إلخ، واعترض أيضاً بأن قولهم : يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة لا نراها يحتمل معنيين أحدهما تجويز ثبوتها في الواقع ونحن لا نراها والثاني تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها ولزوم الأول للشرط اتفاقي لا عقلي ولزوم الثاني عقلي للشركة في تمام علة الرؤية، فإذا لم ير أحدهما مع تمام علته لم يرد الآخر لة في الشرط والظاهر أن هذا هو مراد المستدل فلا نقض بالعاديات والمخلص عنه منع تمام العلة وأنت خبير بأن المتبادر من العبارة هو الاحتمال الأول ولذا سبق ظاهر كلام الشارح عليه، ثم إن النقض على الاحتمال الثاني منعات أيضاً، إذ لا سفسطة في نفس تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها كما لا سفسطة في تجويز نقائض العاديات وإن جزمنا بانتفاء المجوز في الصورتين فليتأمل. قوله : ( لوجب أن لا نجزم إلخ) اعترض عليه الأبهري بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستنداً إلى شيء آخر وجوب عدم حصول العلم به إلا بعد العلم بذلك الآخر لجواز أن يوجد طرق شتى يحصل العلم به بكل من تلك الطرق كيف ولا يلزم من نفي الدليل المعني نفي المدلول وجوابه أن الاستدلال يتوقف على انحصار مأخذ الجزم فيما ذكر إذ لو لم ينحصر لم يلزم من انتفاء ذلك المأخذ تجويز الجبال المذكورة لجواز أن نجزم بعدمها من طريق آخر. قوله : ( ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون نظرياً ) إن قلت : يجوز أن يكون الأخذ بطريق الحدث فلا يلزم النظرية قلت : هي ليست من الحدسيات العامة لأنها مشروطة بتكرر المشاهدة وهذه ليست كذلك وليست من الحدسيات الخاصة أي بالنسبة إلى من له قوة قدسية لأنها عامة لكل أهل عادة من العوام والصبيان على أنه بعد تسلمه لا يقدح في لزوم نظريته بالنسبة إلى العامة وهي التي ادعى بطلانها بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإن رد بحصول الجزم بها لمن لا يخطر بباله ذلك القياس رجع إلى الوجه الأول، وأما الاعتراض بجواز أن يكون الحكم ضرورياً ولميته نظرية كإدراك البصر فإنه ضروري وقد عللوه بخروج الشعاع من البصر أو بانطباع الصورة واحتجوا عليه فجوابه أن المراد إن كان لمية نفس الحكم العقلي، فذلك يقتضي نظريته وإن كان المراد لمية متعلق الحكم، أعني انعدام الجبل فلميته عدم خلق الله تعالى إياه وليس المذكور في البيان هذا فليتأمل.
Its occurrence and there is no sophistry here, so likewise is the case with the lofty mountains that we do not see; for we deem their existence possible and we are certain of their non-existence. That is because possibility does not entail occurrence nor does it oppose certainty of non-existence. So mere deeming them possible is not sophistry. (Then) we say: (If the basis for certainty of non-existence of the mentioned mountain) (is what you mentioned) from the necessity of vision when its conditions are met, (then it would be necessary that we not be certain of it except after knowledge of this. But the consequent is false, because those who are certain of it are certain without this issue crossing their minds, and because it would lead to that certainty being (theoretical) despite universal agreement that it is necessary. And secondly, it is the exceptional syllogism, namely that the consequent is false, based on the necessity of a similar case in the whole class of ordinary happenings with which there is definitely no sophistry. But there is discussion in it due to the possibility that the arguer intends by 'deeming possible' that it is probable for us as in opinion. Then the rebuttal by ordinary happenings does not apply, because their opposites are not deemed possible by deeming the other side in opinion. Rather, the answer then is to deny the implication derived from his statement: 'Otherwise it would be possible for it to be in our presence etc.' It was also objected that their statement: 'It entails deeming possible lofty mountains we do not see' admits two meanings: one, deeming their existence in reality while we do not see them; the second, deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence. The entailment of the first is conventional, not rational, and the entailment of the second is rational due to sharing the complete cause of vision. So if one of them is not seen despite its complete cause, the other is not entailed regarding the condition. And it appears that this is what the arguer intends, so there is no rebuttal by ordinary happenings. The escape from it is to deny the completeness of the cause. And you are aware that what is immediately understood from the expression is the first meaning, hence the apparent statement of the commentator preceded it. Then the rebuttal on the second meaning is also prevented, for there is no sophistry in the very deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence, just as there is no sophistry in deeming the opposites of ordinary happenings, even if we are certain of the non-existence of the permitting factor in both cases—so reflect. His statement: (Then it would be necessary that we not be certain etc.) al-Abhari objected to it: 'That something is based on something else does not entail that knowledge of it is not obtained except after knowledge of that other, because it is possible that there exist multiple ways by which knowledge of it is obtained through each of those ways, and how can it be that negating the intended evidence necessitates negating the conclusion?' Its answer: 'The inference depends on the exclusivity of the basis of certainty to what was mentioned, because if it were not exclusive, then the non-existence of that basis would not entail deeming the mentioned mountains possible, because it is possible that we are certain of their non-existence through another way.' His statement: (And because it leads to it being theoretical) If you say: 'It is possible that the method is through intuition, so the theoretical nature does not follow.' I say: 'It is not among the general intuitions because they are conditioned by repeated observation, and this is not such; nor is it among the special intuitions (i.e., relative to one with a holy power) because it is common to all people of habit, laymen and children. Moreover, even if it were granted, it does not harm the necessity of its being theoretical for the common people, which is what was claimed to be false. There remains discussion in it: namely, that it may be among the innate propositions through analogy. If it is rebutted by the occurrence of certainty in those for whom that analogy does not cross their minds, then it returns to the first point. As for the objection that the judgment may be necessary while its reason is theoretical, like visual perception—it is necessary and they have explained it by the emission of rays from the eye or the impression of the image and argued for it—its answer is: if what is meant is the reason of the rational judgment itself, then that entails its theoretical nature; but if what is meant is the reason of the object of the judgment, i.e., the non-existence of the mountain, then its reason is God's not creating it, and this is not mentioned in the explanation—so reflect.'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that denying the existence of unseen mountains would be sophistry. The author argues that possibility does not imply actuality, and the certainty of non-existence is necessary, not theoretical. He then analyzes the objection through an exceptional syllogism and discusses various nuances about the meaning of 'deeming possible' and the exclusivity of the basis of certainty. The commentary proceeds step by step, first establishing that mere possibility is not sophistry, then refuting the claim that the certainty would have to be based on the condition of vision, then addressing the objection that it would make the judgment theoretical, and finally discussing the possibility of multiple ways of knowing.

Key Terms
سفسطة
تجويز
جزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deeming something possible is itself a form of sophistry or doubt.
Correction: Possibility is a rational judgment that does not entail actuality; it can coexist with certainty of non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that deeming the existence of unseen mountains possible is not sophistry?
A) Because possibility necessitates occurrence
B) Because possibility does not entail occurrence nor oppose certainty of non-existence
C) Because we have no proof of their existence
D) Because sophistry only applies to self-evident matters

Key Takeaway
Possibility and certainty can coexist; mere possibility is not sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a belief you hold with absolute certainty—such as the existence of God or the truth of Islam. When a doubt or a 'what if' crosses your mind, do you feel threatened? How does this lesson's insight—that possibility does not oppose certainty—change how you respond to such passing thoughts? Today, when a doubt arises, instead of suppressing it, acknowledge it as a mere possibility and reaffirm your certainty without fear.
?
Have you ever been accused of inconsistency or sophistry because you held two positions that seemed contradictory? Perhaps you affirm the oneness of God yet struggle with the problem of evil. How does the author's example of the mountains free you from the need to resolve every apparent contradiction in this life? Can you hold both the possibility of unseen evils and the certainty of God's wisdom without sophistry? Write down one such pair of beliefs and commit to not letting the tension undermine your faith.
?
Is there a truth you know to be real—like the Day of Judgment—that you sometimes act as if it were only a possibility? The lesson distinguishes between deeming possible and being certain. Examine your daily actions: do you live with the certainty of the Hereafter, or have you reduced it to a mere possibility? This is not a theoretical question: your life depends on it. What will you change today to align your actions with your certainty?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Certainty of Non-Existence: Necessary or Theoretical?
Arguing that certainty of non-existence is necessary, not theoretical, using an exceptional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the certainty of the non-existence of unseen mountains is necessary (darurī) rather than theoretical (naẓarī), and how the exceptional syllogism refutes the objection.

Core Idea
Certainty of non-existence is necessary, not derived from a condition of vision.

The author continues by examining the basis of our certainty that unseen mountains do not exist. The opponent argues that if we do not see them, we cannot be certain. The author responds by showing that such certainty is a necessary judgment, not a theoretical one, and that the opponent's own logic leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on certainty
Read from '( ثم) نقول : ( إن كان مأخذ الجزم بعدم الجبل)' up to the discussion of the exceptional syllogism (قياس استثنائي).
2
Understand the opponent's assumption
The opponent assumes that certainty of non-existence depends on the condition of vision. The author shows this leads to a problem: then only those who know the condition can be certain, but common people are certain without it.
Also, it would make the certainty theoretical, but it is universally agreed to be necessary.
3
Analyze the exceptional syllogism
The author uses an exceptional syllogism: if the opponent's view were true, then similar cases (like not seeing ordinary objects) would also lead to sophistry, which is false.
This shows the opponent's view is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يكون بحضرتنا إلخ، واعترض أيضاً بأن قولهم : يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة لا نراها يحتمل معنيين أحدهما تجويز ثبوتها في الواقع ونحن لا نراها والثاني تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها ولزوم الأول للشرط اتفاقي العاديات وإن جزمنا بانتفاء المجوز في الصورتين فليتأمل. قوله : ( لوجب أن لا نجزم إلخ) اعترض عليه الأبهري بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستنداً إلى شيء آخر وجوب عدم حصول العلم به إلا بعد العلم بذلك الآخر لجواز أن يوجد طرق شتى يحصل العلم به بكل من تلك الطرق كيف ولا يلزم من نفي الدليل المعني نفي المدلول وجوابه أن الاستدلال يتوقف على انحصار مأخذ الجزم فيما ذكر إذ لو لم ينحصر لم يلزم من انتفاء ذلك المأخذ تجويز الجبال المذكورة لجواز أن نجزم بعدمها من طريق آخر. قوله : ( ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون نظرياً ) إن قلت : يجوز أن يكون الأخذ بطريق الحدث فلا يلزم النظرية قلت : هي ليست من الحدسيات العامة لأنها مشروطة بتكرر المشاهدة وهذه ليست كذلك وليست من الحدسيات الخاصة أي بالنسبة إلى من له قوة قدسية لأنها عامة لكل أهل عادة من العوام والصبيان على أنه بعد تسلمه لا يقدح في لزوم نظريته بالنسبة إلى العامة وهي التي ادعى بطلانها بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإن رد بحصول الجزم بها لمن لا يخطر بباله ذلك القياس رجع إلى الوجه الأول، وأما الاعتراض بجواز أن يكون الحكم ضرورياً ولميته نظرية كإدراك البصر فإنه ضروري وقد عللوه بخروج الشعاع من البصر أو بانطباع الصورة واحتجوا عليه فجوابه أن المراد إن كان لمية نفس الحكم العقلي، فذلك يقتضي نظريته وإن كان المراد لمية متعلق الحكم، أعني انعدام الجبل فلميته عدم خلق الله تعالى إياه وليس المذكور في البيان هذا فليتأمل.
Its occurrence and there is no sophistry here, so likewise is the case with the lofty mountains that we do not see; for we deem their existence possible and we are certain of their non-existence. That is because possibility does not entail occurrence nor does it oppose certainty of non-existence. So mere deeming them possible is not sophistry. (Then) we say: (If the basis for certainty of non-existence of the mentioned mountain) (is what you mentioned) from the necessity of vision when its conditions are met, (then it would be necessary that we not be certain of it except after knowledge of this. But the consequent is false, because those who are certain of it are certain without this issue crossing their minds, and because it would lead to that certainty being (theoretical) despite universal agreement that it is necessary. And secondly, it is the exceptional syllogism, namely that the consequent is false, based on the necessity of a similar case in the whole class of ordinary happenings with which there is definitely no sophistry. But there is discussion in it due to the possibility that the arguer intends by 'deeming possible' that it is probable for us as in opinion. Then the rebuttal by ordinary happenings does not apply, because their opposites are not deemed possible by deeming the other side in opinion. Rather, the answer then is to deny the implication derived from his statement: 'Otherwise it would be possible for it to be in our presence etc.' It was also objected that their statement: 'It entails deeming possible lofty mountains we do not see' admits two meanings: one, deeming their existence in reality while we do not see them; the second, deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence. The entailment of the first is conventional, not rational, and the entailment of the second is rational due to sharing the complete cause of vision. So if one of them is not seen despite its complete cause, the other is not entailed regarding the condition. And it appears that this is what the arguer intends, so there is no rebuttal by ordinary happenings. The escape from it is to deny the completeness of the cause. And you are aware that what is immediately understood from the expression is the first meaning, hence the apparent statement of the commentator preceded it. Then the rebuttal on the second meaning is also prevented, for there is no sophistry in the very deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence, just as there is no sophistry in deeming the opposites of ordinary happenings, even if we are certain of the non-existence of the permitting factor in both cases—so reflect. His statement: (Then it would be necessary that we not be certain etc.) al-Abhari objected to it: 'That something is based on something else does not entail that knowledge of it is not obtained except after knowledge of that other, because it is possible that there exist multiple ways by which knowledge of it is obtained through each of those ways, and how can it be that negating the intended evidence necessitates negating the conclusion?' Its answer: 'The inference depends on the exclusivity of the basis of certainty to what was mentioned, because if it were not exclusive, then the non-existence of that basis would not entail deeming the mentioned mountains possible, because it is possible that we are certain of their non-existence through another way.' His statement: (And because it leads to it being theoretical) If you say: 'It is possible that the method is through intuition, so the theoretical nature does not follow.' I say: 'It is not among the general intuitions because they are conditioned by repeated observation, and this is not such; nor is it among the special intuitions (i.e., relative to one with a holy power) because it is common to all people of habit, laymen and children. Moreover, even if it were granted, it does not harm the necessity of its being theoretical for the common people, which is what was claimed to be false. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that denying the existence of unseen mountains would be sophistry. The author argues that possibility does not imply actuality, and the certainty of non-existence is necessary, not theoretical. He then analyzes the objection through an exceptional syllogism and discusses various nuances about the meaning of 'deeming possible' and the exclusivity of the basis of certainty. The commentary proceeds step by step, first establishing that mere possibility is not sophistry, then refuting the claim that the certainty would have to be based on the condition of vision, then addressing the objection that it would make the judgment theoretical, and finally discussing the possibility of multiple ways of knowing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Certainty about unseen mountains is a theoretical inference based on the condition of vision.
Correction: It is a necessary judgment, not derived from a condition, as shown by universal human certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument the author uses against the opponent?
A) The judgment would be necessary, not theoretical
B) The opponent's view leads to an infinite regress
C) The same logic would apply to ordinary objects, leading to an absurdity
D) The condition of vision is not sufficient

Key Takeaway
Our certainty about unseen mountains is necessary and not dependent on the condition of vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are absolutely certain that something does not exist (e.g., that you did not commit a particular sin), is that certainty immediate or do you reason it out? How does this lesson expose a hidden assumption you may have held about the nature of certainty? What does it ask you to trust about your innate knowledge?
?
This passage argues that some certainties are necessary and not theoretical. Is there a belief about Allah's existence or attributes that you hold as necessary? Have you ever allowed excessive reasoning to weaken that certainty? How can you protect the necessary nature of that belief?
?
Consider the second argument: if the opponent's view were applied to ordinary matters, it would lead to sophistry. In your own life, does overthinking mundane certainties ever lead to doubt or confusion? What step can you take today to embrace necessary knowledge without over-analysis?

advanced

Nuances of 'Deeming Possible' and the Basis of Certainty
Analyzing the meanings of possibility and the exclusivity of the basis of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'deeming possible' and how the exclusivity of the basis of certainty affects the argument.

Core Idea
The term 'deeming possible' can mean either deeming existence or deeming non-vision, and the basis of certainty must be exclusive.

The author delves into a more refined analysis of the opponent's objection. He identifies two possible interpretations of 'deeming possible unseen mountains' and discusses the implications for the exceptional syllogism. He also addresses al-Abhari's criticism about multiple ways of knowing and the nature of the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed analysis
Read the passage from 'وأما ثانياً : فهو القياس الاستثنائي' to the end, focusing on the discussion of the two meanings and the response to al-Abhari.
2
Learn the two meanings
The author explains that 'deeming possible' can mean either (1) deeming existence in reality, or (2) deeming non-vision on the supposition of existence. Only the second is rational.
This affects whether the exceptional syllogism works.
3
Examine the exclusivity condition
Al-Abhari objects that negating one basis does not negate the judgment if there are other bases. The author responds that the argument assumes exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوعها ولا سفسطة هاهنا فكذا الحال في الجبال الشاهقة التي لا نراها فإنا نجوز وجودها ونجزم بعدمها وذلك لأن الجواز لا يستلزم الوقوع ولا ينافي الجزم بعدمها فمجرد تجويزها لا يكون سفسطة ( ثم) نقول : ( إن كان مأخذ الجزم بعدم الجبل) المذكور ( ما ذكرتم ) من وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها ( لوجب أن لا نجزم به إلا بعد العلم بهذا واللازم باطل لأنه يجزم به من يجزم به من لا يخطر بباله هذه المسألة، ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون ذلك الجزم ( نظرياً) مع اتفاق الكل على كونه ضرورياً، وأما ثانياً : فهو القياس الاستثنائي وهي أن اللازم باطل مستنداً بلزوم مثله في جملة العاديات مع أنه لا سفسطة فيه قطعاً لكن فيه بحث لجواز أن يريد المستدل بالتجويز أن يكون محتملاً عندنا كما في الظن، وحينئذ لا يرد النقض بالعاديات إذ لا يجوز نقائضها تجويز الطرف الآخر في الظن، بل الجواب حينئذ منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا إلخ، واعترض أيضاً بأن قولهم : يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة لا نراها يحتمل معنيين أحدهما تجويز ثبوتها في الواقع ونحن لا نراها والثاني تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها ولزوم الأول للشرط اتفاقي لا عقلي ولزوم الثاني عقلي للشركة في تمام علة الرؤية، فإذا لم ير أحدهما مع تمام علته لم يرد الآخر لة في الشرط والظاهر أن هذا هو مراد المستدل فلا نقض بالعاديات والمخلص عنه منع تمام العلة وأنت خبير بأن المتبادر من العبارة هو الاحتمال الأول ولذا سبق ظاهر كلام الشارح عليه، ثم إن النقض على الاحتمال الثاني منعات أيضاً، إذ لا سفسطة في نفس تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها كما لا سفسطة في تجويز نقائض العاديات وإن جزمنا بانتفاء المجوز في الصورتين فليتأمل. قوله : ( لوجب أن لا نجزم إلخ) اعترض عليه الأبهري بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستنداً إلى شيء آخر وجوب عدم حصول العلم به إلا بعد العلم بذلك الآخر لجواز أن يوجد طرق شتى يحصل العلم به بكل من تلك الطرق كيف ولا يلزم من نفي الدليل المعني نفي المدلول وجوابه أن الاستدلال يتوقف على انحصار مأخذ الجزم فيما ذكر إذ لو لم ينحصر لم يلزم من انتفاء ذلك المأخذ تجويز الجبال المذكورة لجواز أن نجزم بعدمها من طريق آخر. قوله : ( ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون نظرياً ) إن قلت : يجوز أن يكون الأخذ بطريق الحدث فلا يلزم النظرية قلت : هي ليست من الحدسيات العامة لأنها مشروطة بتكرر المشاهدة وهذه ليست كذلك وليست من الحدسيات الخاصة أي بالنسبة إلى من له قوة قدسية لأنها عامة لكل أهل عادة من العوام والصبيان على أنه بعد تسلمه لا يقدح في لزوم نظريته بالنسبة إلى العامة وهي التي ادعى بطلانها بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإن رد بحصول الجزم بها لمن لا يخطر بباله ذلك القياس رجع إلى الوجه الأول، وأما الاعتراض بجواز أن يكون الحكم ضرورياً ولميته نظرية كإدراك البصر فإنه ضروري وقد عللوه بخروج الشعاع من البصر أو بانطباع الصورة واحتجوا عليه فجوابه أن المراد إن كان لمية نفس الحكم العقلي، فذلك يقتضي نظريته وإن كان المراد لمية متعلق الحكم، أعني انعدام الجبل فلميته عدم خلق الله تعالى إياه وليس المذكور في البيان هذا فليتأمل.
Its occurrence and there is no sophistry here, so likewise is the case with the lofty mountains that we do not see; for we deem their existence possible and we are certain of their non-existence. That is because possibility does not entail occurrence nor does it oppose certainty of non-existence. So mere deeming them possible is not sophistry. (Then) we say: (If the basis for certainty of non-existence of the mentioned mountain) (is what you mentioned) from the necessity of vision when its conditions are met, (then it would be necessary that we not be certain of it except after knowledge of this. But the consequent is false, because those who are certain of it are certain without this issue crossing their minds, and because it would lead to that certainty being (theoretical) despite universal agreement that it is necessary. And secondly, it is the exceptional syllogism, namely that the consequent is false, based on the necessity of a similar case in the whole class of ordinary happenings with which there is definitely no sophistry. But there is discussion in it due to the possibility that the arguer intends by 'deeming possible' that it is probable for us as in opinion. Then the rebuttal by ordinary happenings does not apply, because their opposites are not deemed possible by deeming the other side in opinion. Rather, the answer then is to deny the implication derived from his statement: 'Otherwise it would be possible for it to be in our presence etc.' It was also objected that their statement: 'It entails deeming possible lofty mountains we do not see' admits two meanings: one, deeming their existence in reality while we do not see them; the second, deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence. The entailment of the first is conventional, not rational, and the entailment of the second is rational due to sharing the complete cause of vision. So if one of them is not seen despite its complete cause, the other is not entailed regarding the condition. And it appears that this is what the arguer intends, so there is no rebuttal by ordinary happenings. The escape from it is to deny the completeness of the cause. And you are aware that what is immediately understood from the expression is the first meaning, hence the apparent statement of the commentator preceded it. Then the rebuttal on the second meaning is also prevented, for there is no sophistry in the very deeming that we do not see them on the supposition of their existence, just as there is no sophistry in deeming the opposites of ordinary happenings, even if we are certain of the non-existence of the permitting factor in both cases—so reflect. His statement: (Then it would be necessary that we not be certain etc.) al-Abhari objected to it: 'That something is based on something else does not entail that knowledge of it is not obtained except after knowledge of that other, because it is possible that there exist multiple ways by which knowledge of it is obtained through each of those ways, and how can it be that negating the intended evidence necessitates negating the conclusion?' Its answer: 'The inference depends on the exclusivity of the basis of certainty to what was mentioned, because if it were not exclusive, then the non-existence of that basis would not entail deeming the mentioned mountains possible, because it is possible that we are certain of their non-existence through another way.' His statement: (And because it leads to it being theoretical) If you say: 'It is possible that the method is through intuition, so the theoretical nature does not follow.' I say: 'It is not among the general intuitions because they are conditioned by repeated observation, and this is not such; nor is it among the special intuitions (i.e., relative to one with a holy power) because it is common to all people of habit, laymen and children. Moreover, even if it were granted, it does not harm the necessity of its being theoretical for the common people, which is what was claimed to be false. There remains discussion in it: namely, that it may be among the innate propositions through analogy. If it is rebutted by the occurrence of certainty in those for whom that analogy does not cross their minds, then it returns to the first point. As for the objection that the judgment may be necessary while its reason is theoretical, like visual perception—it is necessary and they have explained it by the emission of rays from the eye or the impression of the image and argued for it—its answer is: if what is meant is the reason of the rational judgment itself, then that entails its theoretical nature; but if what is meant is the reason of the object of the judgment, i.e., the non-existence of the mountain, then its reason is God's not creating it, and this is not mentioned in the explanation—so reflect.'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that denying the existence of unseen mountains would be sophistry. The author argues that possibility does not imply actuality, and the certainty of non-existence is necessary, not theoretical. He then analyzes the objection through an exceptional syllogism and discusses various nuances about the meaning of 'deeming possible' and the exclusivity of the basis of certainty. The commentary proceeds step by step, first establishing that mere possibility is not sophistry, then refuting the claim that the certainty would have to be based on the condition of vision, then addressing the objection that it would make the judgment theoretical, and finally discussing the possibility of multiple ways of knowing.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is only one meaning of 'deeming possible' in this context.
Correction: The term can refer to deeming existence or deeming non-vision, each with different implications.

B) Deeming non-vision on the supposition of existence, which does not lead to sophistry
C) Deeming probability in opinion, which makes the exceptional syllogism invalid

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Logical Ambiguity of 'لا تدركه الأبصار'
The author analyzes the logical form of the verse to show its ambiguity and potential support for the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical form of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' can be interpreted in two ways, and why this ambiguity undermines the argument that the verse negates the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
A verse's logical form can be ambiguous—here, it may imply that some eyes do see Allah.

The verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' is often used to argue that Allah cannot be seen. However, the author of Sharh al-Mawaqif shows that the logical structure of the statement is not straightforward. By analyzing whether the negation applies to a universal or particular claim, he reveals that the verse might actually support the possibility of vision. Let's explore this logical maneuver.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical categories
Recall the four logical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. The verse's subject is a plural with 'lam', which often indicates generality.
The negation of a universal affirmative is a particular negative.
2
Consider the two interpretations
The author presents two ways to parse the negation: either it negates the entire universal (making it universal negative) or it negates the predicate for the whole subject (making it particular negative).
The order of operation matters: negation first then generality, or generality first then negation.
3
Recognize the implication of the second interpretation
If the verse is a particular negative (some sights do not perceive Him), then by the principle of opposite implication, it implies that some sights do perceive Him.
This turns the verse into a proof for the possibility of vision.
4
Examine the reversal argument
If the 'lam' is not for generality, the proposition becomes an absolute negative, which has the force of a particular. The author then shows how this also supports the Ash'ari position.
The commentary discusses objections regarding synonymity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من وجوه الجواب أن تدركه الأبصار موجبة كلية لأن موضوعها جمع محلي باللازم الاستغراقية وقد دخل عليها النفي فرفعها ورفع الموجبة الكلية سالبة جزئية وبالجملة فيحتمل قوله لا تدركه الأبصار إسناد النفي إلى الكل بأن يلاحظ أولا دخول النفي، ثم ورود العموم عليه فيكون سالبة كلية ونفي الإسناد إلى الكل بأن يعتبر العموم أولاً، ثم ورود النفي عليه فيكون سالبة جزئية ومع احتمال المعنى الثاني لم يبق فيه حجة لكم علينا لأن أبصار الكفار لا تدركه إجماعاً. هذا ما نقوله : لو ثبت أن اللام في الجمع للعموم.

والاستغراق وإلا عكسنا القضية وقلنا : لا تدركه الأبصار سالبة مهملة في قوة الجزئية، فالمعنى لا تدركه بعض الأبصار وتخصيص البعض بالنفي يدل بالمفهوم على الإثبات للبعض فالآية حجة لنا لا علينا.

قوله : ( عكسنا القضية أي ندعي أن الآية حجة لنا لا علينا كما ادعاه الخصم. قوله: فالمعنى لا تدركه بعض الأبصار إنما يستقيم إذا كانت المهملة مرادفة للجزئية وكونها في قوتها لا يفيد المرادفة، ولهذا اعترض عليه بأن الجنس في حيز النفي يفيد العموم اتفاقا نحو ما جاني الرجل وإنما الاحتمال لعموم السلب وسلب العموم عند قصد الاستغراق فكيف بعكس القضية على تقدير حمل اللام على الجنس ولو ثبتت المرادفة لاندفع الاعتراض إذ الآية حينئذ حجة إلزامية حيث يرجع قيد البعضية إلى النفي كما أرجع المستدل قيد العموم على تقدير الاستغراق إليه فتأمل. قوله : (بالمفهوم) أي بمفهوم المخالفة.
The second of the directions of the answer: that 'tudrikuhu al-absar' (sight perceives Him) is a universal affirmative because its subject is a plural modified by what necessitates generality. Negation entered upon it and negated it, and the negation of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. In sum, the statement 'Sight does not perceive Him' can be interpreted as [1] attributing negation to all, meaning negation enters first then generality comes, making it a universal negative; or [2] attributing negation to all, meaning generality is considered first then negation comes, making it a particular negative. With the possibility of the second meaning, there is no proof for you against us, because the sight of disbelievers does not perceive Him by consensus. This is what we say: if it is established that the lam in the plural is for generality.

And if not, we reverse the proposition and say: 'Sight does not perceive Him' is an absolute negative which has the force of a particular negative, so the meaning is 'some sights do not perceive Him.' And specifying some with negation indicates by implication (mafhum) affirmation for others, so the verse is a proof for us, not against us.

He said: 'We reverse the proposition' meaning we claim that the verse is a proof for us not against us as the opponent claimed. He said: 'So the meaning is that some sights do not perceive Him' is only valid if the absolute is synonymous with the particular, but being in its force does not give synonymity. Hence it is objected that the genus in the scope of negation gives generality by agreement, as in 'ma jani al-rajulu' (no man came). The probability is only for the generality of negation or negation of generality when intending generality. So how can the proposition be reversed on the assumption that the lam is for genus? And if synonymity were established, the objection would be repelled, for then the verse is an obligatory proof because the restriction of some-ness goes to the negation as the supporter took the restriction of generality to it on the assumption of generality. So ponder. He said: 'by mafhum' i.e., the opposite implication.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage gives the second response, analyzing the logical form of the verse. It explains that the phrase 'لا تدركه الأبصار' can be read either as a universal negative (no sight perceives Him) or a particular negative (some sights do not perceive Him). Since the verse is ambiguous, it cannot be used as a proof against the possibility of vision. Moreover, if read as a particular negative, it implies that some sights do perceive Him, thus supporting the Ash'ari position. The commentary discusses the logical subtleties of generality and the reversal argument.

سالبة جزئية
الاستغراق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a clear universal negative ruling out any vision.
Correction: The author shows it can be interpreted as a particular negative, which actually implies some vision is possible.

Checkpoint

A) No eyes see Him
B) Some eyes do see Him
C) All eyes see Him
D) The verse is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is logically ambiguous and can be interpreted to support the possibility of seeing Allah.

?

?

Can We Know God's Essence?
Sanusi presents the dispute over knowing God's essence and refutes the philosophers' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over whether God's essence can be known, the philosophers' argument for its impossibility, and the kalam answer that denies the exhaustive enumeration of means of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God's essence is possible because the means of knowing are not limited to intuition and definition.

This passage presents a key theological debate: can human beings know God's essence? The philosophers argue it is impossible, basing their case on a strict dichotomy of knowledge. Imam al-Sanusi (author of the commentary) refutes this by denying the completeness of their classification. Read on to see how the argument unfolds and why the kalam tradition affirms the possibility of knowing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the full Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: positions, philosophers' proof, answer, objection, and resolution.
The passage uses technical terms; keep them in mind.
2
Identify the philosophers' proof
Find the dichotomy: intuition or reflection (which is either description or definition). Conclude that God's essence is neither intuitive nor definable, so unknowable.
This is the core of the objection.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer denies the exhaustive enumeration. Two points: 1) God may create knowledge directly in a person; 2) description (rasm) might convey the essence even if not required to.
These are the counterarguments.
4
Consider the counterobjection and resolution
A further objection says this answer is too universal and could apply to impossibles. The author hints that denying the exhaustive enumeration suffices if we redefine 'intuitive' relative to the generality of people.
This shows depth of the debate.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this matters: if God's essence were unknowable, theology would be severely limited. The kalam tradition defends the possibility, which grounds our knowledge of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : الجواز وفي جواز العلم بحقيقة الله تعالى خلاف منعه الفلاسفة وبعض أصحابنا كالغزالي وإمام الحرمين ومنهم من توقف كالقاضي أبي بكر وضرار بن عمرو وكلام الصوفية في الأكثر مشعر بالامتناع وإنما منعه الفلاسفة ) لأن المعقول إما بالبديهة وحقيقته ليست بديهية وإما بالنظر والنظر إما في الرسم وهو لا يفيد الحقيقة وإما في الحد ) فإذن لا نعلم الحقيقة إلا بالبديهة أو بالحد وحقيقته تعالى ليست بديهية ) ولا يمكن تحديدها لعدم التركيب فيها لما مر فلا يمكن العلم بها والجواب منع حصر المدرك ) بالكنه (في البديهية والحد والرسم لجواز خلق الله تعالى علماً متعلقاً بما ليس ضرورياً) بالقياس إلى عموم الناس ( في شخص بلا سابقة نظر كما سبق) من ( أن النظري قد ينقلب ضرورياً) لبعض الأشخاص ) وأيضاً فالرسم وإن لم يجب أن يفيد الحقيقة فلا يمتنع أن يفيدها. وأجيب بأن هذا أيضاً كلي إذ لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين وإن كان المفروض محالاً . قوله : (الجواب منع حصر المدرك إلخ منع الحصر إنما يتم بجعل البديهي بمعنى بالنسبة إلى عموم الناس كما أشار إليه الشارح، فلك أن تقرر الجواب بمنع عدم البداهة البديهي أيضاً، فتأمل.
The second station: on permissibility. And concerning the permissibility of knowing the essence of God, there is disagreement. The philosophers and some of our fellows (such as al-Ghazali and Imam al-Haramayn) denied it, and some hesitated (like al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Dirar ibn Amr), and the speech of the Sufis mostly indicates impossibility. The philosophers denied it because the knowable is either by intuition (badiha) and His essence is not intuitive, or by reflection (nazar), and reflection is either by description (rasm) which does not convey the essence, or by definition (hadd). So we do not know the essence except by intuition or by definition, and His essence is not intuitive and cannot be defined due to its lack of composition, as previously mentioned. Therefore, knowledge of it is impossible. The answer: We deny the exhaustive enumeration of the means of knowing the essence to intuition, definition, and description, because it is possible for God to create knowledge related to what is not necessary (in relation to the generality of people) in a person without prior reflection, as previously mentioned that the theoretical may become necessary for some individuals. Also, description (rasm), even if it is not necessarily conveying the essence, is not impossible that it may convey it. And it was answered: This is also a universal, as it is not impossible to suppose it true of many, even if the supposed is impossible. His statement: 'The answer is a denial of the exhaustive enumeration, etc.' The denial of exhaustive enumeration is only complete by making 'intuitive' in the sense of 'in relation to the generality of people', as the commentator indicated. So you can formulate the answer by denying the lack of necessity of the intuitive itself. Reflect.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The philosophers argue that knowledge of God's essence is impossible because it can only be through intuition or through definition via genus and differentia, and God has no composition. The author's answer denies the exhaustive enumeration: God may create knowledge in someone directly, and also a description (rasm) may sometimes give the essence. Then a further objection is raised that this answer is also universal and could apply to the impossible, but the author hints that the denial of the exhaustive enumeration is sufficient if we redefine 'intuitive' relative to people.

Key Terms
بديهة
نظر
رسم
حد
كنه
ضروري
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God's essence is impossible because the only means of knowledge are intuition and definition, and God's essence lacks both.
Correction: The means are not limited to those two; God can create direct knowledge in people, and description may also convey the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why is the philosophers' proof that God's essence is unknowable invalid?
A) Because intuition is not a valid source of knowledge
B) Because the means of knowing the essence are not limited to intuition and definition
C) Because God's essence is composite
D) Because reflection is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God's essence is possible because the means of knowing are not limited to intuition and definition; God may grant direct knowledge, and description may convey the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can create direct knowledge of Himself in a person without prior reflection, have you sincerely asked Him for that knowledge in your prayers? When was the last time you pleaded with Him to open your heart with certainty about His essence?
?
The philosophers limited God's ability to grant knowledge of Himself to ordinary means. Are there areas in your life where you have limited what God can teach you because you assume it's too deep or too far from your current understanding? What would it mean to trust that He can bypass your limitations?
?
This passage ends with 'reflect.' In an age of constant distraction, when will you set aside time to be still before God, not studying arguments, but waiting for Him to illuminate your heart about who He is? Will you commit to five minutes of silent reflection tonight?

Classification of Positions on the Effective Cause
Enumeration of theological views on the effective cause based on a logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the systematic classification of theological views on the effective cause in human actions, including the main division between one power alone or both together, and the subdivisions within the joint position regarding object and subordination.

Core Idea
The effective cause is either one power alone or both, with crucial subtypes based on object and subordination.

This lesson presents the 'rule' (al-dabit) that organizes different theological schools according to their view of the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in human acts. The classification starts by asking whether the effective cause is God's power alone, the servant's power alone, or both together. If both are involved, further distinctions are made regarding whether they share the same object or not, and whether one power is subordinated to the other. This framework helps clarify the positions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, philosophers, and others.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule and classification
Read the Arabic passage describing the rule and its branches. Notice the four main positions: (1) God's power alone, (2) servant's power alone, (3) both with same object, (4) both with different objects, and within that, subordination or non-subordination.
The passage includes an objection and reply at the end.
2
Identify each school's position
Match each position to the named schools: the Shaykh (God alone), Mu'tazila majority (servant alone), al-Ustadh and al-Najjar (both, same object), the Imam and philosophers (both, different objects, subordination of servant to God), and al-Qadi (both, different objects, no subordination).
3
Analyze the objection and reply
Consider the possible objection that al-Qadi's position could be reversed (essence by servant, attribute by God). The reply says no one holds that. Reflect on why this matters for the classification.
4
Visualize the classification tree
Draw a tree diagram starting with 'Effective cause' branching into 'Single power' (God alone, servant alone) and 'Joint power'. Then joint branches into 'Same object' and 'Different objects'. Different objects branches into 'With subordination' and 'Without subordination'.
This helps internalize the logical division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد العبد وتأثيره وقالت الحكماء وإمام الحرمين) هي واقعة على سبيل الوجوب وامتناع ( التخلف ) بقدرة يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد إذا قارنت حصول الشرائط وارتفاع الموانع ( والضابط ) في هذا المقام ( أن المؤثر إما قدرة الله أو قدرة العبد ) على الانفراد كمذهبي الشيخ وجمهور المعتزلة ( أو هما معاً، ذلك إما ( مع اتحاد المتعلقين) كمذهب الأستاذ منا والنجار من المعتزلة ( أو دونه ( أي دون الاتحاد ( وحينئذ فإما مع كون إحداهما ) أي إحدى القدرتين ( متعلقة للأخرى، و) لا شبهة في أنه (ليس قدرة الله متعلقة لقدرة العبد ) إذ يستحيل تأثير الحادث في القديم فتعين العكس أن تكون قدرة العبد صادرة عن قدرة الله تعالى وموجبة للفعل، وهو قول الإمام والفلاسفة ( وإما بدون ذلك ) أي بدون أن تكون إحداهما متعلقة للأخرى وهو مذهب القاضي لأن المفروض عدم اتحاد المتعلقتين، فإن قيل : جاز أن يكون عكس مذهبه أن أصل الفعل بقدرة العبد وصفته بقدرة الله قلنا : لم يقل به أحد والمقصود وهو وهو اشتهر في 6 قوله : ( وقالت الحكماء وإمام الحرمين إلخ ) قال شارح المقاصد : هذا النقل من الإمام وإن الكتب إلا أنه خلاف ما صرح به في الإرشاد وغيره . قوله : ( أن المؤثر إما قدرة الله تعالى إلخ) فيه بحث لأن الترديد إما في المؤثر القريب في الفعل فلا يستقيم في قول الفلاسفة والإمام، أما في مطلق المؤثر فلا يتم الانفراد في قول المعتزلة لأن القدرة الحادثة أثر القدرة القديمة، وإن اختير الأول ووجه بأن مجموع القدرتين مؤثر قريب الفعل، وإن كانت القديمة وحدها مؤثرة بعيدة قلنا بعد تسليم صحته، فكذلك عند المعتزلة وبالجملة كما لم يظهر فرق بين مذهبي الفلاسفة والمعتزلة مما ذكره في التفصيل السابق لم يظهر من هذا الضابط أيضاً، نعم لو ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن قدرة العبد صادرة عنه أيضاً لكن لا بطريق الاختيار لا يلزم التسلسل بل بطريق الإيجاب لظهر الفرق بين مذهبهم ومذهب الفلاسفة وصح ما ذكره في الضابط لكن هذا خلاف ما اشتهر منهم والله أعلم .
The sixth observatory - the first objective: on His acts and the acts of servants. The servant and his influence. And the sages and Imam al-Haramayn said: they occur necessarily and cannot be absent, by a power that God creates in the servant when conditions are met and obstacles are removed. The rule in this context is that the effective cause is either God's power or the servant's power alone, as in the two schools of the Shaykh and the majority of the Mu'tazila, or both together. That is either with the two objects being the same, as in the school of al-Ustadh among us and al-Najjar among the Mu'tazila, or without their union. Then either with one of the two powers being subordinated to the other, and there is no doubt that God's power is not subordinated to the servant's power, for it is impossible for the originated to affect the eternal, so the opposite is determined: the servant's power proceeds from God's power and necessitates the act, which is the view of the Imam and the philosophers. Or without that, i.e., without one being subordinated to the other, which is the school of al-Qadi, because it is assumed that the two objects are not the same. If it is said: his reverse view is possible, that the essence of the act is by the servant's power and its attribute by God's power, we say: no one has said that, and the intention is ... His saying: 'And the sages and Imam al-Haramayn said etc.' The commentator of al-Maqasid said: This quotation from the Imam, although in books, is contrary to what he stated in al-Irshad and others. His saying: 'The effective cause is either God's power etc.' contains a discussion, because the division is either regarding the proximate effective cause in the act, then it is not sound in the view of the philosophers and the Imam; or regarding the absolute effective cause, then the 'alone' is not complete in the Mu'tazila's view because the originated power is an effect of the eternal power. If the first is chosen and explained that the sum of the two powers is the proximate effective cause of the act, though the eternal alone is a remote effective cause, we say after conceding its soundness: it is likewise with the Mu'tazila. In summary, just as no difference appeared between the two schools of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila from what he mentioned in the previous detail, it also does not appear from this rule. Indeed, if the Mu'tazila had held that the servant's power also proceeds from him, but not by way of choice so that no regress follows, but by way of necessitation, then the difference between their school and the philosophers' school would appear, and what he mentioned in the rule would be correct. But this is contrary to what is well-known from them, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of theological positions on the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in human actions, and then offers a critical examination of that classification. The author first states the 'rule' (al-dabit) according to which the effective cause is either God's power alone (as held by the Shaykh [al-Ash'ari?] and the majority of Mu'tazila), or the servant's power alone (implicitly rejected), or both together. The joint case has two subdivisions: (i) the two powers have the same object (muta'alliqayn) – the school of al-Ustadh [Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini] among the Ash'aris and al-Najjar among the Mu'tazila; (ii) they do not have the same object. In the latter case, either one power is subordinated to the other – but God's power cannot be subordinated to the servant's, so the servant's power must be subordinate to God's, which is the view of the Imam [al-Razi?] and the philosophers – or neither is subordinated, which is the school of al-Qadi [Abd al-Jabbar?], assuming the objects are not identical. An objection is raised: could the reverse of al-Qadi's view be possible, that the essence of the act is by the servant's power and its attribute by God's power? The response is that no one has held that. The passage then includes a commentary that questions the translation from Imam al-Haramayn, noting a contradiction with his other works. The commentator further argues that the classification is problematic: if 'effective cause' means the proximate cause, the philosophers' and Imam's view does not fit; if it means any cause, the Mu'tazila's 'alone' fails because the servant's power comes from God. Even if the first interpretation is chosen, the claimed difference between the schools collapses. Only if the Mu'tazila held that the servant's power proceeds from him by necessitation (not choice) would there be a clear difference, but that is not their well-known view.

Key Terms
المؤثر
قدرة الله
قدرة العبد
الانفراد
اتحاد المتعلقين
تعلق إحداهما بالأخرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The effective cause is always only God's power; the servant has no real effect.
Correction: Many schools (Mu'tazila, al-Ustadh, etc.) affirm that the servant's power is truly effective, either alone or jointly with God's power.

Checkpoint
In the classification, which school holds that the effective cause is both God's power and the servant's power together, but with different objects and no subordination?
A) The school of the Shaykh and the Mu'tazila
B) The school of al-Ustadh and al-Najjar
C) The school of al-Qadi
D) The school of the philosophers and the Imam

Key Takeaway
The rule divides positions on the effective cause into those attributing it to one power alone and those attributing it to both, with further subdivisions based on object and subordination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you genuinely believe that both your power and God's power are involved in every deed? How does that affect your sense of responsibility and reliance on God?
?
If you truly internalize that your power is subordinated to God's, would you be less proud of your achievements and more humbly dependent? What specific action today can you perform with that awareness?
?
The passage presents different scholarly views. Which view resonates most with your own experience of your will and God's will? Can you point to a moment where you felt your effort was entirely your own or entirely from God?

Critical Analysis of the Classification Rule
Critical commentary on the validity of the classification rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criticisms of the classification rule for the effective cause, including the problems with interpreting 'effective cause' as proximate or absolute, and how the rule fails to distinguish the Mu'tazila from the philosophers unless a specific unheld assumption is made.

Core Idea
The classification rule collapses key distinctions between schools upon closer analysis.

After presenting the classification, the commentator subjects it to rigorous critique. He questions whether 'effective cause' refers to the proximate cause or any cause, showing that neither interpretation makes the rule consistent. He then argues that even if one interpretation is chosen, the rule fails to distinguish the Mu'tazila from the philosophers. Finally, he identifies a condition under which the rule would work, but notes that it is not the actual position of the Mu'tazila. This lesson examines the logical flaws in the rule and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the critical commentary
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'fihi bahth' (there is discussion) to the end. Notice that the commentator analyzes two possible interpretations of 'effective cause' and shows problems with each.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
The commentator considers whether 'effective cause' means the proximate cause (al-mu'aththir al-qarib) or the absolute cause (al-mu'aththir al-mutlaq). Understand how each interpretation affects the classification.
3
See why each interpretation fails
If proximate, the philosophers' and Imam's view does not fit because their proximate cause is the sum of both powers, not one alone. If absolute, the Mu'tazila's 'alone' fails because their servant's power originates from God, so God is also a remote cause.
4
Understand the hypothetical condition
The commentator concludes that if the Mu'tazila had held that the servant's power proceeds from the servant by necessitation (not by choice), the rule would distinguish them from the philosophers. But this is not their actual view, so the rule fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two Proofs That the Servant’s Act Occurs by God’s Power
Presentation of two rational proofs for the Ash'ari position on human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key arguments presented by the Ash'ari author for the claim that the servant's voluntary act is created by God's power, not the servant's own power.

Core Idea
The servant's act is possible; if independent, it would require full knowledge, but the sleeping person lacks it.

This lesson presents the two proofs that the author offers in support of the Ash'ari position. The first is based on the universality of God's power over all possibles and the impossibility of two effective powers on one object. The second argues from the necessary knowledge that an independent agent would have of the details of his action, and shows that the sleeping person's lack of awareness disproves such independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: statement of position, first proof, second proof.
The passage includes the heading and both proofs in a single flow.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate where the first proof ends and the second begins. Notice how each proof is structured: premise and conclusion.
The first proof uses the idea of 'every possible is within God's power' and 'no two effective powers can concur.' The second uses the conditional argument and the sleeping person example.
3
Reflect on the sleeping person example
Ask yourself: How does the example of the sleeping person turning over serve as a counterexample to the conditional? What does it show about voluntary action?
The example is meant to show that voluntary action can occur without detailed knowledge of the action's specifics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : فى أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد لله مخالفة تعالی مقدور ضبط المذاهب دون الاحتمالات العقلية ( لنا ) على أن الفعل الاختياري للعبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى لا بقدرته ( وجوه : الأول إِن فعل العبد ممكن) في نفسه (وكل ممكن مقدور لله تعالى لما مر من شمول قدرته ( للمكنات بأسرها، وقد مر الناس من المعتزلة والفرق الخارجة عن الإسلام في أن كل ممكن مقدور على تفاصيل مذاهبهم وإبطالها في بحث قادرية الله تعالى ( ولا شيء مما هو لله بواقع بقدرة العبد لامتناع اجتماع قدرتين مؤثرتين على مقدور واحد لما مر) الوجه ( الثاني : لو كان العبد موجدا لأفعاله بالاختيار والاستقلال ( لوجب أن يعلم تفاصيلها واللازم باطل أما الشرطية ) أي الملازمة ) فلأن الأزيد والأنقص مما أتى به ممكن منه إذ كل فعل من أفعاله يمكن وقوعه منه على وجوه متفاوتة بالزيادة والنقصان (فوقوع ذلك (المعين منه دونهما لأجل القصد ) إليه بخصوصه ( والاختيار) المتعلق به وحده ( مشروط بالعلم به كما تشهد به البديهة فتفاصيل الأفعال الصادرة عنه باختياره لا بد أن تكون مقصودة معلومة له ( وأما الاستثنائية ) أي بطلان اللازم فلأن النائم وكذا الساهي (قد يفعل باختياره كانقلابه من جنب إلى جنب ) ولا يشعر بكمية ذلك الفعل وكيفيته ) واعترض عليه بأنه يجوز أن يشعر قوله : (لما مر من شمول (قدرته فيه بحث وهو أن المذكور فيما مر شمول الجواز لا شمول الوقوع أعني أن يكون الكل واقعاً بالفعل بقدرته تعالى، ثم إن شمول الجواز إنما يستلزم شمول الوقوع إذا تم دليل التمانع لكن ما ذكره الشارح في بحث القدرة من هذا الموقف جواباً عن قول الجبائي يدفع جريان ذلك الدليل في الخالق والمخلوق فالوجه الأول لا يدل على المدعى. قوله : ( لوجب أن يعلم تفاصيلها قيل : هذا الدليل ينفي الكسب أيضاً لاشتراك الخلق والكسب في العلة المقتضية لكمال العلم وهو الصدور بالقصد ،والاختيار، وأجييب بأن القصد الأصلي موجب للعلم دون الضمن فالمشي إلى موضع مثلا مقصود أصلي فيجب الشعور به في الخلق والكسب بخلاف تحريكات العضلات المقصودة في ضمن المشي وقصد الإيجاد أصلي كلياً بخلاف الكسب . قوله : ( مما أتى به ممكن لفظة من هاهنا بمعنى في كما في قول الشاعر وليست بالأكثر منهم حصى فلا يلزم الجمع بين اللام ومن في أفعل التفضيل . قوله ( والاختيار) إن جر عطفاً على القصد فمشروط خبراً لوقوع ولأجل لغو يتعلق به وإن رفع بالابتداء على أن يكون بتأويل القصد فخبر الوقوع قوله : لأجل القصد قوله : ( فلأن النائم قد يفعل باختياره هذا على أي من لا يجعل النوم ضداً للقدرة لكن فيه بحث وهو أنه قد ذكر الآن أن القصد لا يتصور بدون العلم، ولا شك أن النوم مضاد للعلم فكيف يجعل فعله اختيارياً .
The sixth observatory - the first objective: on His acts and the acts of servants. For God, contrary, exalted, the object of power. The classification of schools without the rational possibilities. For us: that the voluntary act of the servant occurs by God's power, not by his power. [There are] proofs: the first: The servant's act is possible in itself, and every possible is an object of power for God the Exalted, as has been mentioned concerning the encompassing of His power over all [possible] things. And the people, the Mu'tazila and the sects outside Islam, have been mentioned concerning that every possible is an object of power, with details of their schools and their refutation in the discussion of God's omnipotence. And nothing that is for God occurs by the servant's power, due to the impossibility of two effective powers coming together on a single object of power, as has been mentioned. The second proof: If the servant were an originator of his actions by choice and independence, he would necessarily know their details, and the consequent is false. As for the conditional, i.e., the implication, because the more and the less of what he does is possible from him, since each of his actions can occur from him in varying ways of increase and decrease. So the occurrence of that particular [action] without them [the other possibilities] is due to the intention towards it specifically and the choice related to it alone, which is conditioned by knowledge of it, as intuition witnesses. So the details of the actions that proceed from him by choice must be intended and known to him. As for the exceptive, i.e., the falsehood of the consequent, because the sleeping person, as well as the inattentive one, sometimes acts by his choice, like his turning from side to side, while he is not aware of the quantity and quality of that action. And it was objected that it is possible that he is aware. His saying: 'as has been mentioned concerning the encompassing of His power' contains a discussion, namely that what was mentioned previously was the encompassing of possibility, not the encompassing of actual occurrence, i.e., that all are actually occurring by His power. Then that encompassing of possibility only entails encompassing of actual occurrence if the proof of mutual hindrance (dalil al-tamana') is complete, but what the commentator mentioned in the discussion of power from this position in response to al-Jubba'i's statement repels the application of that proof to the Creator and the creature, so the first proof does not indicate the claim. His saying: 'he would necessarily know their details' – it is said: this proof also negates acquisition (al-kasb) because creation and acquisition share the cause that necessitates complete knowledge, which is proceeding by intention and choice. And it was answered that the primary intention necessitates knowledge of itself, but not of the subsidiary. For example, walking to a place is a primary intention, so awareness of it is necessary in creation and acquisition, unlike the movements of muscles that are intended as part of the walk. And the intention of bringing into existence is primary and universal, unlike acquisition. His saying: 'the more and the less of what he does is possible' – the word 'min' here means 'fi' (in), as in the poet's saying: 'and they are not the most numerous among them in number,' so there is no combination of 'lam' and 'min' in the elative. His saying: 'and the choice' – if it is in the genitive as a conjunction with 'intention', then 'conditioned' is the predicate of 'occurrence', and 'for the sake of' is a verbal particle that is syntactically related. If it is raised as the subject, on the interpretation of 'intention', then 'occurrence' is the predicate. His saying: 'for the sake of intention' – his saying: 'because the sleeping person sometimes acts by his choice' – this is according to one who does not consider sleep as opposite to power, but there is a discussion here. It has been mentioned that intention cannot be conceived without knowledge, and there is no doubt that sleep is opposite to knowledge, so how can his action be considered voluntary?
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the Ash'ari position that the servant's voluntary action occurs by God's power, not the servant's own power, and then includes critical commentary on each proof. The first proof relies on the principle that every possible thing is within God's power, and that two effective powers cannot concur on one object. The commentary challenges this by distinguishing between potential power over all possibles and actual power, noting that the argument from mutual hindrance (tamana') does not apply between Creator and creature, thus the first proof fails to establish the claim. The second proof argues that if the servant were an independent creator, he would have to know all details of his actions, but in sleep or inattention he performs actions (e.g., turning) without full awareness. The commentary notes that this argument also negates the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition), since both creation and kasb require intention and knowledge. The response distinguishes between primary and subsidiary intention: primary intention (like walking) requires awareness, but subsidiary movements (like muscle motions) do not, so the argument does not refute kasb. The passage ends with a grammatical note on the phrase 'the more and the less' and a further objection: sleep is opposite to knowledge, so a sleeping person's action cannot be called voluntary. This shows ongoing dialectic between the author and his opponents.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة الله
الممكن
اجتماع قدرتين
القصد
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the sleeping person's action is not voluntary because they lack awareness; the author argues it is still voluntary but without full knowledge.
Correction: The author uses the sleeping person's lack of awareness to show that voluntary action does not require detailed knowledge, thus refuting the claim that independence would require such knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second proof?
A) The servant's action is possible
B) If the servant were independent, he would know all details, but he does not, so he is not independent
C) God's power encompasses all possibles
D) Two powers cannot concur

Key Takeaway
The author uses the universality of divine power and the sleeping person's lack of awareness to argue that the servant's act is created by God, not by the servant's own power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice today, do you truly believe that its occurrence is by God's power, not your own? How would your attitude change if you internalized that every detail of your action is sustained by Him?
?
The sleeping person turns over without awareness of the details of his movement. How often are you unaware of the details of your own actions, yet still claim them as wholly your own? What does this reveal about your need for God's constant creation?
?
If you were to act with full awareness that every possible action is within God's power and not your own, how would that reshape your sense of responsibility and your reliance on Him throughout your day?

Rational Judgments
advanced
6 min
Critiquing the Proofs: Possibility vs Actuality and the Kasb Distinction
Critical commentary on the two proofs, including defense of kasb and a final objection about sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weaknesses identified in the first proof and the defense of the second proof against the objection that it also refutes kasb.

Core Idea
The first proof only shows possibility, not actuality; the second proof requires distinguishing primary from subsidiary intention.

Having presented the two proofs, the author now subjects them to critical scrutiny. The first proof is shown to rely on a mistaken conflation of potential power over all possibles with actual creation of all possible actions. The second proof faces the objection that it also negates the Ash'ari concept of 'acquisition' (kasb). The author responds by distinguishing between primary and subsidiary intention, and then a final objection raises a deeper problem: how can a sleeping person have intention without knowledge?

Guided Path
1
Read the critical commentary
Focus on the objection to the first proof: what is the difference between 'possibility' and 'actuality' in God's power?
The commentator argues that the earlier discussion only established possible power, not actual creation.
2
Understand the kasb objection
The text says the second proof also negates kasb. Why? Because both creation and acquisition involve intention and choice.
The objection is that the same proof that shows the servant is not a creator would also show he is not an acquirer.
3
Analyze the response
The response distinguishes primary intention (e.g., walking) from subsidiary intention (e.g., muscle movements). This is key to preserving kasb.
Primary intention requires knowledge; subsidiary does not. In kasb, the intention is to acquire the action, not to create its details.
4
Consider the final objection
The commentary ends with an objection: sleep is opposite to knowledge, so how can a sleeping person's action be voluntary? This challenges the second proof's use of the sleeping example.
This objection is not answered; it remains a point of discussion.

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the Ash'ari position that the servant's voluntary action occurs by God's power, not the servant's own power, and then includes critical commentary on each proof. Why does the first proof fail according to the commentator?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that even the details of your actions are under God's knowledge and power, yet you are still accountable. When you sin, do you truly believe that God created that action and you only acquired it? How does that change your sense of responsibility and need for repentance right now?
?
Think of a time you took pride in a good deed as if it were entirely your own. The distinction between primary and subsidiary intention teaches that your role is merely acquisition. Will you humble yourself today and attribute every good deed to God's creation, praising Him alone?
?
The unresolved sleep objection leaves a gap: can intention exist without knowledge? Examine moments when you acted without full awareness—were those acts truly voluntary? How does this challenge your illusion of complete self-control, and will you submit more fully to God's omniscience?

Rational Judgments
