Proof That Servant Must Be Creator of His Acts
Enumerates five consequences of denying servant's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that without the servant's independent creation of his acts, the entire structure of religious obligations, rewards, and prophethood collapses.

Core Idea
Without human agency, religious concepts like obligation and reward become meaningless.

This passage presents the core argument of those who affirm human independent agency (such as the Mu'tazilites). The author reduces many arguments to one essential point: if humans are not truly the creators of their own actions, then all religious concepts that depend on moral responsibility—obligation, discipline, praise and blame, reward and punishment, and even the purpose of sending prophets—would be invalid. Let's unpack each consequence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central claim
Identify the key premise: the servant's independence in creating his act by choice is necessary for moral accountability.
The phrase 'لولا استقلال العبد بالفعل' is the linchpin.
2
First consequences: obligation and discipline
Note that commands and prohibitions (taklif) and disciplinary measures (ta'dib) would be rationally invalid if the servant is not independent.
You cannot command someone who does not create his own action.
3
Moral and eschatological consequences
Praise and blame (madḥ wa dhamm) and reward and punishment (thawāb wa 'iqāb) would be lifted because the act is not attributable to the servant.
The act must be freely chosen to deserve moral evaluation.
4
Ultimate consequence: prophethood
The sending of prophets (bi'tha) would have no benefit, as creatures would not deserve reward or punishment, and God could unjustly punish the righteous and reward the wicked.
This shows the entire religious framework depends on human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما غيره ) أي غير أبي الحسين ( فيستدل عليه أي على أن العبد موجد لأفعاله ( بوجوه كثيرة مرجعها إلى أمر واحد وهو أنه لولا استقلال العبد بالفعل) على سبيل الاختيار لبطل التكليف بالأوامر والنواهي لأن العبد إذا لم يكن موجداً لفعله ( مستقلا في إيجاده لم يصح عقلا أن يقال له افعل كذا ولا تفعل كذا (و) بطل ( التأديب) الذي ورد به الشرع، إذ لا لا يستقتل بإيجاد فعله ) وارتفع المدح والذم إذ ليس الفعل مستند إليه مطلقاً حتى يمدح به أو يذم (و) ارتفع ( الثواب والعقاب ( الوارد بهما الوعد والوعيد ) ولم يبق للبعثة فائدة ) لأن العباد ليسوا موجودين لأفعالهم فمن أين لهم استحقاق الثواب والعقاب عليها بل هي مخلوقة لله تعالى، فيجوز حينئذ أن يعكس فيعاقب الأنبياء وأتباعهم ويثبت الفراعنة فلا وأشياعهم يتصور منفعة للبعثة أصلا .
(And other than him) i.e. other than Abu al-Husayn (he argues for it, i.e. that the servant is the creator of his acts, with many ways whose return is to one thing, which is that were it not for the servant's independence in the act) by way of choice, obligation by commands and prohibitions would be invalid because if the servant is not the creator of his act (independent in his creation, it would not be rationally valid to say to him: do this and do not do that, and (discipline) that the Law came with would be invalid, since he is not independent in creating his act. And praise and blame would be lifted since the act is not attributable to him at all such that he is praised or blamed for it. And (reward and punishment) that promise and threat come with would be lifted, and there would remain no benefit for sending messengers) because servants are not agents of their acts, so from where would they deserve reward and punishment for them? Rather, they are created by God, so then it would be possible to reverse: to punish prophets and their followers and to reward pharaohs and their partisans, and no benefit for the sending of messengers can be imagined at all.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of the Mu'tazilites (and others who affirm human agency) for the servant's independent creation of his acts. The author states that the many arguments reduce to a single point: without the servant's independence in his act by choice, all religious notions become invalid. Specifically, (1) obligation (taklif) through commands and prohibitions would be rationally impossible, because you cannot command someone who does not create his own action; (2) discipline (ta'dib) prescribed by revelation would be void; (3) praise and blame (madḥ wa dhamm) would not apply since the act is not attributable to the servant; (4) reward and punishment (thawab wa 'iqab) based on promise and threat would be void; and (5) the sending of prophets (bi'tha) would have no benefit, because creatures are not agents of their deeds, so they could not deserve reward or punishment, and it would be conceivable for God to punish the righteous and reward the wicked. Thus, the entire religious framework collapses if the servant is not an independent creator of his actions.

Key Terms
استقلال العبد
تكليف
مدح وذم
ثواب وعقاب
بعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only reward/punishment requires human agency; other religious concepts are unaffected.
Correction: The proof shows that obligation, discipline, praise/blame, and prophethood also depend on the servant's independence.

Checkpoint
According to the proof presented, which of the following would be invalid if the servant is not independent in his acts?
A) Only reward and punishment.
B) Only praise and blame.
C) Obligation, discipline, praise, blame, reward, punishment, and prophethood.
D) Only divine foreknowledge.

Key Takeaway
Without the servant's independent creation of his acts, all moral and religious concepts that depend on free will become logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you perform is directly created by God without any independent role for you, how would that change the way you approach obeying divine commands? Are you living as if your choices matter, or do you passively attribute everything to God while ignoring your responsibility? This lesson demands you answer: Will you take ownership of your actions starting today?
?
The passage argues that without human agency, prophethood becomes futile—God could punish the righteous and reward the wicked. In what area of your life are you effectively saying 'God will change me' without taking any personal action? How does that make you complicit in the futility this passage exposes? Commit to one concrete step you will take today to act as if your choice matters.
?
Praise and blame are meaningless if the servant is not the originator of his acts. When you last sinned, did you blame your circumstances, your upbringing, or even God? This lesson confronts you: Will you accept full responsibility for your sins as your own actions, or will you continue to excuse yourself at the cost of denying the moral framework of Islam?

The Opponent's Seven Scriptural Proofs and the Ash'ari Response
Al-Jurjani enumerates seven scriptural proofs and gives the rejoinder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven scriptural proofs the Mu'tazila use to argue for human creation of actions, and the Ash'ari response that when scriptural apparent meanings conflict, rational proofs must decide.

Core Idea
Scriptural evidence alone cannot decide the debate; reason must settle conflicting apparent meanings.

Al-Jurjani presents the opponent's (Mu'tazilite) strongest scriptural arguments for human agency, then shows why they are not conclusive. This passage demonstrates a key principle of Ash'ari methodology: when Quranic verses appear to conflict, one must turn to decisive rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first the opponent's seven types of verses, then the response.
2
Identify each of the seven proofs
List the seven types: (1) action attribution, (2) praise/blame and promise/threat, (3) God's transcendence from imperfection, (4) linking actions to human will, (5) command to seek help, (6) prophets' confessions, (7) regret and desire to return.
Each type is supported by Quranic examples.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response counters with other verses affirming divine decree and creation, and verses about guidance/misguidance. Since apparent meanings conflict, rational proofs are necessary.
Notice the principle: conflicting zawāhir are not decisive.
4
Reflect on the role of reason
Why does Al-Jurjani say rational proofs are needed? Because scriptural arguments alone leave ambiguity. The earlier rational proofs for Ash'ari doctrine are sufficient.
This connects to the broader kalam methodology.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the main point about conflicting apparent meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما احتج الخصم على كون العبد موجداً لأفعاله بالظواهر آيات تشعر بمقصوده وهي أنواع : الأول : فيه إضافة الفعل إلى العبد نحو فويل للذين يكتبون الكتاب بأيديهم [البقرة : ٧٩] ذلك بأن الله لم يك مغيراً نعمة أنعمها على قوم حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم [الأنفال : ٥٣] الثاني : ما فيه مدح وذم نحو وإبراهيم الذي وفى [النجم: ٣٧] كيف تكفرون بالله [البقرة : ۸۲] ، وما فيه وعد ووعيد كقوله من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها [الأنعام : ١٦٠]، ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم [الجن : ٢٣]، وهو أكثر من أن يحصى . الثالث : الآيات الدالة على أن أفعال الله تعالى منزهة عما يتصف به فعل العبد من تفاوت واختلاف وقبح وظلم كقوله تعالى ما ترى في خلق الرحمن من تفاوت [الملك :٣] ، ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافاً كثيراً [النساء : ۸۲]، الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه وما ظلمناهم ولكن كانوا أنفسهم يظلمون [النحل : ۱۱۸] . الرابع : تعليق أفعال العباد بمشيئتهم أي الآيات الدالة عليه نحو فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر [الكهف : ٢٩]. الخامس : الأمر بالاستعانة نحو إياك نستعين [الفاتحة : ٥ ] ، استعينوا بالله [الأعراف : ۱۲۸] ولا معنى للاستعانة فيما يوجده في العبد فيما يوجده العبد بإعانة من ربه . السادس : اعتراف الأنبياء بذنوبهم كقول آدم عليه السلام ربنا ظلمنا أنفسنا [الأعراف : ۲۳]، وقول يونس عليه السلام : سبحانك إني كنت من الظالمين [الأنبياء : ۸۷] . السابع : ما يوجد في الآخرة من الكفار والفسقة من التحسر وطلب الرجعة نحو لله ارجعوني لعلي أعمل صالحاً [المؤمنون : ٩٩] . الجواب أن هذه الآيات معارضة جميع بالآيات الدالة على أن الأفعال بقضاء الله وقدره وإيجاده وخلقه نحو والله خلقكم وما تعملون [الصافات : ٩٦] أي عملكم خالق كل شيء وعمل العبد شيء فعال لما يريد وهو يريد الإيمان إجماعاً فيكون فعالاً له، وكذا الكفر إذ لا قائل بالفصل ومعارضة بالآيات المصرحة بالهداية والإضلال والختم نحو يضل كثيراً ويهدي به كثيراً [البقرة : ٢٦ ] ، وختم الله على قلوبهم [البقرة : ٧]، وهي محمولة على حقائقها كما هو الظاهر منها وأنت تعلم أن الظواهر إذا تعارضت لم تقبل شهادتها خصوصاً في المسائل اليقينية ووجب الرجوع إلى غيرها من الدلائل العقلية القطعية وقد مر منها ما فيه كفاية لإثبات مذهبنا .
And perhaps the opponent argues for the servant being the originator of his actions based on apparent verses that indicate his purpose, and they are of types: First: that which attributes the action to the servant, such as 'So woe to those who write the book with their hands' [2:79] 'That is because Allah would not change a favor which He had bestowed upon a people until they change what is within themselves' [8:53]. Second: that which contains praise and blame, such as 'And Abraham who fulfilled [his obligations]' [53:37], 'How can you disbelieve in Allah?' [2:82], and what contains promise and threat, such as His saying: 'Whoever brings a good deed shall have ten times the like thereof' [6:160], and 'Whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, indeed for him is the fire of Hell' [72:23], and it is more than can be counted. Third: the verses indicating that the actions of Allah Almighty are free from the variation, difference, ugliness, and injustice that characterize the action of the servant, such as His saying: 'You do not see in the creation of the Most Merciful any inconsistency' [67:3], and 'If it had been from [any] other than Allah, they would have found within it much contradiction' [4:82], 'Who perfected everything which He created' [32:7], and 'We did not wrong them, but they were wronging themselves' [16:118]. Fourth: linking the actions of the servants to their will, i.e., the verses indicating that, such as 'So whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' [18:29]. Fifth: the command to seek help, such as 'It is You we worship and You we ask for help' [1:5], 'Seek help through Allah' [7:128], and there is no meaning to seeking help in what He produces in the servant in what the servant produces with the help of his Lord. Sixth: the prophets' confession of their sins, such as the saying of Adam, peace be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [7:23], and the saying of Jonah, peace be upon him: 'Exalted are You! Indeed, I have been of the wrongdoers' [21:87]. Seventh: what is found in the Hereafter of the disbelievers and sinners of regret and asking to return, such as 'My Lord, send me back that I might do righteousness' [23:99-100]. The answer is that these verses are all contradicted by the verses indicating that actions are by Allah's decree, power, existence, and creation, such as 'While Allah created you and what you do' [37:96], i.e., your work, 'Creator of all things' [6:102], and the servant's action is a thing, and He does what He wills, and He wills faith by consensus, so He does it, and likewise disbelief since there is no proponent of distinction, and contradicted by the verses explicitly stating guidance and misguidance and sealing, such as 'He misleads many thereby and guides many thereby' [2:26] and 'Allah has set a seal upon their hearts' [2:7], and they are interpreted according to their literal meanings as is apparent from them. And you know that when apparent meanings conflict, their testimony is not accepted, especially in matters of certainty, and one must resort to other proofs, namely decisive intellectual proofs, and enough of them have passed to establish our doctrine.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first presents the opponent's (Mu'tazilite) scriptural evidence for human agency in creating actions, then gives the Ash'ari response. The opponent's evidence is organized into seven types of Quranic verses: (1) verses that directly attribute actions to servants (e.g., 'they write the book with their hands'); (2) verses of praise and blame, and promise and threat, which imply human responsibility; (3) verses asserting God's transcendence from imperfection, implying that human actions are not directly from God; (4) verses linking actions to human will (e.g., 'whoever wills, let him believe'); (5) verses commanding seeking help, implying a role for human effort; (6) prophets' confessions of sins, implying they committed wrong; and (7) verses showing regret and desire to return to do good deeds. The response argues that all these verses are contradicted by other clear verses that affirm actions are by God's decree, power, and creation (e.g., 'Allah created you and what you do'), and by verses about guidance and misguidance and sealing of hearts. Since the apparent meanings (zawāhir) of these two groups conflict, and the matter concerns certain knowledge, the scriptural evidence is not decisive. Instead, one must rely on rational proofs (dalā'il 'aqliyya qaṭ'iyya), which have already been sufficiently presented to establish the Ash'ari doctrine of human acquisition and divine creation of actions.

Misconception: Scriptural verses alone can decide whether humans create their actions.
Correction: Ash'ari theologians hold that when apparent meanings conflict, one must resort to rational proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari response when scriptural apparent meanings (zawāhir) conflict?
A) Accept both as equally valid
B) Resort to decisive rational proofs
C) Prefer the verses that attribute actions to servants
D) Reject all conflicting verses

Key Takeaway
When scriptural apparent meanings conflict about human agency, Ash'ari theologians prioritize rational proofs over conflicting verses.

Resolving the Objection: Specifying the Impossible Side
Gloss on a potential inconsistency regarding impossibility and divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the gloss resolves the apparent problem of the preference-giving attribute by specifying the side as the impossible one.

Core Idea
The objection vanishes when we identify the side as impossible.

This gloss examines a potential objection in the main text regarding the non-faith of the disbeliever. The issue hinges on the interpretation of 'one of the two sides' — whether it is indefinite or specific. The commentator clarifies that the difficulty only arises in one interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms that indicate the two sides: 'أحد الطرفين', 'طرف مستحيل', 'طرف واجب'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The gloss contrasts interpreting 'one of the two sides' as a non-specific indefinite one versus a specific one—the impossible side. Understand how each leads to a different conclusion.
3
Understand why the problem disappears for the impossible side
When the side is specified as the impossible side, it is clear that no preference-giving attribute can be conceived there. This removes the objection.
4
Examine the necessary side's causation chain
For the necessary side, the gloss explains that necessity follows knowledge, which follows occurrence, which follows the eternal will. So the necessary side does have a cause, so the issue is resolved.
Reflect on this chain: will → occurrence → knowledge → necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر إلخ) فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد على توجيه كلام المصنف في حمل أحد الطرفين فيه على الأحد الغير المعين، وأما لو حمل على أحدهما معيناً أعني الطرف المستحيل فلا إذ عدم تصور الصفة المرجحة في الطرف الممتنع ظاهر بخلافه في الطرف الواجب فإن الوجوب أثر العلم والعلم يتبع الوقوع والوقوع أثر الإرادة القديمة فليتأمل
His saying: 'And there is discussion in this because the non-faith of the disbeliever, etc.' There is discussion in this because this only applies to the interpretation of the author's speech in taking 'one of the two sides' there as a non-specific one. But if it is taken as a specific one—namely the impossible side—then no, because the inconceivability of the preference-giving attribute in the impossible side is clear, unlike in the necessary side, for necessity (wujūb) is an effect of knowledge, knowledge follows occurrence, and occurrence is an effect of the eternal will, so reflect.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's gloss on the phrase 'وَفِيهِ بَحْثٌ' (and there is discussion) in the main text. The gloss explains that the apparent inconsistency vanishes if the ambiguous pronoun 'one of the two sides' is interpreted as the impossible side specifically, rather than as an indefinite one. In the impossible side, the absence of a preference-giving attribute is obvious. But in the necessary side, necessity is a result of knowledge, which follows occurrence, which follows the eternal will. Thus the necessary side does not lack a cause, so the issue is resolved.

Key Terms
أحد الطرفين
طرف مستحيل
طرف واجب
الصفة المرجحة
الوجوب أثر العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the 'fīhi baḥth' is a refutation of the main argument rather than a clarification.
Correction: The gloss is a clarification showing that the problem is resolved by proper interpretation; it is not a refutation.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, how does the problem of the preference-giving attribute disappear?
A) By assuming both sides are impossible.
B) By specifying that the side in question is the impossible side.
C) By denying the necessity of a cause for obligation.
D) By claiming the attribute is not needed.

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving the objection is specifying that the side referred to is the impossible side, not an indefinite one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ash'ari Doctrine on Good and Bad: Reason vs. Revelation
Al-Jurjani argues against rational judgment and for shariah as sole moral determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that moral values are not intrinsic but are determined by divine command, and how this contrasts with the Mu'tazila view that reason can judge good and bad.

Core Idea
Good and bad are not in things; they are what God says they are.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the core Ash'ari theological position that good and bad are not objective qualities of actions but are defined solely by divine law. It sets this against the Mu'tazila claim that reason can independently judge moral value. As you read, notice how the author defends the Ash'ari view by arguing that the Lawgiver could reverse rulings (as in abrogation), and that the intellect has no say. The passage also emphasizes the importance of clearly defining the terms of the dispute before arguing.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
First, read the Arabic and then the translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the key terms and the structure.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'hukm al-'aql' and 'shar''.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts the Ash'ari view (no rational judgment, Law establishes good/bad) with the Mu'tazila view (reason judges, act is good/bad intrinsically). Locate each and note how they are introduced.
The Mu'tazila view begins with 'wa qalat al-Mu'tazila'.
3
Examine the argument from abrogation
Al-Jurjani uses the example of abrogation (naskh) to show that if the Lawgiver can reverse a ruling, then moral values cannot be fixed. Consider why this supports the Ash'ari position.
Abrogation changes the ruling but the act remains the same.
4
Understand the need to define the dispute
The passage ends with 'tahrir mahall al-niza' – the importance of defining the point of disagreement before argument. Reflect on why this is crucial for fruitful discussion.
Without clear definitions, both sides may talk past each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالاتفاق . وأما فعل البهائم فقد قيل إنه لا يوصف بحسن ولا قبح باتفاق الخصوم وفعل الصبي مختلف فيه ) ولا حكم للعقل في حسن الأشياء وقبحها، وليس ذلك ) أي حسن الأشياء وقبحها ) عائد إلى أمر حقيقي حاصل ( في الفعل) قبل الشرع ( يكشف عنه (الشرع كما تزعمه المعتزلة ( بل الشرع هو المثبت له والمبين) فلا حسن ولا قبح للأفعال قبل ورود الشرع ( ولو عكس الشارع ( القضية فحسن ما قبحه وقبح ما حسنه لم يكن ممتنعا وانقلب (الأمر فصار القبيح حسنا والحسن قبيحاً كما في النسخ من الحرمة إلى الوجوب ومن الوجوب إلى الحرمة. ( وقالت المعتزلة : بل الحاكم بهما ) هو ( العقل والفعل حسن أو قبيح في نفسه ) إما لذاته وإما لصفة لازمة له وإما لوجوه واعتبارات على اختلاف مذاهبهم. ( والشرع كاشف ومبين للحسن والقبح الثابتين له على أحد الأنحاء الثلاثة ( وليس له أن يعكس القضية ) من عند نفسه. نعم إذا اختلف حال الفعل في الحسن والقبح بالقياس إلى الأزمان أو الأشخاص والأحوال كان له أن يكشف عما تغير الفعل إليه من حسنه أو قبحه في نفسه ولا بد أولاً) أي قبل الشروع في الاحتجاج (من (تحرير محل النزاع ) ليتضح المتنازع فيه ويرد النفي والإثبات على شيء واحد . ( فنقول : ) وبالله التوفيق
By agreement. As for the actions of animals, it has been said that they are not described as good or bad by the agreement of the opponents, and the action of a child is disputed. And there is no ruling of the intellect concerning the goodness and badness of things, and that (goodness and badness) is not a real attribute subsisting in the act before the Law, as the Mu'tazila claim, rather the Law is the establisher and explainer of it. So there is no good or bad for actions before the coming of the Law. And if the Lawgiver were to reverse the case, making good what He had deemed bad and bad what He had deemed good, it would not be impossible, and the matter would be reversed such that the bad becomes good and the good becomes bad, as in abrogation from prohibition to obligation and from obligation to prohibition. And the Mu'tazila said: Rather, the judge of them is the intellect, and the act is good or bad in itself, either by essence or by a necessary attribute or by aspects and considerations according to their various schools. And the Law is a revealer and explainer of the goodness and badness that are established for it in one of the three ways, and it cannot reverse the judgment from itself. Yes, if the state of an act changes in goodness and badness relative to times, persons, or circumstances, it is possible for the Law to reveal what the act has changed to in terms of its goodness or badness in itself. And it is necessary first, i.e., before embarking on argumentation, to define the point of dispute, so that the subject under dispute becomes clear and negation and affirmation refer to the same thing. So we say: And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that moral values are not intrinsic but are determined solely by divine command, and that the intellect has no independent ability to judge good and bad. It contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position that reason can judge, and the Law only reveals what is already inherent. The commentator also discusses the possibility of reversal through abrogation and stresses the importance of defining the terms of the dispute before argument.

Key Terms
hukm al-'aql
shar'
mu'tazila
naskh
tahrir mahall al-niza'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris believe moral values are purely arbitrary, with no relation to divine command.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while good and bad are not intrinsic to actions, they are established by divine command, which is not arbitrary but based on divine wisdom. The example of abrogation shows that the Lawgiver has authority to change rulings, but this is not arbitrary; it is part of the divine plan.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, what would happen if the Lawgiver reversed a ruling?
A) It would be impossible
B) It would change the intrinsic nature of the act
C) It would be permissible and the moral value would change
D) The intellect would still know the original value

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view holds that moral good and bad are not intrinsic but are defined by divine command, and the intellect cannot independently judge them.

Ash'ari and Mu'tazila on Good and Evil
States the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila views on rational judgment of good and evil and the need to define the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views of the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila on whether good and evil are determined by revelation or reason, and why defining the dispute is essential.

Core Idea
Good and evil: revealed or rational? The dispute defines Islamic theology.

Before engaging in debate, clear definitions are crucial. This passage from al-Mawaqif lays out the foundational disagreement between the Ash'aris, who hold that revelation alone establishes moral value, and the Mu'tazila, who argue that the intellect can judge good and evil independently. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping later arguments on divine justice and human obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Ash'ari view, then Mu'tazila view, then a methodological note.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Pinpoint the Ash'ari claim: 'the intellect has no judgment' and 'revelation establishes it.' Then the Mu'tazila claim: 'the intellect is the judge' and 'the act is good or evil in itself.'
Look for the contrast between 'الشرع هو المثبت' and 'العقل الحاكم'.
3
Understand the preliminary requirement
The passage ends with the need to 'refine the subject of dispute.' Why is this important? Because without a shared definition, arguments miss the mark.
This is a key methodological point for all theological debates.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the Ash'ari argument that if the Lawgiver reversed a ruling, moral value would flip. This illustrates their view that value is not intrinsic. Contrast with the Mu'tazila insistence that revelation only uncovers intrinsic value.
This leads to different views on divine justice.

Source Text

المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح بالاتفاق . وأما فعل البهائم فقد قيل إنه لا يوصف بحسن ولا قبح باتفاق الخصوم وفعل الصبي مختلف فيه ) ولا حكم للعقل في حسن الأشياء وقبحها، وليس ذلك ) أي حسن الأشياء وقبحها ) عائد إلى أمر حقيقي حاصل ( في الفعل) قبل الشرع ( يكشف عنه (الشرع كما تزعمه المعتزلة ( بل الشرع هو المثبت له والمبين) فلا حسن ولا قبح للأفعال قبل ورود الشرع ( ولو عكس الشارع ( القضية فحسن ما قبحه وقبح ما حسنه لم يكن ممتنعا وانقلب (الأمر فصار القبيح حسنا والحسن قبيحاً كما في النسخ من الحرمة إلى الوجوب ومن الوجوب إلى الحرمة. ( وقالت المعتزلة : بل الحاكم بهما ) هو ( العقل والفعل حسن أو قبيح في نفسه ) إما لذاته وإما لصفة لازمة له وإما لوجوه واعتبارات على اختلاف مذاهبهم. ( والشرع كاشف ومبين للحسن والقبح الثابتين له على أحد الأنحاء الثلاثة ( وليس له أن يعكس القضية ) من عند نفسه. نعم إذا اختلف حال الفعل في الحسن والقبح بالقياس إلى الأزمان أو الأشخاص والأحوال كان له أن يكشف عما تغير الفعل إليه من حسنه أو قبحه في نفسه ولا بد أولاً) أي قبل الشروع في الاحتجاج (من (تحرير محل النزاع ) ليتضح المتنازع فيه ويرد النفي والإثبات على شيء واحد .
The Sixth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: On al-Husn (good) and al-Qubh (evil). By agreement. As for the act of animals, it has been said that it is not described as good or evil by agreement of the opponents, and the act of a child is disputed. And the intellect has no judgment in the goodness and evilness of things, and that (i.e., the goodness and evilness of things) does not revert to a real matter subsisting (in the act) before revelation which revelation uncovers, as the Mu'tazila claim. Rather, revelation is what establishes it and clarifies it. So there is no good or evil for acts before the arrival of revelation. And if the Lawgiver reversed the proposition, making good what He deemed evil and evil what He deemed good, it would not be impossible, and the matter would change: the evil would become good and the good evil, as in abrogation from prohibition to obligation and from obligation to prohibition. And the Mu'tazila said: Rather, the judge of them is the intellect, and the act is good or evil in itself—either by its essence, or by a necessary attribute, or by aspects and considerations according to their varying doctrines. And revelation is a revealer and clarifier of the good and evil established for it in one of the three ways, and it is not for it to reverse the proposition on its own. Yes, if the state of the act changes in goodness and evil in relation to times or persons and circumstances, it (revelation) may reveal what the act has changed to regarding its goodness or evil in itself. And it is necessary first, i.e., before beginning argumentation, to refine the subject of dispute so that the matter under dispute becomes clear and negation and affirmation are directed to one thing.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the two opposing positions on the rational judgment of good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh) before defining the terms of the dispute. It begins by stating the Ash'ari position: the intellect has no judgment in determining good and evil; rather, good and evil are not real attributes of acts before revelation, but revelation establishes them. The Ash'ari argue that if the Lawgiver reversed a ruling, the moral value would flip, as seen in abrogation. Then the Mu'tazila position is given: the intellect is the judge, and acts are good or evil in themselves—either by essence, necessary attribute, or aspects. Revelation only uncovers this intrinsic value and cannot reverse it. The passage ends by noting that if the act's context changes, revelation can show the change, but first the subject of dispute must be defined to ensure the debate is about the same thing.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبح
العقل
الشرع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny any role for reason in morality; in fact, they affirm rational recognition of perfection and harm, but deny rational obligation of praise and blame apart from revelation.
Correction: Ash'aris agree that reason can perceive benefit and harm, but they hold that moral value (good/evil in terms of praise and blame) is determined solely by revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position stated in this passage, what is the role of revelation regarding good and evil?
A) Revelation uncovers an intrinsic good or evil already present in acts.
B) Revelation establishes and clarifies good and evil; they have no reality before revelation.
C) Revelation has no role; good and evil are purely rational.
D) Revelation only applies to acts of worship, not moral values.

Key Takeaway
The debate over good and evil hinges on whether moral values are intrinsic and knowable by reason (Mu'tazila) or divinely posited and known only through revelation (Ash'aris), and defining the dispute is the first step in argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you faced a moral dilemma where your reason told you one thing but a clear text of revelation said another? Which did you follow, and how did that feel? Now, honest before Allah—would you still follow revelation even if it felt 'unjust' to your reason?
?
If you truly believe that good and evil are defined only by revelation, what does that mean for how you view non-Muslims who live moral lives according to their reason? Does your belief push you to judge them or to examine their accountability before Allah?
?
Consider a legal ruling in Islam that was later abrogated, like the change from facing Jerusalem to Mecca. Did you ever think of that as God 'changing His mind'? How does the Ash'ari view of moral values help you trust that God's commands are always wise, even when they appear to reverse?

The Three Meanings of Good and Evil (al-Husn wa al-Qubh)
Enumerates the three meanings of good and evil and identifies the third as the point of dispute, which is shar'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of al-husn wa al-qubh and why the third meaning (attachment of praise/reward and blame/punishment) is the subject of dispute, considered by the Ash'aris to be revealed rather than rational.

Core Idea
Good and evil have three meanings; only the third is disputed and established by revelation.

Before we can discuss whether acts are inherently good or evil, we need to clarify what 'good' and 'evil' even mean. The scholars identified three distinct usages: one based on perfection, one based on benefit, and one based on divine reward and punishment. This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif enumerates these meanings and pinpoints the third as the real subject of theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three meanings
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Look for the three meanings explicitly enumerated: the first (صفة الكمال والنقص), the second (ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته), and the third (تعلق المدح والثواب). Notice how each is introduced.
The passage uses 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث' to mark them.
2
Note which meanings are rational and undisputed
The commentary tells us that the first two meanings are rational (عقلي) and there is no dispute about them. Confirm this in the text: 'لا نزاع' and 'عقلي' for the first, 'عقلي' for the second.
3
Focus on the third meaning – the subject of dispute
The passage says 'وهذا المعنى الثالث هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي'. This is the key claim: the third meaning is revealed (shar'i), not rational. Understand why this matters for the Ash'ari position on moral obligation.
The third meaning ties good and evil to divine reward and punishment, which only revelation can determine.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If an act's goodness depends on revelation, how does that affect how we know right from wrong? How does this view differ from the Mu'tazilite position?
This distinction is central to the Ash'ari-Mu'tazili debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : وبالله التوفيق ( الحسن والقبح يقال لمعان ثلاثة الأول صفة الكمال والنقص فالحسن كون الصفة صفة كمال والقبح كون الصفة صفة نقصان ) يقال : العلم حسن) أي لمن اتصف به كمال وارتفاع شأن ) والجهل (قبيح أي لمن اتصف به نقصان واتضاع حال ( ولا نزاع) في أن هذا المعنى أمر ثابت للصفات في أنفسها و أن مدركه العقل) ولا تعلق له بالشرع . ( الثاني : ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته ) فما وافق الغرض كان حسناً وما خالفه كان قبيحاً وما ليس كذلك لم يكن حسناً ولا قبيحاً ( وقد يعبر عنهما أي عن الحسن والقبح بهذا المعنى ( بالمصلحة والمفسدة ) فيقال : الحسن ما فيه مصلحة والقبيح ما فيه مفسدة، وما خلا عنهما لا يكون شيء منهما ( وذلك أيضاً عقلي ) أي مدركه العقل كالمعنى الأول ) ويختلف بالاعتبار فإن قتل زيد مصلحة لأعدائه ( وموافق لغرضهم ) ومفسدة لأوليائه ومخالف لغرضهم، فدل هذا الاختلاف على أنه أمر إضافي لا صفة حقيقة وإلا لم يختلف، كما لا يتصور كون الجسم الواحد أسود وأبيض بالقياس إلى شخصين ( الثالث : تعلق المدح والثواب ) بالفعل عاجلا وآجلاً ( أو الذم والعقاب ) كذلك، فما تعلق به المدح في العاجل والثواب في الآجل حسن، وما تعلق به الذم في العاجل والعقاب في الآجل يسمى قبيحا، وما لا يتعلق به شيء منهما فهو خارج عنهما ، هذا في أفعال العباد وإن أريد به ما يشمل أفعال الله تعالى اكتفي بتعلق المدح والذم وترك الثواب والعقاب، (وهذا المعنى الثالث ( هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي ) وذلك لأن الأفعال كلها سواسية ليس شيء
So we say, and by God is success: al-husn (good) and al-qubh (evil) are said for three meanings. The first: the attribute of perfection and deficiency: al-husn is that the attribute is an attribute of perfection, and al-qubh is that the attribute is an attribute of deficiency. It is said: knowledge is good, i.e., for the one characterized by it, it is perfection and elevation of status, and ignorance is evil, i.e., for the one characterized by it, it is deficiency and lowliness of state. And there is no dispute that this meaning is a matter established for attributes in themselves, and its perceiver is the intellect, and it has no connection to revelation. The second: compatibility with purpose and incompatibility: what agrees with the purpose is good, and what opposes it is evil, and what is neither is neither good nor evil. And they may be expressed, i.e., about good and evil in this sense, as benefit and harm. So it is said: good is what has benefit, evil is what has harm, and what is free of both is neither. And this also is rational, i.e., its perceiver is the intellect like the first meaning. And it varies by consideration; for example, killing Zayd is a benefit for his enemies and compatible with their purpose, and a harm for his allies and contrary to their purpose. So this variation indicates that it is a relational matter, not a real attribute, otherwise it would not vary, just as it is inconceivable that one body would be black and white in relation to two persons. The third: the attachment of praise and reward to the act immediately and in the afterlife, or blame and punishment similarly. So what praise in the immediate and reward in the afterlife is attached to is called good, and what blame in the immediate and punishment in the afterlife is attached to is called evil, and what nothing of that is attached to is outside them. This applies to the acts of servants, but if what is intended includes the acts of God, it suffices to mention the attachment of praise and blame, leaving aside reward and punishment. And this third meaning is the subject of dispute, and it is according to us shar'i (revealed). That is because all acts are equal; nothing is...
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the three meanings of al-husn wa al-qubh as part of clarifying the subject of dispute. It begins by enumerating the first meaning: perfection and deficiency (صفة الكمال والنقص). Good and evil in this sense are intrinsic attributes of qualities themselves, such as knowledge being good and ignorance evil. This meaning is rational and undisputed. The second meaning is compatibility with purpose (ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته): what benefits or harms in relation to a goal. This is also rational and relational, varying by perspective. The example of killing Zayd shows that the same act can be good for one party and evil for another, proving it is relative, not absolute. The third meaning is the attachment of praise and reward or blame and punishment (تعلق المدح والثواب). This is the point of contention: the Ash'aris hold that acts are not inherently worthy of reward or punishment; rather, revelation assigns these values. The passage concludes by identifying the third meaning as the subject of dispute and declaring it shar'i (revealed). The final sentence begins to argue that all acts are equal before revelation, but the passage is incomplete.

Key Terms
صفة الكمال والنقص
ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته
تعلق المدح والثواب
الشرعي
العقلي
المصلحة والمفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the good and evil of acts are always rational and do not depend on revelation.
Correction: While the first two meanings are rational, the third meaning (moral value in terms of reward/punishment) is determined by revelation according to Ash'aris.

Checkpoint
Which meaning of good and evil does the Ash'ari school identify as the subject of dispute and consider to be revealed (shar'i) rather than rational?
A) The first meaning: perfection and deficiency of attributes.
B) The second meaning: compatibility with purpose and benefit.
C) The third meaning: attachment of praise/reward and blame/punishment.
D) All three meanings are considered revealed by the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
The third meaning of good and evil—as related to reward and punishment—is the only disputed one, and the Ash'aris hold it to be determined solely by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, did you ever assume that certain actions were 'good' or 'evil' simply because they felt right or wrong? Now that you know the third meaning depends entirely on revelation, think of one action you currently believe is good or evil based on your feelings alone. Will you commit to testing that belief against the Quran and Sunnah today?
?
The first meaning says knowledge is a perfection and ignorance a deficiency. Are you more attached to seeking knowledge that elevates your status among people (this-worldly perfection) or knowledge that brings you closer to Allah and His reward (other-worldly perfection)? Name one change you need to make in your pursuit of knowledge.
?
The passage teaches that all acts are equal before revelation. When you perform acts of worship, do you sometimes feel they are 'good' because you enjoy them or they benefit you in this world? How can you make your intention purely for the hope of reward from Allah and fear of His punishment, regardless of worldly outcome? Resolve to perform one act of worship today solely for the Hereafter.

The Second Meaning of Ḥusn and Qubḥ: Congruence with Purpose
Defines the second meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ and explains its rational, relative nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ (congruence with purpose), its rational and relative nature, and how it differs from a real attribute.

Core Idea
Good and evil are relative; the same act can be good for one and evil for another.

This passage from al-Mawāqif defines the second meaning of good and evil — not as something inherent, but as congruence or opposition to an agent's purpose. It shows that such judgments are rational, relational, and subject to variation, unlike real attributes. The commentator clarifies that the purpose is specifically the agent's own purpose, which leads to important implications for describing actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a brief reference to the first meaning before focusing on the second.
2
Identify the definition of the second meaning
Find where the author defines the second meaning of good and evil as congruence with purpose. Note the phrasing: 'ما وافق الغرض كان حسناً وما خالفه كان قبيحاً'.
3
Understand the relativity argument
The author argues that this meaning is relational (iḍāfī) using the example of killing Zayd. Consider why the variation proves it is not a real attribute.
Think about why a real attribute cannot vary between observers.
4
Examine the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that 'purpose' means the agent's own purpose. Reflect on how this affects the judgment of an act from different agents.
This clarification has implications for acts done by different agents.
5
Apply the concept
Try to think of another act and determine when it would be good or evil according to this definition.
For example, consider lying: when is it congruent with an agent's purpose?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح
العقل) ولا تعلق له بالشرع . ( الثاني : ملاءمة الغرض ومنافرته ) فما وافق الغرض كان حسناً وما خالفه كان قبيحاً وما ليس كذلك لم يكن حسناً ولا قبيحاً ( وقد يعبر عنهما أي عن الحسن والقبح بهذا المعنى ( بالمصلحة والمفسدة ) فيقال : الحسن ما فيه مصلحة والقبيح ما فيه مفسدة، وما خلا عنهما لا يكون شيء منهما ( وذلك أيضاً عقلي ) أي مدركه العقل كالمعنى الأول ) ويختلف بالاعتبار فإن قتل زيد مصلحة لأعدائه ( وموافق لغرضهم ) ومفسدة لأوليائه ومخالف لغرضهم، فدل هذا الاختلاف على أنه أمر إضافي لا صفة حقيقة وإلا لم يختلف، كما لا يتصور كون الجسم الواحد أسود وأبيض بالقياس إلى شخصين . قوله : (فإن قتل زيد إلخ ) لا يخفى أن كلامه الذي نقلناه من شرح المختصر يدل على أن المراد من الغرض غرض الفاعل لا العام منه ومن غرض الغير وإلا لم يخرج فعل الله تعالى لما ذكره فحينئذ مراده من قوله :هاهنا : فإن قتل زيد إلخ إن قتل زيد إذا صدر عن أعدائه يكون حسناً بمعنى موافقته لغرض الفاعل الذي هو الأعداء ولو صدر عن أوليائه يكون قبيحاً بالنسبة إلى الفاعل الذي هو الأولياء، لكن يلزم على هذا أن لا يوصف القتل الصادر عن الأولياء بالحسن ولو بالنسبة إلى الأعداء ولا يوصف القتل الصادر عن الأعداء بالقبح ولو بالنسبة إلى الأولياء ولعل أصحاب هذا التعريف يلتزمونه .
The sixth observation - the fifth objective: On good and evil. (Reason) and it has no connection with the Law. Second: Congruence with the purpose and opposition to it. So what agrees with the purpose is good, what opposes it is evil, and what is neither is neither good nor evil. And they are sometimes expressed, i.e., good and evil in this sense, as benefit and harm. So it is said: good is what has benefit, evil is what has harm, and what is devoid of both is neither. And this too is rational, i.e., its perceiver is reason like the first meaning. And it varies according to consideration. For the killing of Zayd is a benefit for his enemies and congruent with their purpose, and a harm for his friends and opposed to their purpose. So this variation indicates that it is a relational matter, not a real attribute; otherwise it would not vary, just as it is inconceivable that a single body be black and white in relation to two persons. His saying: (For the killing of Zayd, etc.) It is not hidden that his statement which we transmitted from the commentary on al-Mukhtaṣar indicates that what is meant by purpose is the purpose of the agent, not the general purpose and not the purpose of another; otherwise the action of God would not be excluded by what he mentioned. So then his meaning by his saying here: For the killing of Zayd, etc., is that the killing of Zayd when it occurs from his enemies is good in the sense of its congruence with the purpose of the agent, who is the enemies, and if it occurs from his friends, it is evil in relation to the agent, who is the friends. But this entails that the killing occurring from the friends is not described as good even in relation to the enemies, and the killing occurring from the enemies is not described as evil even in relation to the friends. Perhaps the proponents of this definition commit to that.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the first two meanings of ḥusn (good) and qubḥ (evil) as discussed in kalām. It begins by heading the discussion under the sixth observation and fifth objective. The first meaning (mentioned briefly) is that good and evil are rational, independent of divine law. The second meaning defines good as what is congruent with an agent's purpose (mulaʾamat al-gharaḍ) and evil as what opposes it; what is neither is indifferent. This meaning is also rational and is sometimes expressed as benefit (maslaḥa) and harm (mafsada). The author emphasizes that this meaning is relative (iḍāfī), not a real attribute, because the same act, like killing Zayd, can be beneficial for enemies and harmful for friends. He argues that this relativity proves it is not a real attribute, otherwise it would not vary between different observers. The commentator (the sharḥ) then clarifies that the purpose in this definition is specifically the agent's own purpose, not a general purpose or another's purpose. Using the example of killing Zayd, he explains that if done by enemies it is good for them (congruent with their purpose), and if done by friends it is evil for them. He notes an implication: under this definition, the killing by friends is not considered good even relative to enemies, and vice versa. He suggests that the proponents of this definition may accept that consequence.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبح
العقل
ملاءمة الغرض
مصلحة
مفسدة
إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the second meaning of good and evil is absolute and does not vary between observers.
Correction: The passage explicitly argues that it is relational (iḍāfī), using the example of killing Zayd to show that the same act can be good for one person and evil for another.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the second meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ is relative (iḍāfī) and not a real attribute?
A) Because it is based on rational judgment independent of revelation.
B) Because the same act can be beneficial for one person and harmful for another, showing variation.
C) Because it is always defined by the Law.
D) Because it applies only to acts of God.

Key Takeaway
The second meaning of good and evil is rational, relational, and varies according to the agent's purpose, not an inherent quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you personally consider good. According to this lesson, is it good for every possible agent? What would it mean for your own sense of right and wrong if you discovered that someone else's purpose could make the same action evil for them? How does that realization challenge you to examine your own judgments before condemning others? Commit to pausing before judging someone whose motives you don't know.
?
The lesson uses the example of killing Zayd to show moral relativity based on purpose. When have you justified an action as good for yourself while forgetting that it might harm others? Are you willing to reconsider whether your personal purposes are truly just? Write down one area where your self-interest might blind you to how your actions appear to others—and what you will do about it this week.
?
If God's actions are excluded from this relational meaning because He has no purpose in the human sense, then your every action is subject to this judgment—your purposes either make your deeds good or evil in this sense. Look at your daily routines: what is the underlying purpose driving your actions? Is it alignment with truth and justice, or is it selfish desire? How can you align your purposes with what is truly good, beyond mere personal benefit?

The Third Meaning and the Ashʿarī Position
Defines the third meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ and establishes the Ashʿarī position that it is sharʿī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ as defined by the Ashʿarīs and why it is considered sharʿī rather than rational.

Core Idea
Moral value is not intrinsic; it is conferred by divine command.

In this passage, the author presents the third meaning of good and evil, which ties moral value to praise/reward and blame/punishment. This meaning is the locus of the debate between Ashʿarīs and Muʿtazila. The Ashʿarī position is that this moral value is determined by revelation, not reason, because all acts are equal in themselves. We will read the definition and examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the third meaning
Read the passage thoroughly, noticing how good and evil are defined in terms of praise/reward and blame/punishment.
The definition distinguishes three categories: good, evil, and neutral.
2
Understand the scope: human vs divine acts
Notice that for divine acts, only praise and blame are considered. Why might this be? The commentary explains it is to enable debate with the Muʿtazila.
3
Grasp the Ashʿarī claim that it is sharʿī
The author asserts that this third meaning is based on revelation. All acts are equal; none intrinsically necessitates praise or blame. Moral value arises only through divine command and prohibition.
4
Examine the rebuttal of a potential objection
Some Ahl al-Sunna argued for rational good and evil in human acts. Read how the commentator rebuts this, distinguishing between rational certainty and deserving reward/punishment.

الثالث : تعلق المدح والثواب ) بالفعل عاجلا وآجلاً ( أو الذم والعقاب ) كذلك، فما تعلق به المدح في العاجل والثواب في الآجل سناً، وما تعلق به الذم في العاجل والعقاب في الآجل يسمى قبيحا، وما لا يتعلق به شيء منهما فهو خارج عنهما ، هذا في أفعال العباد وإن أريد به ما يشمل أفعال الله تعالى اكتفي بتعلق المدح والذم وترك الثواب والعقاب، (وهذا المعنى الثالث ( هو محل النزاع فهو عندنا شرعي ) وذلك لأن الأفعال كلها سواسية ليس شيء منها في نفسه بحيث يقتضي مدح فاعله وثوابه ولا ذم فاعله وعقابه وإنما صارت كذلك بواسطة أمر الشارع بها ونهيه عنها . قوله : (فما تعلق به المدح إلخ لا يخفى أن هذا يخالف التعريف السابق المختار عند الأكثرين لشمول ذلك للمباح بخلاف هذا . قوله : (اكتفي بتعلق المدح والذم أي اكتفى في المعنى الثالث ليصح محل النزاع بيننا وبين المعتزلة في أفعال الباري تعالى فإن المعتزلة فرعوا على هذا الأصل تنزيه الباري تعالى عن القبائح لإيجابها استحقاق الذم لا لاستحقاق العقاب . قوله : (فهو عندنا شرعي) ذهب بعض أهل السنة إلى الحسن والقبح العقليين في أفعال العباد دون أفعال الله تعالى مستدلاً عليه بأن وجوب تصديق النبي عليه السلام إن توقف على الشرع لزم الدور وإلا كان واجباً عقلياً فيكون حسناً عقلاً فإذا وجب تصديقه حرم تكذيبه فيكون قبيحا عقلا ورد بأن وجوب تصديقه بمعنى جزم العقل بصدقه للدلائل العقلية مما لا نزاع فيه كالتصديق بوجود الصانع و أما بمعنى استحقاق الثواب والعقاب فيجوز إن ثبت بنص الشارع. قوله : (سواسية) قال الأخفش : سواسية أي أشباه فإن سواء فعال وسية يجوز أن يكون فعه
Third: The connection of praise and reward with the act immediately and in the future, or blame and punishment likewise. So what is connected with praise immediately and reward in the future is called good, and what is connected with blame immediately and punishment in the future is called evil, and what is not connected with either is outside both. This is concerning the acts of servants; but if what is intended includes the acts of God, one is satisfied with the connection of praise and blame and leaves aside reward and punishment. And this third meaning is the locus of dispute; for us it is sharʿī. That is because all acts are equal; none of them is such in itself that it necessitates praise of its doer and reward, nor blame of its doer and punishment. Rather, they become so only by means of the Lawgiver's command and prohibition. His saying: (For what is connected with praise, etc.) It is not hidden that this contradicts the previous definition chosen by the majority, because that definition included the permissible, unlike this one. His saying: (One is satisfied with the connection of praise and blame) i.e., one is satisfied in the third meaning in order that the locus of dispute between us and the Muʿtazila concerning the acts of God be sound. For the Muʿtazila built upon this principle the transcendence of God above evils because they necessitate deserving blame, not because they necessitate deserving punishment. His saying: (For us it is sharʿī) Some of the Ahl al-Sunna went to rational good and evil in the acts of servants but not in the acts of God, adducing that the obligation to believe the Prophet, if dependent on the Law, would entail a circularity; otherwise it would be rationally obligatory, so it would be rationally good, and if belief is obligatory, then denial is forbidden, so it would be rationally evil. It is rebutted that the obligation to believe him, in the sense of the mind's certainty of his truthfulness due to rational proofs, is uncontroversial, like belief in the existence of the Creator; but in the sense of deserving reward and punishment, it is permissible if established by the text of the Lawgiver. His saying: (Sawāsiya) Al-Akhfash said: sawāsiya means likenesses; for sawāʾ is fiʿāl, and siya may be...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the third meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ and presents the Ashʿarī position. The third meaning ties good and evil to praise/reward and blame/punishment, both immediate and eschatological. For human acts, an act is good if it merits praise now and reward later, evil if it merits blame now and punishment later. For divine acts, only praise and blame are considered, since reward and punishment are less relevant to God. This third meaning is the point of disagreement (maḥall al-nizāʿ). The Ashʿarī author asserts that for us (the Ashʿarīs), this meaning is sharʿī (based on revelation), because all acts are equal in themselves; none intrinsically necessitates praise or blame. Moral qualities arise only through divine command and prohibition. The commentator then explains that this third meaning differs from the previous definition (the second meaning) because it excludes permissible acts, which the previous definition included. The commentator also explains why only praise and blame are mentioned for God: to enable debate with the Muʿtazila, who base God's transcendence on avoiding blame (not punishment). The commentator then records a debate among some Ahl al-Sunna: some argue for rational good and evil in human acts only, using the example of believing prophets—if belief were not rationally obligatory, a circularity would result; thus it is rational. The rebuttal clarifies that rational obligation (wujūb) can mean certainty of truth, which is acceptable, but deserving reward/punishment is known only by revelation. Finally, a lexical note explains the word 'sawāsiya' as 'likenesses'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ashʿarī position denies any rational basis for moral judgments entirely.
Correction: The Ashʿarīs accept rational knowledge of God's existence and certain obligations, but they hold that merit (thawāb/ʿiqāb) and specific moral values are determined by revelation. The passage states that all acts are equal in themselves regarding praise/reward and blame/punishment, but does not deny rational knowledge of God.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are all created by God.
B) Because none in themselves necessitate praise, reward, blame, or punishment.
C) Because the intellect cannot distinguish good from evil at all.
D) Because they are all permissible until revelation comes.

Key Takeaway
The third meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ ties moral value to praise/reward and blame/punishment, and the Ashʿarīs hold that this value is determined by revelation because acts are intrinsically neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all acts are neutral in themselves and moral value comes only from God's command, how has your own understanding of right and wrong been shaped more by culture or personal preference than by revelation? What specific step can you take this week to align your moral judgments with divine command?
?
This lesson defines 'good' as that which merits praise and reward. In your daily life, are you more motivated by seeking God's reward or by avoiding human criticism? Which areas of your life need a shift toward divine accountability?
?
The Ashʿarī position denies intrinsic moral value. Have you ever struggled to accept a divine command because your reason judged it as 'unjust' or 'unfair'? How will you submit to God's command even when it conflicts with your own sense of fairness?

Muʿtazilī View: Ḥusn and Qubḥ Are Rational
Muʿtazilī threefold division of how moral qualities are known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muʿtazilī position that good and evil are intrinsic rational qualities, and how they classify the perception of these qualities into necessity, reflection, and revelation.

Core Idea
Good and evil are objective qualities known by reason, and sometimes by revelation.

The Muʿtazila argued that acts have inherent qualities of good and evil, independent of divine command. They distinguished three ways these qualities are known: by immediate necessity, by reflective reasoning, or only through revelation. This passage presents their classification and provides key examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to capture the overall argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms: jihat muḥsina and jihat muqabbiḥa.
2
Identify the two key terms
Note that 'jihat muḥsina' means a quality making an act good, and 'jihat muqabbiḥa' means a quality making it evil. These are inherent in the act itself.
3
Understand the three categories
The passage divides how these qualities are perceived into three: by necessity (ḍarūra), by reflection (naẓar), and not by reason but only by revelation (sharʿ).
Each category has a distinct example.
4
Examine the examples
Beneficial truthfulness/harmful lying (necessity); harmful truthfulness/beneficial lying (reflection); fasting last day of Ramadan/first day of Shawwāl (revelation only).
Reflect on why the first two can be known by reason but the last cannot.
5
Note the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that the necessary perception concerns the second meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ (congruence with purpose), not the third meaning (praise/reward), which is the actual point of dispute.
This avoids confusion about what is being argued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وعند المعتزلة عقلي ) فإنهم ( قالوا : للفعل ) في نفسه مع قطع النظر عن الشرع ( جهة محسنة ) مقتضية لاستحقاق فاعله مدحاً وثواباً ( أو مقبحة) مقتضية لاستحقاق فاعلة ذماً وعقاباً، ثم إنها ) أي تلك الجهة المحسنة أو المقبحة ( قد تدرك بالضرورة ) من غير تأمل وفكر ( كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار فإن كل عاقل يحكم بهما بلا توقف ( وقد تدرك بالنظر كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع مثلا وقد لا تدرك بالعقل ) لا بالضرورة ولا بالنظر ) ولكن إذا ورد به الشرع علم أنه ثمة جهة محسنة كما في صوم آخر يوم من (رمضان) حيث أوجبه الشارع ( أو ) جهة ( مقبحة كصوم أول يوم من شوال) حيث حرمه الشارع فإدراك الحسن والقبح في هذا القسم موقوف على كشف الشرع عنهما بأمره ونهيه . وأما كشفه عنهما في القسمين الأولين فهو مؤيد لحكم العقل بهما إما بضرورته أو بنظره ). قوله : ( كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار لا يخفى أن المدرك بالضرورة فيهما هو الحسن والقبح بالمعنى الثاني لا بالمعنى الثالث المتنازع فيه
And with the Muʿtazila it is rational. For they said: For the act in itself, without regard to the Law, there is a quality that makes it good (jihat muḥsina) necessitating that its doer deserves praise and reward, or a quality that makes it evil (muqabbiḥa) necessitating that its doer deserves blame and punishment. Then that quality, i.e., the good-making or evil-making one, is sometimes perceived by necessity (bi-ḍ-ḍarūra) without reflection and thought, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful lying, for every rational person judges them without hesitation. And it is sometimes perceived by reflection (bi-n-naẓar), like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evil of beneficial lying, for example. And it may not be perceived by reason, neither by necessity nor by reflection, but when the Law comes with it, it is known that there is a good-making quality, like fasting the last day of Ramadan where the Lawgiver made it obligatory, or an evil-making quality like fasting the first day of Shawwāl where the Lawgiver forbade it. So the perception of good and evil in this division depends on the Law's disclosure of them by its command and prohibition. As for the Law's disclosure of them in the first two divisions, it is a confirmation of reason's judgment regarding them, either by its necessity or by its reflection. His saying: (Like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful lying) It is not hidden that what is perceived by necessity in these two is good and evil in the second meaning, not in the third meaning that is disputed.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage explains the Muʿtazilī position that ḥusn and qubḥ are rational ('aqliyyān). The Muʿtazila hold that acts in themselves, independent of divine law, possess a quality of being good or evil (jihat muḥsina or muqabbiḥa) that makes the doer deserve praise/reward or blame/punishment. They then classify how these qualities are known: (1) By necessity (ḍarūra) — without reflection, e.g., the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful lying; every rational person immediately knows this. (2) By reflection (naẓar) — e.g., the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evil of beneficial lying, which require thought. (3) Not known by reason at all, but only through revelation — like the goodness of fasting the last day of Ramadan (obligatory) and the evil of fasting the first day of Shawwāl (forbidden). In this third division, revelation discloses the presence of a good-making or evil-making quality that reason could not discover. Revelation in the first two divisions merely confirms reason's judgment. The commentator adds a crucial note: the examples of beneficial truthfulness and harmful lying are perceived by necessity, but this perception concerns the second meaning of ḥusn and qubḥ (congruence with purpose), not the third meaning (praise/reward) which is the real point of dispute. This is an important clarification to avoid confusion between the meanings.

Key Terms
جهة محسنة
جهة مقبحة
ضرورة
نظر
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Muʿtazila believe all moral knowledge is known by reason without revelation.
Correction: They accept a category where moral values are known only through revelation, like specific religious obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the Muʿtazilī classification in this passage, how is the goodness of fasting the last day of Ramadan known?
A) By necessity (ḍarūra).
B) By reflection (naẓar).
C) Only through revelation.
D) It is not known at all.

Key Takeaway
The Muʿtazila hold that good and evil are rational, but they can be known by necessity, reflection, or only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Muʿtazila claim that some acts are good or evil by necessity, like beneficial truthfulness. Is there a time when you instinctively knew something was right or wrong without anyone telling you? How does that experience align with or challenge this claim?
?
Consider an act that revelation declares good but your reason finds puzzling (e.g., fasting the last day of Ramadan—why is that obligatory?). Does this lesson's third category give you a way to submit to divine wisdom even when you do not see the intrinsic goodness? Be honest: how do you handle such moments?
?
If good and evil are truly objective and known by reason, then you are accountable for discovering them. Have you ever excused a moral failing by saying 'I didn't know'? After studying this lesson, how does that excuse sound? What will you do differently when you face a moral decision where reason and revelation seem to diverge?

The Mu'tazila Doctrine of Rational Good and Evil
Jarjani explains the Mu'tazila claim that good and evil are rational and their three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view that moral values are inherent in actions, and how they are perceived in three categories: necessarily, through reflection, or solely through revelation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila believe actions have intrinsic moral qualities known by reason or, failing that, by revelation.

In this passage, Jarjani explains the Mu'tazila doctrine that actions possess inherent qualities of goodness or badness, independent of divine command. He then categorizes how these qualities are perceived: some are known necessarily, some through reflection, and some only through revelation. This framework is essential for understanding Mu'tazila ethics and their debates with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to the technical terms for goodness, badness, and the modes of perception.
The passage uses technical terms like 'جهة محسنة' and 'جهة مقبحة'.
2
Identify the three categories
Note how the passage divides the perception of moral qualities into three types: by necessity (ضرورة), by reflection (نظر), and by revelation (شرع). Each has a distinct example.
The examples are: necessary – beneficial truthfulness; reflective – harmful truthfulness; revelatory – fasting on specific days.
3
Understand the role of revelation
Recognize that for the Mu'tazila, revelation confirms reason in the first two categories but is the sole source in the third. This distinguishes them from other schools.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can correctly classify examples according to the three categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وعند المعتزلة عقلي) فإنهم (قالوا: للفعل) في نفسه مع قطع النظر عن الشرع (جهة محسنة) مقتضية لاستحقاق فاعله مدحاً وثواباً (أو مقبحة) مقتضية لاستحقاق فاعلة ذماً وعقاباً، ثم إنها) أي تلك الجهة المحسنة أو المقبحة (قد تدرك بالضرورة) من غير تأمل وفكر (كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار فإن كل عاقل يحكم بهما بلا توقف (وقد تدرك بالنظر كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع مثلا وقد لا تدرك بالعقل) لا بالضرورة ولا بالنظر (ولكن إذا ورد به الشرع علم أنه ثمة جهة محسنة كما في صوم آخر يوم من رمضان حيث أوجبه الشارع (أو) جهة (مقبحة كصوم أول يوم من شوال) حيث حرمه الشارع فإدراك الحسن والقبح في هذا القسم موقوف على كشف الشرع عنهما بأمره ونهيه . وأما كشفه عنهما في القسمين الأولين فهو مؤيد لحكم العقل بهما إما بضرورته أو بنظره)
And according to the Mu'tazila, it is rational. For they said: For the action in itself, irrespective of the Revealed Law, there is a quality of goodness that necessitates its doer deserving praise and reward, or a quality of badness that necessitates its doer deserving blame and punishment. Then these — that is, that quality of goodness or badness — may be perceived necessarily, without reflection or thought, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the badness of harmful lying, for every rational person judges them without hesitation. And it may be perceived by reflection, like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the badness of beneficial lying, for example. And it may not be perceived by reason, neither necessarily nor by reflection, but when the Revealed Law comes concerning it, one knows that there is a quality of goodness — as in fasting the last day of Ramadan where the Lawgiver made it obligatory — or a quality of badness — like fasting the first day of Shawwal where the Lawgiver prohibited it. So the perception of goodness and badness in this category depends on the Revealed Law revealing them by its command and prohibition. As for its revealing them in the first two categories, it confirms the judgment of reason regarding them, whether by its necessity or by its reflection.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position that moral values (good and evil) are rational, meaning they are inherent in actions independently of divine command. It first states the claim: actions have an inherent quality of goodness (giving praise and reward) or badness (giving blame and punishment). Then it divides the perception of these qualities into three categories: (1) perceived necessarily, without reflection, such as the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the badness of harmful lying; (2) perceived through reflection, such as the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the badness of beneficial lying; and (3) not perceived by reason at all, neither necessarily nor through reflection, but known only through revelation, as in the case of fasting on the last day of Ramadan (obligatory) or the first day of Shawwal (prohibited). In the third category, revelation discloses the moral quality. In the first two, revelation merely confirms what reason already knows. The passage establishes the Mu'tazila framework before proceeding to internal disagreements.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Mu'tazila believe all moral knowledge comes from reason alone.
Correction: Mu'tazila argue that while many moral truths are known by reason, some are only accessible through revelation, as in the third category here.

Can you think of an action in your own life that you accept as obligatory or forbidden simply because Allah commanded it, even if your mind doesn't see the wisdom?
