The First Ash'ari Proof Against Rational Good and Evil: Argument from Compulsion
Jarjani presents the first of two proofs that good/evil are not rational, based on the compulsion of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tripartite analysis of human action in the Ash'ari argument from compulsion, and why it undermines the claim that good and evil are rationally known.

Core Idea
If human actions are compelled, reason cannot judge them as good or evil.

The Ash'ari school argues that good and evil are not rational categories but are determined by revelation. One key proof is the argument from compulsion: human actions are not freely chosen in a way that would allow reason to assign moral value. In this passage, Jarjani lays out a tripartite analysis of human action to show that in every possible scenario, the action lacks the necessary voluntary quality for moral judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis
Read the opening claim: the servant is compelled in his actions, so reason does not judge them as good or evil. This is the conclusion the argument aims to support.
The opponents (Mu'tazila) agree that only voluntary acts are morally judged.
2
Examine the first case: inability to refrain
If the servant cannot refrain from the action, that is pure compulsion (jabr). The action is necessary and refraining impossible. Clearly not voluntary.
3
Examine the second case: ability to refrain but no preponderating cause
If the servant can refrain but acts without a preponderating factor (murajjih), the action is accidental (ittifaqi) and not voluntary. Voluntary action requires a decisive will.
This case rules out random or capricious actions as voluntary.
4
Examine the third case: dependence on a preponderating factor
If the action depends on a preponderating factor, that factor cannot come from the servant (to avoid infinite regress). Thus the action is necessitated by an external factor, making it necessary, not voluntary.
The argument concludes that all human actions fall into one of these three non-voluntary categories.
5
Conclude the argument
Since in every scenario the action is not genuinely voluntary, reason cannot assign moral judgments of good or evil to human actions. Therefore good and evil are known only through revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تصورت هذا التحرير نقول : لنا على أن الحسن والقبح ليسا عقليين وجهان : الأول : أن العبد مجبور في أفعاله وإذا كان كذلك لم يحكم العقل فيها بحسن ولا قبح لأن ما ليس فعلاً اختيارياً لا يتصف بهذه الصفات اتفاقاً منا ومن الخصوم . بيانه أن العبد إن لم يتمكن الترك فذاك هو الجبر لأن الفعل حينئذ واجب، والترك ممتنع وإن تمكن من الترك ولم يتوقف وجود الفعل منه على مرجح بل صدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى من غير سبب يرجح وجوده على عدمه كان ذلك الفعل حينئذ اتفاقياً صادراً بلا سبب يقتضيه فلا يكون اختيارياً لأن الفعل الاختياري لا بد له من إرادة جازمة ترجحه وإن توقف وجود الفعل منه على مرجح لم يكن ذلك المرجح من العبد وإلا نقلنا الكلام إلى صدور ذلك المرجح عنه وتسلسل وهو محال ووجب الفعل عنده أي عند المرجح الذي يتوقف عليه وإلا جاز الفعل
And when you have conceived this exposition, we say: We have two proofs that good and evil are not rational: The first: That the servant is compelled in his actions. And if this is so, reason does not judge in them as good or evil, because what is not a voluntary action is not described by these attributes, by agreement between us and the opponents. Its explanation: The servant, if he cannot refrain, then that is compulsion, for then the action is necessary and refraining is impossible. And if he can refrain and the existence of the action from him does not depend on a preponderating factor, but rather occurs from him sometimes and does not occur at other times without a cause that preponderates its existence over its non-existence, then that action is accidental, occurring without a cause that necessitates it, and so it is not voluntary, because the voluntary action must have a decisive will that preponderates it. And if the existence of the action from him depends on a preponderating factor, then that preponderating factor is not from the servant; otherwise we would transfer the discussion to the occurrence of that preponderating factor from him and there would be an infinite regress, which is impossible. And the action becomes necessary at that preponderating factor on which it depends; otherwise the action would be permissible.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two Ash'ari arguments (wajhan) against the Mu'tazila claim that good and evil are rational. The argument begins by asserting that the human servant ('abd) is compelled (majbur) in his actions. If so, reason cannot judge them morally. The explanation proceeds by exhausting possibilities: (1) If the servant cannot refrain from the action, that is compulsion (jabr) — the action is necessary, refraining impossible. (2) If he can refrain but the action occurs without a preponderating factor (murajjih), then it is accidental (ittifaqi), not caused, and therefore not voluntary, since voluntary action requires a decisive will (irada jazima). (3) If the action depends on a preponderating factor, that factor cannot come from the servant (otherwise infinite regress); thus the action becomes necessary at that factor. In all three cases, the action is not freely chosen and thus cannot be subject to rational moral judgment. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila basis for rational good/evil is undermined because human actions lack the necessary voluntary quality.

Key Terms
الجبر
الاختيار
الإرادة الجازمة
المرجح
التسلسل
الاتفاقي
العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument from compulsion denies all human responsibility for actions.
Correction: The argument does not deny human responsibility; it denies that reason independently judges moral value without revelation. Actions can still have moral value based on divine command.

Checkpoint
What is the second possibility in the Ash'ari argument: if the servant can refrain but acts without a preponderating cause?
A) The action is necessary
B) The action is accidental and not voluntary
C) The action is voluntary
D) The action is good

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument from compulsion shows that human actions lack the voluntary quality required for rational moral judgment, thereby supporting the position that good and evil are revealed rather than rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone's action as 'evil', do you first pause to consider whether they could truly have chosen otherwise? How often do you judge without that pause?
?
This argument suggests that without true choice, moral judgment collapses. Where in your own life do you assume others have full freedom to choose, perhaps unjustly condemning them?
?
If you truly believed that every action is compelled by factors beyond your control, how would that change your sense of guilt, repentance, or gratitude? Are you willing to live with that implication?

The First Two Cases: Compulsion and Accident
Sanusi presents the first two cases of his determinist argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two scenarios of the determinist argument and why they exclude voluntary action, establishing that the servant cannot have free choice in these cases.

Core Idea
Without a decisive will, an act is either necessary or accidental—neither is voluntary.

Sanusi begins his proof that good and evil are not rational by examining the nature of human action. He first clarifies the term 'accidental' (ittifāqī) and then outlines two straightforward cases where the servant's act is not voluntary: when he cannot refrain (jabr) and when he can but there is no preponderant cause (ittifāq). In both, the act lacks the decisive will required for moral description.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage up to the third case
Read the Arabic from the beginning until before 'wa-in tawaqqafa' ('وإن توقف'). This covers the clarification and the first two cases.
Notice the definition of 'accidental' given at the start.
2
Identify the first case: inability to refrain
Find the sentence 'That if the servant is not able to refrain, that is compulsion (al-jabr)'. Understand that this makes the act necessary.
3
Identify the second case: ability to refrain without a preponderant
Find the sentence about ability to refrain and the act occurring without a cause. This results in an accidental act, which is not voluntary.
The author notes that a voluntary act requires a decisive will (irāda jāzima).
4
Summarize the two cases
Reflect that both cases eliminate free choice. In the first, refraining is impossible; in the second, there is no cause for the act, so it is random.
This sets up the third case which is more complex.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (صادراً بلا سبب أشار إلى أنه المراد بالاتفاق هاهنا وإن كان معناه في المنطق ما يتوقف على مرجح تام لا نعلمه بعينه . وإذا تصورت هذا التحرير نقول : (لنا ) على أن الحسن والقبح ليسا عقليين ( وجهان : الأول : أن العبد مجبور في أفعاله وإذا كان كذلك لم يحكم العقل فيها بحسن ولا قبح لأن ما ليس فعلاً اختيارياً لا يتصف بهذه الصفات ( اتفاقاً) منا ومن الخصوم . ( بيانه ) أي بيان كونه مجبوراً ( أن العبد إن لم يتمكن الترك فذاك هو الجبر ) لأن الفعل حينئذ واجب، والترك ممتنع ( وإن تمكن من الترك ( ولم يتوقف ) وجود الفعل منه ( على مرجح بل صدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى من غير سبب ) يرجح وجوده على عدمه ( كان ذلك ) الفعل حينئذ (اتفاقياً صادراً بلا سبب يقتضيه فلا يكون اختيارياً لأن الفعل الاختياري لا بد له من إرادة جازمة ترجحه ( وإن توقف ) وجود الفعل منه ) على مرجح لم يكن ذلك) المرجح ( من العبد وإلا نقلنا الكلام إلى صدور ذلك المرجح عنه و(تسلسل) وهو محال ( ووجب الفعل عنده ) أي عند المرجح الذي يتوقف عليه ( وإلا جاز الفعل والترك واحتاج حينئذ إلى مرجح (آخر) إذ لو لم يحتج إليه وصدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى كان اتفاقياً كما مر . وإذا احتاج إلى مرجح آخر نقلنا الكلام إليه ( وتسلسل، فيكون) الفعل على تقدير وجوبه مع ذلك المرجح (اضطرارياً وعلى التقادير) أعني امتناع الترك وكون الفعل اتفاقياً واضطرارياً ( فلا اختيار للعبد) في أفعاله ( فيكون مجبوراً فيها فلا يتصف شيء منها بالحسن والقبح العقليين بالإجماع المركب، أما عندنا فلأنه لا مدخل للعقل فيهما، وأما عندهم صفات فلأنهما من الأفعال الاختيارية )
His saying: ('Proceeding without a cause' he indicated that this is what is meant by 'accidental' (al-ittifāq) here, even if its meaning in logic is what depends on a complete preponderant that we do not know precisely. And when you have understood this explanation, we say: We have two proofs that good and evil are not rational: The first: that the servant is compelled (majbūr) in his acts; and if that is so, reason does not judge them as good or evil, because what is not a voluntary act is not described with these qualities—by agreement between us and the opponents. (Its explanation) i.e., the explanation that he is compelled: (that if the servant is not able to refrain, that is compulsion (al-jabr)) because then the act is necessary and refraining is impossible. (And if he is able to refrain) (and the existence of the act from him does not depend) (on a preponderant) but occurs sometimes and does not occur at other times without a cause that preponderates its existence over non-existence, (then) that act (is accidental, proceeding without a cause necessitating it, and is not voluntary, because the voluntary act necessarily requires a decisive will that preponderates it. (And if the existence of the act from him depends) on a preponderant, then) that preponderant (is not from the servant; otherwise we would transfer the discourse to the occurrence of that preponderant from him and there would be an infinite regress, which is impossible. (And the act becomes necessary at the time of that preponderant) (i.e., at the preponderant on which it depends) (otherwise, the act and the refraining would be possible, and then it would need another preponderant, because if it did not need it and occurred sometimes and not at other times, it would be accidental as before. And if it needs another preponderant, we transfer the discourse to it and there would be an infinite regress. (Thus, the act, on the assumption of its necessity due to that preponderant, is compulsory (iḍṭirārī). And on all the suppositions—i.e., the impossibility of refraining, the act being accidental, and the act being compulsory—(there is no free choice for the servant) in his actions, (so he is compelled in them, and thus none of them is described as good or evil rationally, by composite consensus. As for us, because reason has no entrance in them; as for them (the opponents), the descriptions are from voluntary acts).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument that good and evil are not rational, based on determinism (jabr). The author begins with a note clarifying the term 'accidental' (ittifāqī): it means an event without a known cause, unlike its logical sense. Then the main argument: The servant (al-ʿabd) has no free choice; reason cannot judge coerced acts as good or evil. The proof proceeds by analyzing three cases: (1) If the servant cannot refrain, the act is necessary (jabr) and not voluntary. (2) If the servant can refrain but there is no preponderant cause, the act is accidental and also not voluntary. (3) If the servant can refrain and the act depends on a preponderant, then either that preponderant comes from the servant (leading to infinite regress) or it is from outside, making the act compulsory. In all cases, no free will exists, so no act is eligible for rational moral judgment. This conclusion is agreed by the author's school (Ashʿari) and even by the opponents' criteria, as they require acts to be voluntary for moral description. The argument systematically eliminates possibility of freedom, aiming to show that good and evil cannot be rationally discerned.

Key Terms
الجبر
الاختيار
الاتفاق
الإرادة الجازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for an act to be considered voluntary?
A) The ability to refrain
B) A decisive will that preponderates the act
C) The absence of a preponderant cause
D) The necessity of the act

Key Takeaway
In the first two cases, the servant's act is either necessary or accidental, neither of which involves free choice, so reason cannot judge it as good or evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an action you performed today without truly deciding—maybe out of habit or impulse. In light of Sanusi's first two cases, was that act truly voluntary? Does your accountability before Allah depend on whether you exercised a decisive will?
?
If every act you do without a decisive will is either compelled or accidental, how many of your daily deeds are genuinely your own? Will you examine one action today and ask yourself: 'Did I really want this, or did it just happen?'
?
The lesson says reason cannot judge an act as good or evil if it is not voluntary. Think of a sin you committed without full intention. Does that excuse you? Or does it reveal a deeper problem—that your will is not engaged with obedience? What will you do to strengthen your decisive will for good actions starting now?

The Third Case: Preponderant and Infinite Regress
Sanusi completes the determinist argument with the third case and concludes that the servant is compelled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third case of the determinist argument, including the infinite regress objection, and how it concludes that the servant is compelled in all acts, thus no rational moral judgment.

Core Idea
If the act depends on a preponderant, that preponderant cannot come from the servant—otherwise infinite regress—so the act is compulsory.

Sanusi now tackles the remaining possibility: the act depends on a preponderant cause. He argues that if this preponderant comes from the servant, it leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible. If it comes from outside, the act becomes necessary and compulsory. Thus, in all three scenarios, the servant has no free choice. This completes the proof that the servant is compelled, and therefore good and evil are not rational judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third case
Read from 'wa-in tawaqqafa' (وإن توقف) to the end of the passage. This covers the third case and the conclusion.
Focus on the conditional reasoning and the conclusion that the act is compulsory.
2
Follow the regress argument
If the preponderant is from the servant, we ask what caused that preponderant? This leads to an infinite chain (tasalsul), which is impossible.
Infinite regress is a standard impossibility argument in kalām.
3
Understand the necessity if preponderant is from outside
If the preponderant is not from the servant, the act becomes necessary at the time of that preponderant. If it were not necessary, it would need another preponderant, leading to regress again.
Thus, the act is compulsory (iḍṭirārī).
4
Conclude the argument
In all three possibilities (impossible refrain, accidental, compulsory), the servant has no free choice. Therefore, the servant is compelled, and acts cannot be judged rationally as good or evil.
Sanusi notes that even the opponents would agree that moral descriptions require voluntary acts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any form of human responsibility or causality.
Correction: The argument is about the ultimate source of the preponderant cause, not about human experience of choice. It aims to show that rational moral judgment cannot be based on voluntary acts because the chain of causes ultimately depends on God.

Checkpoint
What happens if the preponderant cause is assumed to come from the servant?
A) The act becomes voluntary
B) It leads to an infinite regress
C) The act becomes accidental
D) The act is impossible

Key Takeaway
The third case completes the proof that the servant is compelled, because any attempt to ground the act in a preponderant from the servant leads to infinite regress, and an external preponderant makes the act compulsory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every act of yours depends on a preponderant that is not from you, do you truly believe that your moment of sin was not in your control? How does that change the way you seek forgiveness—are you more reliant on Allah's mercy or more reckless with His commands?
?
Sanusi concludes that you are compelled in every action. Yet you feel guilt, pride, and responsibility. Are these feelings simply illusions? If they are real, how do you reconcile them with the proof that you have no free choice? What does that say about Allah's justice?
?
The passage says there is 'no free choice for the servant' and thus no act is rationally good or evil. Does this mean you should stop striving to obey Allah and avoid sin? If not, what compels you to act—and can that compulsion be a form of worship?

Refuting the Objection from Self-Evidence and Divine Power
Dismissal of objections from commonsense and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the determinist argument addresses objections that deny its validity based on the self-evident feeling of free will and the apparent implication that God's power is negated.

Core Idea
The determinist argument is universal; objections from commonsense and divine power are dismissed as sophistry.

This lesson tackles two initial objections to the determinist argument: first, that it contradicts the self-evident knowledge that humans have free will, and second, that the same reasoning would apply to God, denying His power. We will see how the author dismisses the first as sophistry and responds to the second by showing that the argument's universal application does not invalidate it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two objections
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the first objection is from self-evident free will (الضرورة), the second from divine power.
2
Understand the dismissal of the first objection
The author says the first objection is 'futile sophistry and blatant stubbornness' because it relies on a feeling that is not a valid proof in kalam.
This reflects the Ash'ari position that rational self-evidence is sometimes overridden by theological reasoning.
3
Analyze the response to the second objection
The author points out that if the argument applies to all agents, then God is included. This is not a refutation but a consistency check. The response to any answer they give is the same answer we give.
This shows the argument's strength: it is not refuted by its own implications.
4
Reflect on the overall strategy
The author uses a 'tu quoque' style argument: the same reasoning the opponent uses against us can be turned back against them.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا أي استدلالكم على كون العبد مجبوراً ( نصب للدليل في مقابلة الضرورة) إذ كل واحد من العقلاء يعلم أن له اختياراً في أفعاله يفرق بين الاختياري والاضطراري منها ( فلا يسمع) لأنه سفسطة باطلة ومكابرة ظاهرة، وأيضاً فإنه أي دليلكم ) ينفي قدرة الله تعالى لاطراد الدليل في أفعاله والمقدمات المقدمات والتقرير (التقرير فيقال : إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإن تمكن منه لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر فقد انتقض الدليل المذكور بأفعاله تعالى (وأيضاً فإنه أي هذا الدليل كما ينفي الحسن والقبح العقليين ( ينفي) أيضاً (الحسن والقبح الشرعيين المتفرعين على ثبوت التكليف، وإذا كان مجبوراً لم يثبت عليه تكليف لأنه تكليف مالا يطاق) ونحن لا نجوزه ) وأنتم وإن جوزتموه فلا تقولون بوقوعه ولا يكون كل التكاليف كذلك ) أي تكليفاً ( بما لا يطاق كما لزم من دليلكم، والحاصل أن كون العبد مجبوراً ينافي كونه مكلفاً يوصف فعله بحسن ولا قبح شرعي مع أنهما ثابتان عندكم فانتقض دليلكم بهما، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا والأظهر أن يقال : إنه ينفي الشرعيين أيضاً لأن أيضاً لأنهما من العبد فلا . قوله : ( وتسلسل وهو محال قد مر معنا أنه إنما يلزم التسلسل إذا كان صادراً عن العبد باختياره، وأما إذا صدر عنه لا به فلا واعلم أن بعض الاعتراضات التي أوردناها في المقصد الأول من هذا المرصد متوجه على ماذكره في هذا المقصد أيضاً فلا حاجة إلى تكريرها فليطلب منه . قوله : (كان اتفاقياً كما مر أي صادراً بلا سبب يقتضيه ويخصص وجوده بالنسبة إلى ذلك الوقت الذي وقع فيه فإن قلت : المرجح الأول يجوز أن يرجح وقت وجوده على سائر الأوقات كما أنه نفس وجوده على عدمه إذ لا ممانعة بين المرجحين. قلت: لما كان يرجح المرجح الأول هو الاختيار المعلق به في الوقتين معا على الغرض السابق كان نسبته إلى الوقتين حينئذ على السوية فلا معنى لترجيحه وقت وجوده على سائر الأوقات وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( والأظهر أن يقال إلخ ) أي الأظهر أن يجعل كل من لزوم نفي الشرعيين وتكليف ما لا يطاق فساداً مستقلاً لا أن يجعل اللازم هو الأول ويجعل الثاني دليلاً عليه كما هو الظاهر من كلام المصنف وإن كان المتبادر من كلام الشارح أنه تعليل المقدر.
For it is said: This (i.e., your inference that the servant is compelled) (is setting up a proof in opposition to the self-evident), because every rational person knows that he has choice in his actions, distinguishing between voluntary and compulsory ones, so it is not to be listened to, because it is futile sophistry and blatant stubbornness. Also, your proof denies the power of Allah Almighty because the proof runs through His actions and the premises. The tenor: it is said: If He is not able to refrain, then that; and if He is able to refrain, the act does not depend on a preponderant, etc., as previously, so your proof is invalidated by the acts of Allah. Also, your proof, just as it negates rational good and evil, also negates revealed good and evil that are derived from the establishment of obligation; and if he is compelled, no obligation is established for him, because it is an obligation of what cannot be borne—and we do not deem it permissible. And even if you deem it permissible, you do not say it occurs, and not all obligations are like that—i.e., an obligation of what cannot be borne—as your proof necessitates. The conclusion: that the servant being compelled contradicts his being obligated such that his act is described as good or evil by revealed law, while they are established in your view, so your proof is invalidated by them. So whatever is your answer is our answer. And the more apparent is to say: It negates the revealed ones as well, because they are from the servant, so no. His saying: ('And an infinite regress, and it is impossible' has passed with us that the infinite regress is only necessitated if it proceeds from the servant with his choice; but if it proceeds from him without it, then no. And know that some objections that we advanced in the First Objective of this Observation Point also apply to what he mentioned in this Objective, so there is no need to repeat them; let them be sought from there.) His saying: ('It was accidental as previously' meaning proceeding without a cause necessitating it, and it specifies its existence in relation to the time in which it occurred; for if you say: the first preponderant may preponderate its time of existence over other times just as it preponderates its existence over non-existence, since there is no conflict between the two preponderants, I say: Since what the first preponderant preponderates is the choice attached to it in both times together, based on the prior purpose, its relation to the two times is then equal, so there is no meaning to its preponderating its time of existence over other times, and it is apparent.) His saying: ('And the more apparent is to say, etc.') means the more apparent is to make each of the necessity of negating the revealed ones and the obligation of what cannot be borne as independent corruptions, not that the necessary is the first and the second is made a proof for it, as is apparent from the author's speech, even if what comes to mind from the commentator's speech is that it is an explanation of the implied.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage collects objections against the determinist argument and then provides responses and clarifications. The first objection is that the argument contradicts the self-evident fact that people feel they have free choice; this is dismissed as sophistry. The second objection is that the same reasoning would apply to God, denying His power—if He cannot refrain or acts accidentally, etc. The third objection is that the argument negates revealed good and evil because if the servant is compelled, obligation (taklīf) is impossible. The response (al-aẓhar) states that the argument indeed negates revealed good and evil as well, and one should take each consequence separately rather than making them dependent. The note on infinite regress clarifies that regress only applies if the action originates from the servant's own choice, not from an external cause. The note on 'accidental' explains that a preponderant can specify the time of existence, but if the preponderant is based on an equal relation to all times, specification is impossible. The final note on 'al-aẓhar' explains that the commentator intends to treat the negation of revealed norms and the impossibility of obligation as two independent invalidations of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
الضرورة
السفسطة
قدرة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the determinist argument is obviously false because people feel free.
Correction: The argument challenges the reliability of subjective feeling; theological reasoning takes precedence over commonsense intuition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author respond to the objection that the argument denies God's power?
A) He accepts the objection and modifies the argument
B) He argues that the objection is merely sophistry
C) He points out that the same reasoning applies to God, so it is not a refutation
D) He says God is exempt from the argument

Key Takeaway
The determinist argument is not refuted by appealing to subjective free will or by its application to God; it remains consistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you rely on your gut feeling or common sense to judge whether something is true, even when clear rational evidence contradicts it? Will you now examine one such belief you hold and test it against the teachings of this lesson?
?
If the determinist argument is consistent even when applied to God, does that unsettle your understanding of God’s power? How do you reconcile your innate sense of responsibility with the possibility that your actions are determined? Take a moment to pray for guidance on this matter.
?
The author calls reliance on self-evidence 'sophistry and stubbornness'. When you cling to a comfortable belief despite rational proof against it, are you not guilty of the same? Can you identify a belief you defend emotionally rather than intellectually, and what will you do to correct it?

The Objection from Revealed Law and the Final Responses
Response to the objection from revealed law and logical clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the determinist argument handles the objection that it negates revealed good/evil and makes obligation impossible, and how clarifications on infinite regress and accidental specification resolve logical issues.

Core Idea
The argument negates both rational and revealed norms; obligation of the impossible is avoided by treating each consequence independently.

The most serious objection to determinism is that it destroys the foundation of religious obligation: if the servant is compelled, how can he be commanded? The author responds that the argument indeed negates revealed good and evil, but this is not a flaw. Further clarifications address infinite regress (only if choice is involved) and the idea of accidental specification of time. This lesson unpacks these crucial responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection carefully
The objection states that determinism negates revealed good/evil and makes obligation impossible because it would be an obligation of what cannot be borne (تكليف ما لا يطاق).
This is a key point in the debate between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis.
2
Understand the author's response (al-aẓhar)
The author says the more apparent (الأظهر) is that the argument negates revealed norms as well, and that both consequences should be taken as independent corruptions of the opponent's position.
3
Analyze the clarification on infinite regress
The infinite regress objection is only problematic if the act originates from the servant's own choice; if it is compelled, no regress occurs.
This shows that the argument's implications are not infinite in the wrong way.
4
Examine the note on accidental specification
The note explains that if the preponderant's relation to all times is equal, specification of one time is impossible, hence the need for a specific preponderant.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That determinism necessarily makes God unjust because He commands the impossible.
Correction: The Ash'ari position holds that obligation of what cannot be borne is not impossible for God; but here the author argues that the argument does not entail that all obligations are impossible, only that revealed norms are negated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'more apparent' (الأظهر) way to handle the objection that determinism negates revealed norms and makes obligation impossible?
A) Accept that the argument is flawed and modify it
B) Treat both consequences as independent corruptions of the opponent's position
C) Deny that the argument negates revealed norms
D) Argue that obligation is not necessary

Key Takeaway
The determinist argument consistently negates revealed norms, and objections about obligation and logical regress are resolved by treating each consequence independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that God's commands are too difficult or that you are 'forced' by your circumstances to sin, does this passage challenge you to reconsider that feeling? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed that no obligation is impossible, even if your actions are under God's decree?
?
This lesson teaches that determinism does not destroy moral categories; your actions still have ethical weight. Is there a sin you have excused by thinking 'it was already written'? What would it take for you to take full responsibility for that action while still affirming God's sovereignty?
?
The author says the proper response is to treat the two implications (negation of revealed norms and obligation of the impossible) as independent flaws. Does your own heart ever treat God's laws as burdensome or irrelevant because of fatalism? How can you personally adopt the author's stance that neither flaw is acceptable?

The Ash'ari Argument for Compulsion and Its Conclusion
Conclusion of the compulsion argument and its doctrinal consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument from infinite regress leads to the conclusion that human actions are compelled and cannot be described as rationally good or evil.

Core Idea
Human actions are compelled because any presumed choice requires an infinite chain of preponderators.

Before we examine the objections to the Ash'ari position on human action, we must first be clear on what that position actually claims. This passage presents the conclusion of the argument: if the servant's act requires a preponderator to avoid being coincidental, and that preponderator itself requires another, an infinite regress results, making the act compulsory. Hence, the servant has no genuine choice and his actions cannot be judged as rationally good or evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: if abandonment requires a preponderator, and that preponderator requires another, we get an infinite regress, making the act compulsory.
This argument is a standard Ash'ari proof against human free will in the causal sense.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three possibilities for an act without a preponderator: impossible (compulsory), coincidental, or compulsory with preponderator. Each leads to compulsion.
3
Understand the composite consensus
The passage says there is composite consensus (ijmā‘ murakkab) that rational good/evil do not apply. For Ash'aris, reason has no role; for Mu'tazila, the acts are not voluntary.
Composite consensus means both parties agree on the non-application, though for different reasons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح
والترك واحتاج حينئذ إلى مرجح (آخر) إذ لو لم يحتج إليه وصدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى كان اتفاقياً كما مر . وإذا احتاج إلى مرجح آخر نقلنا الكلام إليه وتسلسل، فيكون الفعل على تقدير وجوبه مع ذلك المرجح اضطرارياً وعلى التقادير أعني امتناع الترك وكون الفعل اتفاقياً واضطرارياً فلا اختيار للعبد في أفعاله فيكون مجبوراً فيها فلا يتصف شيء منها بالحسن والقبح العقليين بالإجماع المركب، أما عندنا فلأنه لا مدخل للعقل فيهما، وأما عندهم صفات فلأنهما من الأفعال الاختيارية. فإن قيل : هذا أي استدلالكم على كون العبد مجبوراً نصب للدليل في مقابلة الضرورة إذ كل واحد من العقلاء يعلم أن له اختياراً في أفعاله يفرق بين الاختياري والاضطراري منها فلا يسمع لأنه سفسطة باطلة ومكابرة ظاهرة، وأيضاً فإنه أي دليلكم ينفي قدرة الله تعالى لاطراد الدليل في أفعاله والمقدمات والتقرير فيقال : إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإن تمكن منه لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر فقد انتقض الدليل المذكور بأفعاله تعالى وأيضاً فإنه أي هذا الدليل كما ينفي الحسن والقبح العقليين ينفي أيضاً الحسن والقبح الشرعيين المتفرعين على ثبوت التكليف، وإذا كان مجبوراً لم يثبت عليه تكليف لأنه تكليف مالا يطاق ونحن لا نجوزه وأنتم وإن جوزتموه فلا تقولون بوقوعه ولا يكون كل التكاليف كذلك أي تكليفاً بما لا يطاق كما لزم من دليلكم، والحاصل أن كون العبد مجبوراً ينافي كونه مكلفاً يوصف فعله بحسن ولا قبح شرعي مع أنهما ثابتان عندكم فانتقض دليلكم بهما، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا. والأظهر أن يقال : إنه ينفي الشرعيين أيضاً لأنهما من صفات الأفعال الاختيارية، فإن حركة المرتعش والنائم والمغمى عليه لا توصف في الشرع بحسن ولا قبح ويستلزم أيضاً كون التكاليف بأسرها تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ولا قائل به. وأيضاً فالمرجح الذي يتوقف عليه فعل العبد داع له يقتضي اختياره الموجب للفعل وذلك لا ينفي الاختيار بل يثبته وهذا السؤال هو الحل وما قبله إما نقض أو في حكمه. قلنا : أما الأول : فلان الضروري وجود القدرة والاختيار لا وقوع الفعل بقدرته واختياره واستدلالنا إنما هو على نفي الثاني دون الأول فلا يكون مصادماً للضرورة. وأما الثاني : وهو النقض بأفعال الباري فالمقدمة القائلة بأن الفعل الواقع لا لمرجح اتفاقي لا اختياري إنما هي مقدمة إلزامية بالنسبة إلى المعتزلة القائلين بأن قدرة العبد لا تؤثر في فعل إلا إذا انضم إليها مرجح يسمونه الداعي ونحن لا نقول بها فإن الترجيح بمجرد الاختيار المتعلق بأحد طرفي الفعل لا لداع عندنا جائز ولا يخرج ذلك الفعل عن كونه اختبارياً كما تقدم في مسألة الهارب من السبع والعطشان الواجد للقدحين المتساويين. وإذا لم نقل بهذه المقدمة لم يرد علينا النقض بفعل الله تعالى. وأيضاً على تقدير صحة هذه المقدمة عندنا ليس هذا الدليل بعينه جارياً في فعله تعالى لأنا نختار أنه متمكن من الترك وأن فعله يتوقف على مرجح لكن ذلك المرجح قديم فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل في المرجحات كما في فعل العبد إذا كان مرجحه صادرا عنه، إذ لا بد أن يكون ذلك الصادر عنه حادثاً محتاجاً إلى آخر فالمقدمة القائلة بأن مرجح الفعل إذا كان صادراً عن فاعله لزم التسلسل غير صادقة في حقه تعالى بل في حق العبد، وإلى ما قررناه أشار بقوله : ( فمرجح فاعليته تعالى قديم هو إرادته وقدرته المستندتان إلى ذاته إيجاباً والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص.
The Sixth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On Good and Evil. And the abandonment required then a (another) preponderator, for if it did not need it and it issued from him sometimes and did not issue from him at other times, it would be coincidental as previously mentioned. And if it needed another preponderator, we would transfer the discourse to it and [there would be] an infinite regress, so the act on the assumption of its necessity with that preponderator is compulsory, and on the assumptions, namely the impossibility of abandonment and the act being coincidental and compulsory, there is no choice for the servant in his acts, so he is compelled in them, and none of them is qualified by rational good or evil by composite consensus. As for according to us, because the intellect has no entry into them; as for according to them [the Mu'tazila], because they are characteristics of voluntary acts. If it is said: This, i.e., your inference that the servant is compelled, sets up the proof in opposition to necessity, for every rational person knows that he has choice in his acts by which he distinguishes between the voluntary and the compulsory among them, so it is not heard because it is false sophistry and manifest obstinacy. Also, that your proof negates the power of God Most High because the proof applies uniformly to His acts, the premises and the presentation, so it is said: If He is not able to abandon, then that is it; and if He is able to abandon, then the act does not depend on a preponderator, etc., as previously, so the mentioned proof is refuted by His acts Most High. Also, this proof, just as it negates rational good and evil, also negates the legal good and evil that branch off from the establishment of obligation, and if he is compelled, obligation is not established for him because it is an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and we do not allow it, and even if you allow it, you do not say it occurs, and not all obligations are like that, i.e., an obligation of what is beyond capacity, as necessitated by your proof. The conclusion is that the servant being compelled contradicts his being obligated whose act is described by legal good or evil, while both are established according to you, so your proof is invalidated by them. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. And the more apparent is to say: That it also negates the legal [good and evil] because they are attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the tremulous, the sleeper, and the unconscious is not described in law as good or evil, and it also necessitates that all obligations are an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and no one says that. Also, the preponderator on which the servant's act depends is a motive that necessitates his choice necessitating the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it, and this question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: Because the necessary is the existence of power and choice, not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our inference is only to negate the second, not the first, so it does not conflict with necessity. As for the second: The refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the act that occurs without a preponderator is coincidental not voluntary is only a binding premise with respect to the Mu'tazila who say that the servant's power does not affect an act unless a preponderator joins it which they call al-da'ii (motive), and we do not say it, for preponderance by mere choice connected to one of the two sides of the act without a motive is permissible according to us, and that act does not exit from being voluntary, as previously mentioned in the issue of the one fleeing from the lion and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. And if we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God Most High does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the correctness of this premise according to us, this very proof does not apply to His act Most High, for we choose that He is able to abandon and that His act depends on a preponderator, but that preponderator is eternal, so it does not need another preponderator, such that infinite regress in preponderators follows, as in the act of the servant when his preponderator issues from him, for that issuing must be originated and in need of another. So the premise that the preponderator of the act, if it issues from its agent, leads to infinite regress, is not true with respect to Him Most High, but rather with respect to the servant. And to what we have established he alluded by his saying: 'So the preponderator of His agency Most High is eternal, being His will and power, which are based on His essence by way of necessitation and are related to the act at a specific time.'
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the treatment of the issue of good and evil, specifically focusing on the objection to the Ash'ari argument that humans are compelled (majbur) and the author's response. The passage begins with the conclusion of an earlier argument: if the act's abandonment requires a preponderator, and that preponderator itself requires another, an infinite regress follows, making the act compulsory, hence the servant has no choice and his actions cannot be described as rationally good or evil. This is the Ash'ari position. Then an objection (fa-in qila) is raised: this argument contradicts the intuitive necessity that humans have choice, it negates God's power by the same logic, and it negates both rational and legal good and evil, leading to impossible obligations. The objector concludes that the argument fails. The author then says 'the more apparent is to say' (al-azhar an yuqal), presenting a refined way to reject the objection: the argument also negates legal good and evil because they are properties of voluntary acts, and it entails all obligations being impossible, which is false. Then a key point is made: the preponderator is a motive (da'ii) that necessitates choice, not a coercive cause, so it affirms choice rather than negating it. This is the solution. The author then begins the formal reply (qulna) addressing each objection. For the first objection (about intuitive necessity), he distinguishes: what is intuitively necessary is the existence of power and choice, not that the act actually occurs by that power and choice. His argument only negates the latter, so no conflict. For the second objection (about God's acts), he explains that the premise that an act without a preponderator is coincidental is only a binding premise against the Mu'tazila, who hold that a created power cannot effect an act without a motive. The Ash'ari does not accept this premise, so the objection does not apply. Even if the premise were accepted, God's acts are different: His preponderator is eternal and does not require another, so no regress. The author concludes by referencing the earlier point about the eternal preponderator being God's will and power related to the act at a specific time.

Key Terms
مرجح
جبر
حسن وقبح عقليان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument denies any human choice or responsibility entirely.
Correction: The Ash'ari position denies that human power directly causes the act, but they affirm acquisition (kasb) and that the act is created by God while the human has a role of 'acquisition'. The passage here focuses on the logical consequence of Mu'tazila premises, not the full Ash'ari view.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can human actions not be described as rationally good or evil?
A) Because the actions are necessary and not voluntary.
B) Because reason has no role in determining good and evil.
C) Both A and B depending on the school (Ash'ari vs Mu'tazila).
D) Because God directly creates the action.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument from infinite regress concludes that human actions are compulsory, thus not subject to rational good/evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action is ultimately compelled by a chain of divine preponderators, what does that mean for the sins you committed yesterday? Are you genuinely remorseful, or do you merely feel regret that something occurred? How should you approach repentance when your will is not truly free?
?
The passage says your acts cannot be called rationally good or evil. Yet you constantly judge your own and others' actions as right or wrong. Does this shake your confidence in your moral judgments? How can you be sure that your sense of right and wrong is not just a reflection of your compelled nature?
?
Think of a time you made a difficult moral choice that you are proud of. According to this argument, that choice was ultimately necessitated. Does that diminish your pride? Or does it shift your gratitude from yourself to Allah? What is the proper response of the heart to this teaching?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
4 min
The Objection: Intuitive Knowledge and God's Power
Presents two prongs of the objection: intuitive necessity and uniform application to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two parts of the objection against the compulsion argument: that it contradicts the intuitive knowledge of human choice and that it would also negate God's power.

Core Idea
If the compulsion argument were valid, it would deny self-evident human choice and apply equally to God.

The objection begins by attacking the foundation of the argument: it claims that the argument goes against what every rational person knows intuitively—that they have choice. Furthermore, the same reasoning would also apply to God's actions, negating His power. These are powerful objections that must be addressed carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the first two parts of the objection: the appeal to intuitive necessity and the refutation by the acts of God.
Note how the objector uses the opponent's own method against them.
2
Understand the intuitive knowledge claim
The objector argues that every rational person knows they have choice and can distinguish voluntary from involuntary actions. To deny this is sophistry.
3
Understand the refutation by God's acts
The objector says: your proof would apply to God as well, because God also acts; if the same reasoning leads to compulsion for God, it negates His power.
The objector employs a tu quoque (you too) argument.

Source Text

المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح
والترك واحتاج حينئذ إلى مرجح (آخر) إذ لو لم يحتج إليه وصدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى كان اتفاقياً كما مر . وإذا احتاج إلى مرجح آخر نقلنا الكلام إليه وتسلسل، فيكون الفعل على تقدير وجوبه مع ذلك المرجح اضطرارياً وعلى التقادير أعني امتناع الترك وكون الفعل اتفاقياً واضطرارياً فلا اختيار للعبد في أفعاله فيكون مجبوراً فيها فلا يتصف شيء منها بالحسن والقبح العقليين بالإجماع المركب، أما عندنا فلأنه لا مدخل للعقل فيهما، وأما عندهم صفات فلأنهما من الأفعال الاختيارية. فإن قيل : هذا أي استدلالكم على كون العبد مجبوراً نصب للدليل في مقابلة الضرورة إذ كل واحد من العقلاء يعلم أن له اختياراً في أفعاله يفرق بين الاختياري والاضطراري منها فلا يسمع لأنه سفسطة باطلة ومكابرة ظاهرة، وأيضاً فإنه أي دليلكم ينفي قدرة الله تعالى لاطراد الدليل في أفعاله والمقدمات والتقرير فيقال : إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإن تمكن منه لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر فقد انتقض الدليل المذكور بأفعاله تعالى وأيضاً فإنه أي هذا الدليل كما ينفي الحسن والقبح العقليين ينفي أيضاً الحسن والقبح الشرعيين المتفرعين على ثبوت التكليف، وإذا كان مجبوراً لم يثبت عليه تكليف لأنه تكليف مالا يطاق ونحن لا نجوزه وأنتم وإن جوزتموه فلا تقولون بوقوعه ولا يكون كل التكاليف كذلك أي تكليفاً بما لا يطاق كما لزم من دليلكم، والحاصل أن كون العبد مجبوراً ينافي كونه مكلفاً يوصف فعله بحسن ولا قبح شرعي مع أنهما ثابتان عندكم فانتقض دليلكم بهما، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا. والأظهر أن يقال : إنه ينفي الشرعيين أيضاً لأنهما من صفات الأفعال الاختيارية، فإن حركة المرتعش والنائم والمغمى عليه لا توصف في الشرع بحسن ولا قبح ويستلزم أيضاً كون التكاليف بأسرها تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ولا قائل به. وأيضاً فالمرجح الذي يتوقف عليه فعل العبد داع له يقتضي اختياره الموجب للفعل وذلك لا ينفي الاختيار بل يثبته وهذا السؤال هو الحل وما قبله إما نقض أو في حكمه. قلنا : أما الأول : فلان الضروري وجود القدرة والاختيار لا وقوع الفعل بقدرته واختياره واستدلالنا إنما هو على نفي الثاني دون الأول فلا يكون مصادماً للضرورة. وأما الثاني : وهو النقض بأفعال الباري فالمقدمة القائلة بأن الفعل الواقع لا لمرجح اتفاقي لا اختياري إنما هي مقدمة إلزامية بالنسبة إلى المعتزلة القائلين بأن قدرة العبد لا تؤثر في فعل إلا إذا انضم إليها مرجح يسمونه الداعي ونحن لا نقول بها فإن الترجيح بمجرد الاختيار المتعلق بأحد طرفي الفعل لا لداع عندنا جائز ولا يخرج ذلك الفعل عن كونه اختبارياً كما تقدم في مسألة الهارب من السبع والعطشان الواجد للقدحين المتساويين. وإذا لم نقل بهذه المقدمة لم يرد علينا النقض بفعل الله تعالى. وأيضاً على تقدير صحة هذه المقدمة عندنا ليس هذا الدليل بعينه جارياً في فعله تعالى لأنا وقدرته المستندتان إلى ذاته إيجاباً والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the objection based on?
A) Logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have an immediate sense of making choices every day. This passage says that denying that sense is 'false sophistry and manifest obstinacy.' When you reflect on your own belief about free will, do you ever intellectually dismiss this inner feeling? If so, are you willing to accuse yourself of obstinacy? What would it take for you to trust your own self-awareness over a logical proof?
?
The objector argues that if the compulsion proof were applied to God, it would negate His power. You claim to believe God is all-powerful. How comfortable are you with a theological argument that, if followed consistently, seems to strip God of His ability to act freely? Does your current position on human free will actually protect God's power or undermine it?
?
When you give in to sin, have you ever excused yourself by thinking 'I couldn't help it' or 'God made me do it'? This passage reminds you that every rational person knows they have choice. That excuse is not just weak — it is, as the objector says, sophistry. Will you today repent of blaming God for your own choices, and instead take full responsibility for your actions?

The Objection: Legal Good/Evil and Impossible Obligation
Continuation of the objection concerning legal evaluation and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third part of the objection: that the compulsion argument destroys legal good and evil and leads to the impossible consequence of obliging what is beyond capacity.

Core Idea
If humans are compelled, legal good/evil vanish and all obligations become impossible.

The objector presses further: the compulsion argument not only negates rational good and evil but also the legal (shar‘ī) good and evil that underlie Islamic obligations. Moreover, if the servant is compelled, then imposing any obligation would be obliging the impossible, which even the Mu'tazila reject for most cases. This is a strong reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the part of the objection that deals with legal good/evil and obligation.
2
Identify the consequences
The objector points out that legal good/evil are attributes of voluntary acts, and if acts are compulsory, they cannot be so described. Also, all obligations would be impossible.
3
Understand the 'more apparent' response
The author interjects with 'al-azhar an yuqāl', offering a sharper version of the objection: that the argument also negates legal judgments.
This is part of the objection, not the author's final answer.

للعقل فيهما، وأما عندهم صفات فلأنهما من الأفعال الاختيارية. فإن قيل : هذا أي استدلالكم على كون العبد مجبوراً نصب للدليل في مقابلة الضرورة إذ كل واحد من العقلاء يعلم أن له اختياراً في أفعاله يفرق بين الاختياري والاضطراري منها فلا يسمع لأنه سفسطة باطلة ومكابرة ظاهرة، وأيضاً فإنه أي دليلكم ينفي قدرة الله تعالى لاطراد الدليل في أفعاله والمقدمات والتقرير فيقال : إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك، وإن تمكن منه لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح إلى آخر ما مر فقد انتقض الدليل المذكور بأفعاله تعالى وأيضاً فإنه أي هذا الدليل كما ينفي الحسن والقبح العقليين ينفي أيضاً الحسن والقبح الشرعيين المتفرعين على ثبوت التكليف، وإذا كان مجبوراً لم يثبت عليه تكليف لأنه تكليف مالا يطاق ونحن لا نجوزه وأنتم وإن جوزتموه فلا تقولون بوقوعه ولا يكون كل التكاليف كذلك أي تكليفاً بما لا يطاق كما لزم من دليلكم، والحاصل أن كون العبد مجبوراً ينافي كونه مكلفاً يوصف فعله بحسن ولا قبح شرعي مع أنهما ثابتان عندكم فانتقض دليلكم بهما، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا. والأظهر أن يقال : إنه ينفي الشرعيين أيضاً لأنهما من صفات الأفعال الاختيارية، فإن حركة المرتعش والنائم والمغمى عليه لا توصف في الشرع بحسن ولا قبح ويستلزم أيضاً كون التكاليف بأسرها تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ولا قائل به. وأيضاً فالمرجح الذي يتوقف عليه فعل العبد داع له يقتضي اختياره الموجب للفعل وذلك لا ينفي الاختيار بل يثبته وهذا السؤال هو الحل وما قبله إما نقض أو في حكمه. قلنا : أما الأول : فلان الضروري وجود القدرة والاختيار لا وقوع الفعل بقدرته واختياره واستدلالنا إنما هو على نفي الثاني دون الأول فلا يكون مصادماً للضرورة. وأما الثاني : وهو النقض بأفعال الباري فالمقدمة القائلة بأن الفعل الواقع لا لمرجح اتفاقي لا اختياري إنما هي مقدمة إلزامية بالنسبة إلى المعتزلة القائلين بأن قدرة العبد لا تؤثر في فعل إلا إذا انضم إليها مرجح يسمونه الداعي ونحن لا نقول بها فإن الترجيح بمجرد الاختيار المتعلق بأحد طرفي الفعل لا لداع عندنا جائز ولا يخرج ذلك الفعل عن كونه اختبارياً كما تقدم في مسألة الهارب من السبع والعطشان الواجد للقدحين المتساويين. وإذا لم نقل بهذه المقدمة لم يرد علينا النقض بفعل الله تعالى. وأيضاً على تقدير صحة هذه المقدمة عندنا ليس هذا الدليل بعينه جارياً في فعله تعالى لأنا نختار أنه متمكن من الترك وأن فعله يتوقف على مرجح لكن ذلك المرجح قديم فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل في المرجحات كما في فعل العبد إذا كان مرجحه صادرا عنه، إذ لا بد أن يكون ذلك الصادر عنه حادثاً محتاجاً إلى آخر فالمقدمة القائلة بأن مرجح الفعل إذا كان صادراً عن فاعله لزم التسلسل غير صادقة في حقه تعالى بل في حق العبد، وإلى ما قررناه أشار بقوله : ( فمرجح فاعليته تعالى قديم هو إرادته وقدرته المستندتان إلى ذاته إيجاباً والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص.
The Sixth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On Good and Evil. And the abandonment required then a (another) preponderator, for if it did not need it and it issued from him sometimes and did not issue from him at other times, it would be coincidental as previously mentioned. And if it needed another preponderator, we would transfer the discourse to it and [there would be] an infinite regress, so the act on the assumption of its necessity with that preponderator is compulsory, and on the assumptions, namely the impossibility of abandonment and the act being coincidental and compulsory, there is no choice for the servant in his acts, so he is compelled in them, and none of them is qualified by rational good or evil by composite consensus. As for according to us, because the intellect has no entry into them; as for according to them [the Mu'tazila], because they are characteristics of voluntary acts. If it is said: This, i.e., your inference that the servant is compelled, sets up the proof in opposition to necessity, for every rational person knows that he has choice in his acts by which he distinguishes between the voluntary and the compulsory among them, so it is not heard because it is false sophistry and manifest obstinacy. Also, that your proof negates the power of God Most High because the proof applies uniformly to His acts, the premises and the presentation, so it is said: If He is not able to abandon, then that is it; and if He is able to abandon, then the act does not depend on a preponderator, etc., as previously, so the mentioned proof is refuted by His acts Most High. Also, this proof, just as it negates rational good and evil, also negates the legal good and evil that branch off from the establishment of obligation, and if he is compelled, obligation is not established for him because it is an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and we do not allow it, and even if you allow it, you do not say it occurs, and not all obligations are like that, i.e., an obligation of what is beyond capacity, as necessitated by your proof. The conclusion is that the servant being compelled contradicts his being obligated whose act is described by legal good or evil, while both are established according to you, so your proof is invalidated by them. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. And the more apparent is to say: That it also negates the legal [good and evil] because they are attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the tremulous, the sleeper, and the unconscious is not described in law as good or evil, and it also necessitates that all obligations are an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and no one says that. Also, the preponderator on which the servant's act depends is a motive that necessitates his choice necessitating the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it, and this question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: Because the necessary is the existence of power and choice, not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our inference is only to negate the second, not the first, so it does not conflict with necessity. As for the second: The refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the act that occurs without a preponderator is coincidental not voluntary is only a binding premise with respect to the Mu'tazila who say that the servant's power does not affect an act unless a preponderator joins it which they call al-da'ii (motive), and we do not say it, for preponderance by mere choice connected to one of the two sides of the act without a motive is permissible according to us, and that act does not exit from being voluntary, as previously mentioned in the issue of the one fleeing from the lion and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. And if we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God Most High does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the correctness of this premise according to us, this very proof does not apply to His act Most High, for we choose that He is able to abandon and that His act depends on a preponderator, but that preponderator is eternal, so it does not need another preponderator, such that infinite regress in preponderators follows, as in the act of the servant when his preponderator issues from him, for that issuing must be originated and in need of another. So the premise that the preponderator of the act, if it issues from its agent, leads to infinite regress, is not true with respect to Him Most High, but rather with respect to the servant. And to what we have established he alluded by his saying: 'So the preponderator of His agency Most High is eternal, being His will and power, which are based on His essence by way of necessitation and are related to the act at a specific time.'
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the treatment of the issue of good and evil, specifically focusing on the objection to the Ash'ari argument that humans are compelled (majbur) and the author's response. The passage begins with the conclusion of an earlier argument: if the act's abandonment requires a preponderator, and that preponderator itself requires another, an infinite regress follows, making the act compulsory, hence the servant has no choice and his actions cannot be described as rationally good or evil. This is the Ash'ari position. Then an objection (fa-in qila) is raised: this argument contradicts the intuitive necessity that humans have choice, it negates God's power by the same logic, and it negates both rational and legal good and evil, leading to impossible obligations. The objector concludes that the argument fails. The author then says 'the more apparent is to say' (al-azhar an yuqal), presenting a refined way to reject the objection: the argument also negates legal good and evil because they are properties of voluntary acts, and it entails all obligations being impossible, which is false. Then a key point is made: the preponderator is a motive (da'ii) that necessitates choice, not a coercive cause, so it affirms choice rather than negating it. This is the solution. The author then begins the formal reply (qulna) addressing each objection. For the first objection (about intuitive necessity), he distinguishes: what is intuitively necessary is the existence of power and choice, not that the act actually occurs by that power and choice. His argument only negates the latter, so no conflict. For the second objection (about God's acts), he explains that the premise that an act without a preponderator is coincidental is only a binding premise against the Mu'tazila, who hold that a created power cannot effect an act without a motive. The Ash'ari does not accept this premise, so the objection does not apply. Even if the premise were accepted, God's acts are different: His preponderator is eternal and does not require another, so no regress. The author concludes by referencing the earlier point about the eternal preponderator being God's will and power related to the act at a specific time.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the compulsion argument entail regarding legal (shar‘ī) good and evil?
A) It negates them because they are based on voluntary acts.
B) It affirms them because the argument only concerns rational good/evil.
C) It has no effect on them.
D) It establishes them more firmly.

Key Takeaway
The compulsion argument is further challenged for destroying the basis of legal accountability and implying impossible obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a sin you repeatedly commit. Do you ever tell yourself that you can't help it? How does this lesson's insistence that you are accountable for your actions—because obligation implies ability—confront that excuse? What will you do this week to genuinely act against that sin, knowing you have the capacity?
?
If you truly believed that all your actions were compelled by Allah's decree, how would that change your motivation to pray, fast, and strive in obedience? Now, how does the fact that your acts are described as good or evil in the law move you to take ownership of your spiritual state?
?
The objector says that if compulsion were true, then every obligation would be impossible—meaning you could never be truly responsible. Do you live as if some commands are too hard for you? Which command of Allah do you most often treat as beyond your capacity, and what step will you take today to prove yourself wrong?

Rational Judgments
advanced
6 min
The Author's Refutation: Distinguishing Power and Act, and the Special Case of God
The author's systematic response to the objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objections by distinguishing between the existence of choice and its effective causation, and by showing that the argument does not apply to God's acts due to the eternal nature of His preponderator.

Core Idea
Choice exists but does not cause the act; God's preponderator is eternal, breaking the regress.

Now the author gives the formal reply (qulnā). He addresses each objection systematically. To the first, he distinguishes between having power/choice and actually causing the act. To the second, he shows that the regress argument only applies to created agents because their preponderators are originated, whereas God's preponderator is eternal, thus no regress. This preserves both human choice (as a condition) and God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the author's reply beginning with 'qulnā'. It contains two parts: the answer to the intuitive objection and the answer to the refutation by God's acts.
2
Understand the first reply: necessity of existence, not efficacy
The author says what is necessary is that humans have power and choice, not that the act occurs by that power. The argument only negates the latter.
This is a subtle but crucial distinction.
3
Understand the second reply: the premise about preponderator is only binding on Mu'tazila
The author explains that the premise 'act without preponderator is coincidental' is a Mu'tazili assumption; Ash'aris can accept that choice alone can preponderate without a separate motive.
Even if we accept the premise, God's preponderator is eternal and does not require an infinite chain.
4
See how God's case is different
God's will and power are eternal, based on His essence, so they do not need another preponderator. The regress only applies when the preponderator is created (ḥādith).

المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح
والترك واحتاج حينئذ إلى مرجح (آخر) إذ لو لم يحتج إليه وصدر عنه تارة ولم يصدر عنه أخرى كان اتفاقياً كما مر . وإذا احتاج إلى مرجح آخر نقلنا الكلام إليه وتسلسل، فيكون الفعل على تقدير وجوبه مع ذلك المرجح اضطرارياً وعلى التقادير أعني امتناع الترك وكون الفعل اتفاقياً واضطرارياً فلا اختيار للعبد في أفعاله فيكون مجبوراً فيها فلا يتصف شيء منها بالحسن والقبح العقليين بالإجماع المركب، أما عندنا فلأنه لا مدخل للعقل فيهما، وأما عندهم صفات فلأنهما من الأفعال الاختيارية. فإن قيل : هذا أي استدلالكم على كون العبد مجبوراً نصب للدليل في مقابلة الضرورة إذ كل واحد من العقلاء يعلم أن له اختياراً في أفعاله يفرق بين كان مجبوراً لم يثبت عليه تكليف لأنه تكليف مالا يطاق ونحن لا نجوزه وأنتم وإن جوزتموه فلا تقولون بوقوعه ولا يكون كل التكاليف كذلك أي تكليفاً بما لا يطاق كما لزم من دليلكم، والحاصل أن كون العبد مجبوراً المرتعش والنائم والمغمى عليه لا توصف في الشرع بحسن ولا قبح ويستلزم أيضاً كون التكاليف بأسرها تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ولا قائل به. وأيضاً فالمرجح الذي يتوقف عليه فعل العبد داع له يقتضي اختياره الموجب للفعل وذلك لا ينفي الاختيار بل يثبته وهذا السؤال هو الحل وما قبله إما نقض أو في حكمه. قلنا : أما الأول : فلان الضروري وجود القدرة والاختيار لا وقوع الفعل بقدرته واختياره واستدلالنا إنما هو على نفي الثاني العبد لا تؤثر في فعل إلا إذا انضم إليها مرجح يسمونه الداعي ونحن لا نقول بها فإن الترجيح بمجرد الاختيار المتعلق بأحد طرفي الفعل لا لداع عندنا جائز ولا يخرج ذلك الفعل عن كونه اختبارياً كما تقدم في مسألة الهارب من السبع والعطشان الواجد للقدحين المتساويين. وإذا لم نقل بهذه المقدمة لم يرد علينا النقض بفعل الله تعالى. وأيضاً على تقدير صحة هذه المقدمة عندنا ليس هذا الدليل بعينه جارياً في فعله تعالى لأنا نختار أنه متمكن من الترك وأن فعله يتوقف على مرجح لكن ذلك المرجح قديم فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر حتى يلزم التسلسل في المرجحات كما في فعل العبد إذا كان مرجحه صادرا عنه، إذ لا بد أن يكون ذلك الصادر عنه حادثاً محتاجاً إلى آخر فالمقدمة القائلة بأن مرجح الفعل إذا كان صادراً عن فاعله لزم التسلسل غير صادقة في حقه تعالى بل في حق العبد، وإلى ما قررناه أشار بقوله : ( فمرجح فاعليته تعالى قديم هو إرادته وقدرته المستندتان إلى ذاته إيجاباً والمتعلقتان بالفعل في وقت مخصوص.
The Sixth Observation - The Fifth Objective: On Good and Evil. And the abandonment required then a (another) preponderator, for if it did not need it and it issued from him sometimes and did not issue from him at other times, it would be coincidental as previously mentioned. And if it needed another preponderator, we would transfer the discourse to it and [there would be] an infinite regress, so the act on the assumption of its necessity with that preponderator is compulsory, and on the assumptions, namely the impossibility of abandonment and the act being coincidental and compulsory, there is no choice for the servant in his acts, so he is compelled in them, and none of them is qualified by rational good or evil by composite consensus. As for according to us, because the intellect has no entry into them; as for according to them [the Mu'tazila], because they are characteristics of voluntary acts. If it is said: This, i.e., your inference that the servant is compelled, sets up the proof in opposition to necessity, for every rational person knows that he has choice in his acts by which he distinguishes between the voluntary and the compulsory among them, so it is not heard because it is false sophistry and manifest obstinacy. Also, this proof, just as it negates rational good and evil, also negates the legal good and evil that branch off from the establishment of obligation, and if he is compelled, obligation is not established for him because it is an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and we do not allow it, and even if you allow it, you do not say it occurs, and not all obligations are like that, i.e., an obligation of what is beyond capacity, as necessitated by your proof. The conclusion is that the servant being compelled contradicts his being obligated whose act is described by legal good or evil, while both are established according to you, so your proof is invalidated by them. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. And the more apparent is to say: That it also negates the legal [good and evil] because they are attributes of voluntary acts, for the movement of the tremulous, the sleeper, and the unconscious is not described in law as good or evil, and it also necessitates that all obligations are an obligation of what is beyond capacity, and no one says that. Also, the preponderator on which the servant's act depends is a motive that necessitates his choice necessitating the act, and that does not negate choice but rather affirms it, and this question is the solution, and what precedes it is either a refutation or in its ruling. We said: As for the first: Because the necessary is the existence of power and choice, not the occurrence of the act by his power and choice, and our inference is only to negate the second, not the first, so it does not conflict with necessity. As for the second: The refutation by the acts of the Creator, the premise that the act that occurs without a preponderator is coincidental not voluntary is only a binding premise with respect to the Mu'tazila who say that the servant's power does not affect an act unless a preponderator joins it which they call al-da'ii (motive), and we do not say it, for preponderance by mere choice connected to one of the two sides of the act without a motive is permissible according to us, and that act does not exit from being voluntary, as previously mentioned in the issue of the one fleeing from the lion and the thirsty person who finds two equal cups. And if we do not say this premise, the refutation by the act of God Most High does not apply to us. Also, on the assumption of the correctness of this premise according to us, this very proof does not apply to His act Most High, for we choose that He is able to abandon and that His act depends on a preponderator, but that preponderator is eternal, so it does not need another preponderator, such that infinite regress in preponderators follows, as in the act of the servant when his preponderator issues from him, for that issuing must be originated and in need of another. So the premise that the preponderator of the act, if it issues from its agent, leads to infinite regress, is not true with respect to Him Most High, but rather with respect to the servant. And to what we have established he alluded by his saying: 'So the preponderator of His agency Most High is eternal, being His will and power, which are based on His essence by way of necessitation and are related to the act at a specific time.'

Commentary
This passage presents the treatment of the issue of good and evil, specifically focusing on the objection to the Ash'ari argument that humans are compelled (majbur) and the author's response. The passage begins with the conclusion of an earlier argument: if the act's abandonment requires a preponderator, and that preponderator itself requires another, an infinite regress follows, making the act compulsory, hence the servant has no choice and his actions cannot be described as rationally good or evil. This is the Ash'ari position. Then an objection (fa-in qila) is raised: this argument contradicts the intuitive necessity that humans have choice, it negates God's power by the same logic, and it negates both rational and legal good and evil, leading to impossible obligations. The objector concludes that the argument fails. The author then says 'the more apparent is to say' (al-azhar an yuqal), presenting a refined way to reject the objection: the argument also negates legal good and evil because they are properties of voluntary acts, and it entails all obligations being impossible, which is false. Then a key point is made: the preponderator is a motive (da'ii) that necessitates choice, not a coercive cause, so it affirms choice rather than negating it. This is the solution. The author then begins the formal reply (qulna) addressing each objection. For the first objection (about intuitive necessity), he distinguishes: what is intuitively necessary is the existence of power and choice, not that the act actually occurs by that power and choice. His argument only negates the latter, so no conflict. For the second objection (about God's acts), he explains that the premise that an act without a preponderator is coincidental is only a binding premise against the Mu'tazila, who hold that a created power cannot effect an act without a motive. The Ash'ari does not accept this premise, so the objection does not apply. Even if the premise were accepted, God's acts are different: His preponderator is eternal and does not require another, so no regress. The author concludes by referencing the earlier point about the eternal preponderator being God's will and power related to the act at a specific time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response denies any human role in action.
Correction: The Ash'ari affirms that humans have power and choice, but these are not causally effective; they are conditions for acquisition. The response here emphasizes that choice exists, just not as the cause of the act.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the compulsion argument would also apply to God?
A) He says the premise is not binding on Ash'aris, and even if it were, God's preponderator is eternal.
B) He says God is above such arguments and cannot be analyzed.
C) He accepts the objection and modifies the argument.
D) He says the argument only applies to created beings, not to God.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objections by distinguishing between the necessary existence of choice and its causal efficacy, and by showing God's eternal preponderator breaks the regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'I had no choice'? How does this lesson's distinction between having choice and the act occurring by your power confront that excuse? Will you stop using that excuse starting today?
?
The author says choice alone can serve as a preponderator without a motive. Think of a time you made a decision that had no apparent reason – a truly free choice. Did you recognize that moment as a reflection of your own agency, or did you dismiss it as random? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for such choices?
?
God's preponderator is eternal, not needing another cause. Do you live as if your own reasons and motives are independent and self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge they are contingent on God? What one decision today can you make to consciously rely on God's eternal will rather than your own fleeting motives?
