The Conditional Evil of a False Statement
The author presents a weak argument about 'Zayd is in the house' and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a weak argument about the evil of a false statement is refuted and how the response preserves conditional evil by allowing non-existential conditions.

Core Idea
Evil of a false statement can be conditional on a non-existential condition.

This passage presents a second weak argument from those who deny intrinsic evil. The argument attempts to show that the evil of a false statement must be either intrinsic or composite, but both options are rejected. The response cleverly introduces the concept of a non-existential condition, preserving the possibility of conditional evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the translation to understand the argument.
Notice the structure: argument, two divisions, refutation, then response.
2
Identify the two divisions
The argument divides the evil of saying 'Zayd is in the house' when he is not into two possibilities: either due to its essence alone or due to its essence combined with Zayd's absence.
A third division is dismissed by the arguer.
3
Understand the refutation
The first division is refuted because it would make the statement evil even if Zayd were present. The second is refuted because it would make non-existence part of the cause of existence, which is impossible.
These refutations aim to show that evil cannot be conditional.
4
Examine the response
The response ('qulnā') rejects the refutation by stating that the evil may be conditioned upon Zayd's absence, and a condition can be non-existential.
This shows that the arguer's assumption that a condition must be existential is false.
5
Reflect on the key insight
Consider how this argument relates to the broader discussion of moral evil: evil can be relative to circumstances (conditions) that may be absences.
This is a key move in defending the possibility of conditional evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من الضعيفة : من قال : زيد في الدار ولم يكن زيد فيها فقبح هذا القول إما لذاته وحده أو مع عدم كون زيد في الدار إذ لا قائل بقسم ثالث والقسمان باطلان . فالأول لاستلزامه قبحه وإن كان زيد في الدار والثاني لأنه يستلزم كون العدم جزء علة الوجود . قلنا : قد يكون قبحه مشروطاً بعدم كون زيد في الدار والشرط لا يمنع أن يكون عدمياً .
The second [of the weak arguments]: He who said 'Zayd is in the house' when Zayd was not in it. The evil of this statement is either [due to] its essence alone, or [due to] its essence along with the absence of Zayd from the house, since there is no proponent of a third division. Both divisions are false. The first [division] would entail that it is evil even if Zayd were in the house, and the second would entail that non-existence is part of the cause of existence. We say: its evil may be conditioned upon Zayd's absence from the house, and a condition is not prevented from being non-existential.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a second weak argument from those who deny intrinsic evil. The argument considers the false statement 'Zayd is in the house' when Zayd is absent. The evil of this statement must be either due to its essence alone or due to its essence combined with the absence of Zayd; a third option is dismissed. Both options are then refuted: the first would imply the statement is evil even if Zayd were present, which is absurd; the second would make non-existence a part of the cause of existence, which is impossible. The response ('qulnā') rejects this refutation: the evil may be conditioned upon Zayd's absence, and a condition may be non-existential, so the statement is evil only when the condition (absence) is met. This preserves the possibility of conditional or relative evil.

Key Terms
ضعيفة
قبيح لذاته
شرط عدمي
علة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students think that a condition must always be an existent thing, but here the response shows that a condition can be an absence (non-existential).
Correction: The passage teaches that a condition can be a non-existential state, such as the absence of Zayd from the house.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the response to the second weak argument?
A) The evil of the statement is intrinsic and not conditional.
B) The evil may be conditional upon Zayd's absence, and a condition can be non-existential.
C) The statement is evil only due to its essence alone.
D) Non-existence cannot be a condition for evil.

Key Takeaway
The evil of a false statement can be conditional on a non-existential condition, which refutes the argument that it must be intrinsic or composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that a false statement is not evil in itself, but only under a condition. Does this make you more accountable for judging statements based on their context? Take a moment to recall a recent statement you made that was not fully truthful. On what condition was its evil contingent?

The Locus of Evil in False Speech: The Third Argument
The author examines whether evil inheres in each letter or the whole, with al-Amidi's view and a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument about whether the evil of lying attaches to each letter or the whole, and how the response distinguishes between intrinsic and relational attributes.

Core Idea
Evil in a false speech is an intrinsic attribute, not requiring inherence in parts.

This passage presents a nuanced discussion about where the evil of a false statement resides. Does it inhere in each letter, or in the whole? Both possibilities seem problematic. Al-Amidi examines these possibilities, and the response offers a subtle resolution by distinguishing between intrinsic and relational attributes. Understanding this argument clarifies important principles in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three distinct parts: initial argument, al-Amidi's objection, and the qulna response.
2
Identify the three sub-arguments
The passage has three clear sections: (1) 'The third [argument]: Its evil...' presenting the dilemma, (2) 'Al-Amidi said...' refining the dilemma, (3) 'We say: Evil is among its intrinsic attributes...' resolving it. Find each.
3
Analyze the response carefully
The response says evil is a nafsi (intrinsic) attribute, not ma'nawi (relational). Why does this solve the problem? Reflect: if evil is intrinsic, it does not depend on a separate cause ('lying') nor on inherence in individual parts.
Consider how this relates to the earlier dilemma about the whole having no existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قبحه ( أي قبح الكلام الكاذب ) لكونه كذباً إن قام بكل حرف فكل حرف كذب إذ المفروض أنه متصف بالقبح المعلل بالكذب فهو خبر وبطلانه ظاهر وإن قام بالمجموع فلا وجود له لترتبها وتقضي المتقدم منها عند حصول المتأخر وإذا لم يكن للمجموع وجود فكيف يتصور اتصافه بالقبح الذي هو صفة ثبوتية . وأما الآمدي فإنه قال : لو كان الخبر الكاذب قبيحاً عقلاً فالمقتضى لقبحه إما أن يكون صفة لمجموع حروفه أو لآحادها، والأول باطل لأن ما لا وجود له لا يتصف بصفة مقتضية لأمر ثبوتي المقتضى له لا بد أن يكون ثبوتياً فلا يكون صفة للعدم ، والثاني باطل أيضاً لأن مقتضى القبح في الخبر الكاذب إنما هو الكذب ولا يمكن قيامه بكل حرف وإلا كان كل حرف خبرا وهو محال . قلنا: هو أي القبح من صفاته النفسية لا المعنوية فلا يستدعي صفة يكون هو معللاً بها كما هو مذهب بعضهم .
The third [argument]: Its evil (i.e., the evil of the false speech) is because it is a lie. If [the evil] inheres in each letter, then each letter is a lie, since it is assumed to be characterized by evil whose cause is lying, so it is a report—and its falseness is apparent. If it inheres in the whole, then it has no existence because of the succession [of letters] and the prior [letter] ceases upon the occurrence of the later. And if the whole has no existence, how can it be imagined to be characterized by evil, which is a positive attribute? Al-Amidi said: If a false report were rationally evil, then the cause of its evil must be either an attribute of the whole of its letters or of its individual letters. The first is false because what has no existence cannot be characterized by an attribute that necessitates something positive—the cause must be positive, so it cannot be an attribute of non-existence. The second is also false because the cause of evil in a false report is lying, and lying cannot inhere in each letter, otherwise each letter would be a report, which is absurd. We say: Evil is among its intrinsic attributes (ṣifāt nafsīyya), not relational attributes (maʿnawiyya), so it does not require an attribute by which it is caused, as is the view of some.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument about the locus of evil in a false statement. It asks whether the evil inheres in each letter or in the whole speech. If in each letter, then each letter would itself be a lie and a report, which is absurd. If in the whole, the whole has no independent existence because letters are sequential and the earlier letter vanishes when the later appears—thus the whole is non-existent and cannot bear a positive attribute like evil. Al-Amidī refines this: if the false report is rationally evil, the cause of its evil is either an attribute of the whole (which is non-existent) or of the individual letters (which would make each letter a report). Both are impossible. The response (qulnā) is that evil is an intrinsic attribute (nafsī) of the speech, not a relational one (maʿnawī), so it does not require a separate cause like 'lying' to inhere in the parts. This response allows evil to be a property of the speech as a whole without needing to inhere in individual letters or in a temporally existing whole.

قبح
كذب
صفة ثبوتية
صفة نفسية
صفة معنوية
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if evil is a real attribute, it must inhere in each part individually.
Correction: The response explains that evil is an intrinsic attribute of the whole speech, not requiring inherence in each letter.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi's argument, why is it impossible for the evil of a false report to inhere in the whole of its letters?
A) Because the whole has no existence due to the succession of letters.
B) Because each letter would then be a report.
C) Because evil is a relational attribute.
D) Because the whole is a single entity that cannot be characterized.

Key Takeaway
Evil in a false speech is an intrinsic attribute, not requiring a cause like 'lying' to inhere in each letter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Argument: Ugliness of Lying and the Nafsi Response
Presents the third argument about the ugliness of lying and refutes objections by asserting moral qualities are inherent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument about the ugliness of lying and why the response that qubḥ is a nafsī attribute resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Moral ugliness is inherent to the act, not dependent on external attributes.

This passage presents the third argument concerning the ugliness of false speech. The argument considers whether the ugliness resides in each letter or in the total sequence, leading to an objection by al-Āmidī. The response introduces a crucial distinction: ugliness is a nafsī (inherent) attribute, not a ma‘nawī (intentional) one. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of the argument and the significance of the nafsī response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two possibilities for where ugliness resides: every letter or the total.
Pay attention to al-Āmidī's objection and the response.
2
Understand the objection
Al-Āmidī argues that ugliness cannot reside in the total because the total has no existence due to temporal sequence. Why does he think a positive attribute cannot qualify a non-existent?
This ties to the concept of positive (thubūtī) attributes.
3
Learn the nafsī response
The response asserts that ugliness is a nafsī attribute—inherent to the act—so it does not require an external explanatory attribute. This avoids the need for ugliness to be a real property of letters.
This is the key doctrine: moral qualities are self-evident, not derived.
4
Reflect on implications
Why does calling qubḥ 'nafsī' resolve al-Āmidī's dilemma? Consider how this aligns with the Ash‘arī view that good and evil are known by revelation, not reason.
This connects to broader kalām debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : فلا وجود له لترتبها إلخ الظاهر من كلام المصنف أن ضمير له راجع إلى القبح والمعنى وإن قام بالمجموع القبح فلا تحقق للقبيح في المحل لعدم وجود موصوفه فيلزم الخلف، ولا يرد أنه لا يلزم من عدمية القبح عدم اتصاف المجموع به لجواز الاتصاف بالصفات العدمية، لأن الكلام مبني على أن القبح صفة ثبوتية فإذا لم يثبت لم يتصف به المحل كما لا يتصف بالسواد المعدوم وقد مر نظيره .
قوله : ( وأما الآمدي فإنه قال إلخ قيل : إنما عدل عن هذا التقرير لأن مآله إلى إنكار كون الكذب صفة لمجموع الكلام وأنه باطل إجماعاً وبداهة ولقائل أن يقول : مآله إلى إنكار كوان كذب المجموع سبباً لقبحه بناء على أن القبح صفة ثبوتية لا إنكار قيام الكذب بالمجموع كما لا يخفى .
قوله : (قلنا : هو من صفاته النفسية هذا الجواب عمن يقول : قبح الأشياء لذاتها، ومن يقول : هو لصفاتها فجوابه ما سيأتي . قوله : ( فلا يستدعي (صفة حتى يقال : إنها كونه كاذباً فيلزم على الشق الأول كون كل حرف خبراً فظهر أن الجواب يتجه على تقرير المصنف أيضاً، لا كما زعم الشارح أنه إنما يتجه على تقرير الآمدي .
بأن حسن الأفعال وقبحها لذواتها لا لصفات حقيقة قائمة بها، وهذا الجواب إنما يتجه على تقرير الآمدي، وأما على تقرير الكتاب فينبغي أن يقال : ليس يلزم من كون القبح ذاتياً أي مستنداً إلى ذات الشيء أن يكون موجوداً خارجياً حتى يمتنع وقوعه صفة لأمر عدمي لجواز أن يقتضي ذات الشيء اتصافه بصفة اعتبارية ويستحيل انفكاكها عنه ، ( أو يقوم ) القبيح ( بكل حرف بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فقبحه لكونه جزء خبر كاذب أو ) يقوم القبيح ( بالمجموع لكونه كاذباً وما هو جوابكم فيه فهو جوابنا ) في قيام القبح به
The third: its ugliness (i.e., the ugliness of false speech) because it is a lie, if it resides in every letter, then every letter is a lie, since it is assumed that it is qualified by ugliness which is explained by lying, so it is a report because lying is a quality of reports, and its falsity is apparent. And if it resides in the total, then there is no existence for it due to the sequence of the letters, and the preceding ones cease when the later occur. And if there is no existence for the total, how can it be imagined to be qualified by ugliness, which is a positive attribute? So the author doubted concerning the ugliness itself: does it reside in every letter or in the total? Al-Amidi, however, said: If the false report were intellectually ugly, then the determinant of its ugliness is either a quality of the totality of its letters or of its individuals. The first is false because that which has no existence cannot be qualified by a determinant that requires a positive matter—the determinant must be positive, so it cannot be a quality of non-existence. The second is also false because the determinant of ugliness in the false report is nothing but lying, and it is impossible for it to reside in every letter, otherwise every letter would be a report, which is absurd. We said: That is, ugliness is among its nafsī attributes, not intentional attributes, so it does not require an attribute by which it is explained, as is the doctrine of some who say that the goodness and ugliness of actions are by their essences, not by real attributes subsisting in them. This answer is only directed at al-Amidi's formulation. As for the formulation of the book, it should be said: It does not follow from ugliness being essential, i.e., attributed to the essence of the thing, that it must be external existent such that it is prevented from being a quality of a non-existent, because it is possible that the essence of the thing requires it to be qualified by an attributive quality and its inseparability from it is impossible. Or the ugly thing may reside in every letter conditioned by the other being joined to it, so its ugliness is because it is part of a false report; or the ugly thing may reside in the total because it is a lie, and whatever your answer is to that, it is our answer regarding the residence of ugliness in it.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument concerning the ugliness (qubḥ) of false speech (kalām kādhib). The argument considers whether the ugliness resides in each individual letter or in the total sequence. If it resides in each letter, then every letter would be a lie, which is absurd. If it resides in the total, then the total has no existence because the letters occur sequentially, and the preceding letters cease to exist when the later ones occur—so how can a non-existent total be qualified with ugliness, which is a positive (thubūtī) attribute? The author al-Ījī (or the commentator) then notes that al-Āmidī raised a similar objection: the determinant of ugliness cannot be a quality of a non-existent totality, nor can it reside in each letter. The response given is that ugliness is a nafsī (inherent) attribute, not an intentional (ma‘nawī) attribute, so it does not require an external explanatory attribute. This aligns with the doctrine that goodness (ḥusn) and ugliness are by the essences of things, not by real attributes. The commentator (shāriḥ) adds further clarifications: the pronoun in 'no existence for it' refers to ugliness; the discussion is based on ugliness being a positive attribute, so if a subject is non-existent, it cannot be qualified. The response to al-Āmidī is clarified, and the commentator emphasizes that the answer works for both the author's and al-Āmidī's formulations. The passage ends by reiterating that ugliness can be considered as residing in each letter conditioned by the others, or in the total, and the same answer applies.

Key Terms
قبح (qubḥ)
كذب (kadhib)
خبر (khabar)
صفات نفسية (nafsī attributes)
صفات معنوية (ma‘nawī attributes)
ثبوتي (thubūtī)
عدم (‘adam)
آمدي (al-Āmidī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That moral qualities like ugliness must be real (wujūdī) attributes that inhere in their subjects as external properties.
Correction: In this passage, ugliness is a nafsī attribute—inherent and not requiring a separate explanatory property. It can qualify an act without being a real attribute residing in letters or a non-existent total.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, why does ugliness (qubḥ) not require an external explanatory attribute?
A) Because ugliness is a positive (thubūtī) attribute.
B) Because ugliness is a nafsī (inherent) attribute, not a ma‘nawī (intentional) attribute.
C) Because ugliness resides only in the total, not in individuals.
D) Because ugliness is a quality of non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Ugliness is an inherent (nafsī) attribute, so the ugliness of lying does not require a real quality like being a lie to be predicated; it is directly known from the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes rationalize lying by thinking it's not really 'ugly' because it's harmless or for a good cause? How does the lesson's assertion that ugliness is inherent in the act itself, regardless of circumstances, force you to reconsider your justifications? What lie will you stop telling today?
?
If ugliness is nafsī (inherent), then you cannot escape moral responsibility by claiming that your lie wasn't 'really' ugly. Think of one lie you have told recently. Does knowing that its ugliness is inherent make you feel a deeper need for repentance? What specific step will you take to make amends?
?
The lesson distinguishes between nafsī and ma‘nawī attributes. How does this distinction affect your understanding of your own sins? Are you treating some sins as if they are only bad because of consequences, rather than inherently ugly? What change in your daily behavior does this demand starting tomorrow?

Ugliness: Additional Attribute or Not? The Opposite Argument
Introduction of the fourth argument and its initial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that ugliness is an additional attribute and why the argument from being the opposite of non-ugliness does not prove its existence.

Core Idea
The opposite of a non-existent does not have to exist.

In this passage, the author presents the fourth argument of the kalam tradition regarding ugliness (qubḥ). The proponent claims that ugliness cannot be just the act itself; it must be an additional positive attribute because it is the opposite of non-ugliness. But the response quickly dismantles this, showing that opposites do not require existence. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and translation to grasp the initial claim and response.
Notice how the claim is stated as a premise: ugliness is not essence, not part, but additional.
2
Identify the key argument
The proponent argues that ugliness must exist because it is the opposite of non-ugliness, which subsists in the non-existent. Identify this logical move.
Think about what it means for something to be a 'opposite' in kalam.
3
Analyze the refutation
The response points out that the opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent, and the impossibility of lifting two opposites applies only to truth, not existence. Understand this distinction.
This is a key principle in dealing with negative attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كونه أي كون الفعل ( قبيحاً ليس نفس ذاته ولا جزءاً منها ( لتعلقها دونه بل زائد ) عليها ( وأنه موجود لأنه نقيض اللاقبيح القائم بالمعدوم فيلزم ) حينئذ ( قيام المعنى ( الذي هو القبح ( بالمعنى ) الذي هو الفعل، (قلنا : قد سبق الكلام على مقدماته) فإن نقيض العدمي لا يجب أن يكون موجوداً وارتفاع النقيضين إنما يستحيل في الصدق دون الوجود وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض إذ لم يقم عليه دليل كما عرفت ( مع انتقاضه بالإمكان والحدوث ) فإن هذا الدليل الذي أوردتموه على كون القبح أمراً موجوداً جارٍ فيهما مع كونهما اعتباريين .
قوله : (بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فإن قلت: عندما وجد الأول لانضمام، وعندما وجد الآخر الأول لقبح قلت: المراد بشرط كونه بحيث ينضم إليه الآخر، فإن قلت: الكلام في الكل فكيف يقوم بكل حرف بالشرط المذكور . قلت: قبح الكل جملة قبحات الأجزاء فلما وجد الموصوف على التقضي وجد الصفة كذلك. قوله : ( وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع إلخ) وأيضاً ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من قيامه بالمعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي وبعضها عدمي كاللاممكن الصادق على الواجب والممتنع، وأيضاً لا نسلم إمكان تعقل ذات الفعل بالكنه، وأما التعقل بالوجه فلا يجد فإن قلت: لم ينقض الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض . قلت : لأن الحسن والقبح إذا لم يكونا لذات الفعل أو لصفة من صفاته بل بالشرع لا يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين به بل يكونان بمجرد اعتبار الشرع، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض بخلاف ما إذا كانا لذاته أو لصفته فإنهما حينئذ يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين بالفعل سواء اعتبرهما معتبر أو لا، فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، كذا في شرح الصحائف، هذا وقد حقق المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الأصول أن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له فلينظر فيه .
The fourth: That the act is ugly is neither its essence nor a part of it, because it is attached to it without being it, but rather additional to it. And that it exists because it is the opposite of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, so then the subsistence of the attribute (which is ugliness) upon the attribute (which is the act) would follow. We said: The discussion of its premises has preceded, for the opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent, and the lifting of two opposites is only impossible in truth, not in existence. Also, we do not accept the impossibility of the subsistence of an accident upon an accident, since no proof has been established for it, as you know, and it is also contradicted by contingency and originatedness, for this proof that you have advanced that ugliness is an existent thing applies to them as well, while they are attributive. (The commentator says:) He said: (conditioned by the other being joined to it) for if you say: when the first exists for joining, and when the other exists the first is already gone? I say: What is meant is conditioned by its being such that the other joins it. If you say: the discussion is about the whole, so how does it subsist in every letter with the mentioned condition? I say: The ugliness of the whole is the totality of the ugliness of the parts, so when the described (thing) exists successively, the attribute also exists likewise. He said: (And also we do not accept the impossibility etc.) And also, what has the letter of negation, it does not follow from its subsistence in the non-existent that it is pure privation, because it may be a universal concept that applies to some individuals that are existent and some that are non-existent, like the impossible which applies to the Necessary and the non-existent. Also, we do not accept the possibility of comprehending the essence of the act in its reality, but comprehension in terms of aspect does not help. For if you say: the argument is not invalidated by that it requires that the act not be qualified by legal goodness due to the necessity of the subsistence of an accident upon an accident? I say: Because goodness and ugliness, if they are not by the essence of the act or by a quality of it but by the Law, then they are not real accidents subsisting in it but rather by mere consideration of the Law, so the subsistence of an accident upon an accident does not follow, unlike if they were by its essence or by its quality, for then they would be real accidents subsisting in the act, whether a considerer considers them or not, so the subsistence of an accident upon an accident would follow. Thus in Sharh al-Sahā'if. And the Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani has verified in Sharh al-Usul that legal goodness upon investigation is eternal, not an accident, and is related to the act without being a quality of it, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning the nature of ugliness (qubḥ). The argument states that the ugliness of an act is neither the act's essence nor a part of it, but an additional attribute. The proponent claims ugliness must exist as a positive attribute because it is the opposite (naqīḍ) of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, and that this leads to the subsistence of an accident upon an accident (‘araḍ ‘alā ‘araḍ). The response refutes this: the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent; the impossibility of two opposites being lifted pertains only to truth, not to existence. Furthermore, the impossibility of an accident subsisting on an accident is not proven and is contradicted by concepts like contingency (imkān) and origination (ḥudūth), which are attributive (i‘tibārī) yet discussed as if they are positive. The commentator elaborates on conditions of joining letters and explains that the ugliness of the whole is the sum of uglinesses of parts. Another point: the universal concept of 'impossible' applies to both the Necessary Being (existent) and the non-existent, so it is not purely privative. Regarding the worry that this leads to accident upon accident in legal (shar‘ī) goodness, the response distinguishes: if goodness/uGliness are by the Law, they are not real accidents but legal considerations; if they were by essence, they would be real accidents, leading to accident upon accident. The commentator cites al-Taftazani's verification that legal goodness is eternal (qadīm), not an accident.

Key Terms
قبح (qubḥ)
نقيض (naqīḍ)
عدم (‘adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opposite of a non-existent (e.g., non-ugliness) must be an existent positive attribute.
Correction: The opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent; the impossibility of lifting two opposites concerns truth, not existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation to the claim that ugliness must be a positive attribute because it is the opposite of non-ugliness?
A) The opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent.
B) Ugliness is not an accident.
C) The act itself is non-existent.
D) The attribute of ugliness is the same as the act.

Key Takeaway
The claim that ugliness is a positive attribute because it is the opposite of non-ugliness fails because opposites do not imply existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider an action you've done as ugly, do you treat that ugliness as a real, separate thing attached to the action? How does the refutation in this lesson—that the opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent—challenge you to reconsider whether your judgment is about the action itself or your perception of it?
?
Think of a belief you hold about what is 'ugly' or 'good'. Have you ever assumed that because something is not good, it must be positively bad? How can the lesson's insight—that opposites do not imply existence—change how you evaluate your own moral assessments?

Accident upon Accident: Objection and Rebuttal
Refutation of the impossibility of accident upon accident using examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of accident upon accident and how the rebuttal uses contingency and the nature of universal concepts to defend the possibility.

Core Idea
Accident upon accident is not impossible, and is even found in contingency and origination.

Having shown that the alleged necessity of existence for ugliness is unsound, the text now turns to a deeper objection: if ugliness is an accident, does it not lead to an accident subsisting on another accident? The author refutes this by arguing that such subsistence is not proven impossible and that we actually accept it in other concepts like contingency and origination. This lesson unpacks those responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the flow of the argument: objection then multiple rebuttals.
Notice the phrase 'وأيضاً لا نسلم' introduces each rebuttal.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection is that if ugliness is an accident (attribute) of the act, then we have an accident subsisting on another accident, which is deemed impossible by some.
3
Examine the rebuttals
Three rebuttals are given: (1) The impossibility of accident upon accident is not proven; (2) It is contradicted by the examples of contingency (imkān) and origination (ḥudūth) which are attributive yet accepted; (3) The universal concept of 'impossible' applies to both existent and non-existent, so it is not purely privative.
Also note the commentator's elaboration on the condition for the subsistence of attributes in parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كونه أي كون الفعل ( قبيحاً ليس نفس ذاته ولا جزءاً منها ( لتعلقها دونه بل زائد ) عليها ( وأنه موجود لأنه نقيض اللاقبيح القائم بالمعدوم فيلزم ) حينئذ ( قيام المعنى ( الذي هو القبح ( بالمعنى ) الذي هو الفعل، (قلنا : قد سبق الكلام على مقدماته) فإن نقيض العدمي لا يجب أن يكون موجوداً وارتفاع النقيضين إنما يستحيل في الصدق دون الوجود وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض إذ لم يقم عليه دليل كما عرفت ( مع انتقاضه بالإمكان والحدوث ) فإن هذا الدليل الذي أوردتموه على كون القبح أمراً موجوداً جارٍ فيهما مع كونهما اعتباريين .
قوله : (بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فإن قلت: عندما وجد الأول لانضمام، وعندما وجد الآخر الأول لقبح قلت: المراد بشرط كونه بحيث ينضم إليه الآخر، فإن قلت: الكلام في الكل فكيف يقوم بكل حرف بالشرط المذكور . قلت: قبح الكل جملة قبحات الأجزاء فلما وجد الموصوف على التقضي وجد الصفة كذلك. قوله : ( وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع إلخ) وأيضاً ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من قيامه بالمعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي وبعضها عدمي كاللاممكن الصادق على الواجب والممتنع، وأيضاً لا نسلم إمكان تعقل ذات الفعل بالكنه، وأما التعقل بالوجه فلا يجد فإن قلت: لم ينقض الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض . قلت : لأن الحسن والقبح إذا لم يكونا لذات الفعل أو لصفة من صفاته بل بالشرع لا يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين به بل يكونان بمجرد اعتبار الشرع، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض بخلاف ما إذا كانا لذاته أو لصفته فإنهما حينئذ يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين بالفعل سواء اعتبرهما معتبر أو لا، فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، كذا في شرح الصحائف، هذا وقد حقق المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الأصول أن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له فلينظر فيه .
The fourth: That the act is ugly is neither its essence nor a part of it, because it is attached to it without being it, but rather additional to it. And that it exists because it is the opposite of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, so then the subsistence of the attribute (which is ugliness) upon the attribute (which is the act) would follow. We said: The discussion of its premises has preceded, for the opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent, and the lifting of two opposites is only impossible in truth, not in existence. This passage presents the fourth argument concerning the nature of ugliness (qubḥ). The argument states that the ugliness of an act is neither the act's essence nor a part of it, but an additional attribute. The proponent claims ugliness must exist as a positive attribute because it is the opposite (naqīḍ) of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, and that this leads to the subsistence of an accident upon an accident (‘araḍ ‘alā ‘araḍ). The response refutes this: the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent; the impossibility of two opposites being lifted pertains only to truth, not to existence. The commentator elaborates on conditions of joining letters and explains that the ugliness of the whole is the sum of uglinesses of parts. Another point: the universal concept of 'impossible' applies to both the Necessary Being (existent) and the non-existent, so it is not purely privative. Regarding the worry that this leads to accident upon accident in legal (shar‘ī) goodness, the response distinguishes: if goodness/uGliness are by the Law, they are not real accidents but legal considerations; if they were by essence, they would be real accidents, leading to accident upon accident. The commentator cites al-Taftazani's verification that legal goodness is eternal (qadīm), not an accident.

Key Terms
عرض (‘araḍ)

اعتباري (i‘tibārī)
إمكان (imkān)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accident upon accident is absolutely impossible and cannot occur in any form.
Correction: The passage shows that such subsistence is not proven impossible and is even accepted in concepts like contingency and origination.

Checkpoint

A) Ugliness and goodness
B) Contingency and originatedness
C) Essence and attribute
D) Existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of accident upon accident is not proven and is contradicted by common concepts like contingency and origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author refuses to accept a claim without proof. In what areas of your faith or daily life have you accepted beliefs without proof, simply because they were comfortable or convenient? What would it cost you to honestly re-examine those beliefs in light of clear evidence?
?
The passage explains that ugliness of a whole is the totality of ugliness of parts. When you judge your own character as 'good' or 'bad', do you base it on the sum of your individual actions? If you were to calculate that totality now, what would it reveal about your spiritual state, and what one action could you change today to shift that balance?
?
The concept of 'attributive' (iʻtibārī) challenges the idea that everything must be a real independent entity. Are there aspects of your worship or understanding of Allah that you treat as mere intellectual theories rather than realities that shape your daily decisions? How would your life change if you treated them as real accidents subsisting on your soul?

Legal Goodness: Not a Real Accident, but Divine Consideration
Clarification of legal vs. real goodness/ugliness to avoid accident upon accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between legal and essential goodness/ugliness and how it avoids the problem of accident upon accident.

Core Idea
Legal goodness is not a real accident but a divine consideration, so no accident upon accident.

A potential problem remains: if ugliness were an accident, then legal goodness (al-ḥasan al-shar‘ī) would also be an accident, leading to the unwelcome consequence. The text clarifies that legal goodness is not a real accident but a mere legal consideration, thus avoiding the problem. The lesson also notes al-Taftazani's verification that legal goodness is eternal. This is a crucial distinction in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
The passage presents an objection: does the argument not also imply that legal goodness leads to accident upon accident? Then the answer distinguishes between real and legal attributes.
Notice the structure: 'فإن قلت... قلت'
2
Understand the distinction
The key distinction: if goodness/ugliness is by the Law (shar'), it is not a real accident but a legal consideration; only if by essence would it be real.
3
Reflect on al-Taftazani's verification
Al-Taftazani's point that legal goodness is eternal (qadīm) and not an accident reinforces that it is not a real accident subsisting in the act.
This shows that legal goodness is not a created attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كونه أي كون الفعل ( قبيحاً ليس نفس ذاته ولا جزءاً منها ( لتعلقها دونه بل زائد ) عليها ( وأنه موجود لأنه نقيض اللاقبيح القائم بالمعدوم فيلزم ) حينئذ ( قيام المعنى ( الذي هو القبح ( بالمعنى ) الذي هو الفعل، (قلنا : قد سبق الكلام على مقدماته) فإن نقيض العدمي لا يجب أن يكون موجوداً وارتفاع النقيضين إنما يستحيل في الصدق دون الوجود وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض إذ لم يقم عليه دليل كما عرفت ( مع انتقاضه بالإمكان والحدوث ) فإن هذا الدليل الذي أوردتموه على كون القبح أمراً موجوداً جارٍ فيهما مع كونهما اعتباريين .
قوله : (بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فإن قلت: عندما وجد الأول لانضمام، وعندما وجد الآخر الأول لقبح قلت: المراد بشرط كونه بحيث ينضم إليه الآخر، فإن قلت: الكلام في الكل فكيف يقوم بكل حرف بالشرط المذكور . قلت: قبح الكل جملة قبحات الأجزاء فلما وجد الموصوف على التقضي وجد الصفة كذلك. قوله : ( وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع إلخ) وأيضاً ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من قيامه بالمعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي وبعضها عدمي كاللاممكن الصادق على الواجب والممتنع، وأيضاً لا نسلم إمكان تعقل ذات الفعل بالكنه، وأما التعقل بالوجه فلا يجد فإن قلت: لم ينقض الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض . قلت : لأن الحسن والقبح إذا لم يكونا لذات الفعل أو لصفة من صفاته بل بالشرع لا يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين به بل يكونان بمجرد اعتبار الشرع، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض بخلاف ما إذا كانا لذاته أو لصفته فإنهما حينئذ يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين بالفعل سواء اعتبرهما معتبر أو لا، فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، كذا في شرح الصحائف، هذا وقد حقق المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الأصول أن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له فلينظر فيه .
The fourth: That the act is ugly is neither its essence nor a part of it, because it is attached to it without being it, but rather additional to it. And that it exists because it is the opposite of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, so then the subsistence of the attribute (which is ugliness) upon the attribute (which is the act) would follow. We said: The discussion of its premises has preceded, for the opposite of a non-existent does not have to be existent, and the lifting of two opposites is only impossible in truth, not in existence. Also, we do not accept the impossibility of the subsistence of an accident upon an accident, since no proof has been established for it, as you know, and it is also contradicted by contingency and originatedness, for this proof that you have advanced that ugliness is an existent thing applies to them as well, while they are attributive. (The commentator says:) He said: (conditioned by the other being joined to it) for if you say: when the first exists for joining, and when the other exists the first is already gone? I say: What is meant is conditioned by its being such that the other joins it. If you say: the discussion is about the whole, so how does it subsist in every letter with the mentioned condition? I say: The ugliness of the whole is the totality of the ugliness of the parts, so when the described (thing) exists successively, the attribute also exists likewise. He said: (And also we do not accept the impossibility etc.) And also, what has the letter of negation, it does not follow from its subsistence in the non-existent that it is pure privation, because it may be a universal concept that applies to some individuals that are existent and some that are non-existent, like the impossible which applies to the Necessary and the non-existent. Also, we do not accept the possibility of comprehending the essence of the act in its reality, but comprehension in terms of aspect does not help. For if you say: the argument is not invalidated by that it requires that the act not be qualified by legal goodness due to the necessity of the subsistence of an accident upon an accident? I say: Because goodness and ugliness, if they are not by the essence of the act or by a quality of it but by the Law, then they are not real accidents subsisting in it but rather by mere consideration of the Law, so the subsistence of an accident upon an accident does not follow, unlike if they were by its essence or by its quality, for then they would be real accidents subsisting in the act, whether a considerer considers them or not, so the subsistence of an accident upon an accident would follow. Thus in Sharh al-Sahā'if. And the Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani has verified in Sharh al-Usul that legal goodness upon investigation is eternal, not an accident, and is related to the act without being a quality of it, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning the nature of ugliness (qubḥ). The argument states that the ugliness of an act is neither the act's essence nor a part of it, but an additional attribute. The proponent claims ugliness must exist as a positive attribute because it is the opposite (naqīḍ) of the non-ugliness that subsists in the non-existent, and that this leads to the subsistence of an accident upon an accident (‘araḍ ‘alā ‘araḍ). The response refutes this: the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent; the impossibility of two opposites being lifted pertains only to truth, not to existence. Furthermore, the impossibility of an accident subsisting on an accident is not proven and is contradicted by concepts like contingency (imkān) and origination (ḥudūth), which are attributive (i‘tibārī) yet discussed as if they are positive. The commentator elaborates on conditions of joining letters and explains that the ugliness of the whole is the sum of uglinesses of parts. Another point: the universal concept of 'impossible' applies to both the Necessary Being (existent) and the non-existent, so it is not purely privative. Regarding the worry that this leads to accident upon accident in legal (shar‘ī) goodness, the response distinguishes: if goodness/uGliness are by the Law, they are not real accidents but legal considerations; if they were by essence, they would be real accidents, leading to accident upon accident. The commentator cites al-Taftazani's verification that legal goodness is eternal (qadīm), not an accident.

Key Terms
حسن شرعي (shar‘ī goodness)
اعتباري (i‘tibārī)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That legal goodness is a real accident (like physical whiteness) that subsists in the act.
Correction: Legal goodness is not a real accident but a divine legal consideration, and according to al-Taftazani, it is eternal and not an accident at all.

Key Takeaway

The Fifth Argument: Cause of Ugliness Before the Act
Presents the fifth argument that the cause of ugliness precedes the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key premise of the fifth argument: the cause of moral ugliness must pre-exist the act and grounds its prohibition.

Core Idea
The cause of ugliness precedes the act, making the act prohibited.

The fifth argument in the debate over moral attributes focuses on the timing of the cause of ugliness. Theologians argued whether an act's wrongness is due to something before the act or the act itself. This passage introduces the claim that the cause is present before the act, establishing a foundation for the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice that the passage is incomplete, but the main claim is clear: the cause of ugliness is present before the act.
The text breaks off; we only have the premise.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states two things: (1) the cause of ugliness is present before the act, and (2) therefore the agent is not allowed to do it. Identify how the second follows from the first.
3
Consider the significance
Why does it matter that the cause precedes the act? This is a key point in the debate about whether moral qualities are intrinsic to acts or depend on prior factors. Reflect on how this premise could support a particular view of divine command or moral realism.
The full argument would develop this further.

Commentary
This short passage introduces the fifth argument. It asserts that the cause (‘illa) of ugliness (qubḥ) must be present before the act occurs; otherwise, there would be no reason for the act to be prohibited prior to its performance. The phrase 'therefore he is not allowed to do it' indicates that the prohibition (or moral judgment) is based on a pre-existing cause. The argument is incomplete in this window, as the text cuts off with 'for if not for that which necessitates...' The commentator likely continues with further analysis, but the available content only establishes the premise that the cause of ugliness precedes the act, which is a key point in the debate over whether moral qualities are grounded in the act itself or in prior considerations.

The Fourth Argument and the Core Response
The fourth argument and its immediate refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that ugliness is an existent attribute and the Ash'ari response that the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent and that accident-on-accident is not impossible.

Core Idea
The opposite of a non-existent is not necessarily existent.

The Mu'tazila argue that ugliness must be a real, existent attribute because it is the opposite of non-ugliness, which they claim exists in non-existents. This lesson examines the fourth argument and the initial Ash'ari rebuttal, which challenges the logical necessity of an existent opposite and the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure of the argument and the counter-argument.
Notice that the response uses a direct refutation of the premises.
2
Identify the key logical moves
Focus on the claim that 'the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent' and the rejection of the impossibility of accident-on-accident.
The response also brings examples of possibility and origination.
3
Connect to broader kalam debates
Consider why this argument matters for the debate on divine attributes and moral values.
This is a recurring theme in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كونه أي كون الفعل قبيحاً ليس نفس ذاته ولا جزءاً منها لتعلقها دونه بل زائد عليها وأنه موجود لأنه نقيض اللاقبيح القائم بالمعدوم فيلزم حينئذ قيام المعنى الذي هو القبح بالمعنى الذي هو الفعل، قلنا : قد سبق الكلام على مقدماته فإن نقيض العدمي لا يجب أن يكون موجوداً وارتفاع النقيضين إنما يستحيل في الصدق دون الوجود وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض إذ لم يقم عليه دليل كما عرفت مع انتقاضه بالإمكان والحدوث فإن هذا الدليل الذي أوردتموه على كون القبح أمراً موجوداً جارٍ فيهما مع كونهما اعتباريين. قوله : (بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فإن قلت : عندما وجد الأول لانضمام، وعندما وجد الآخر الأول لقبح قلت : المراد بشرط كونه بحيث ينضم إليه الآخر، فإن قلت : الكلام في الكل فكيف يقوم بكل حرف بالشرط المذكور . قلت : قبح الكل جملة قبحات الأجزاء فلما وجد الموصوف على التقضي وجد الصفة كذلك. قوله : ( وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع إلخ) وأيضاً ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من قيامه بالمعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي وبعضها عدمي كاللاممكن الصادق على الواجب والممتنع، وأيضاً لا نسلم إمكان تعقل ذات الفعل بالكنه، وأما التعقل بالوجه فلا يجد فإن قلت: لم ينقض الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض . قلت : لأن الحسن والقبح إذا لم يكونا لذات الفعل أو لصفة من صفاته بل بالشرع لا يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين به بل يكونان بمجرد اعتبار الشرع، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض بخلاف ما إذا كانا لذاته أو لصفته فإنهما حينئذ يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين بالفعل سواء اعتبرهما معتبر أو لا، فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، كذا في شرح الصحائف، هذا وقد حقق المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الأصول أن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له فلينظر فيه.
The fourth: that the act's being ugly is neither its essence nor a part of it, because [the act] is attached to [the ugliness] without it, rather it is additional to it, and that it is existent because it is the opposite of non-ugliness which subsists in a non-existent, so there follows the subsistence of the meaning which is ugliness in the meaning which is the act. We said: The discussion of its premises has already passed, for the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent, and the removal of two opposites is only impossible in truth, not in existence. Additionally, we do not accept the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident, as no proof has been established for it, as you know, and it is refuted by possibility and origination, for this proof which you have brought that ugliness is an existent thing applies to them both while they are both mental considerations. He said: (on condition of the other's conjunction to it) — if you say: when the first exists for conjunction, and when the other exists the first is ugly, I say: the meaning is on condition that it is such that the other is conjoined to it. If you say: the discourse is about the whole, so how does it subsist in each letter with the mentioned condition? I say: the ugliness of the whole is the sum of the uglinesses of the parts, so when the described is found upon the condition, the attribute is found likewise. He said: (And also we do not accept the impossibility, etc.) — and also what contains a negation particle: from its subsistence in the non-existent it does not follow that it is a pure negation, because it may be a universal concept that applies to individuals some of which are existent and some non-existent, like the impossible which is true of the Necessary and the impossible. Also, we do not accept the possibility of conceiving the essence of the act in depth; as for conceiving it in a certain respect, it is not sufficient. If you say: the proof is not refuted, because it requires that the act not be described with legally good, due to the necessity of accident subsisting in accident. I say: because goodness and ugliness, if they are not for the essence of the act or for an attribute of it, but rather by the Law, they are not two real accidents subsisting in it, rather they are merely by the consideration of the Law, so the subsistence of an accident in an accident does not follow. Unlike if they are for its essence or for its attribute, then in that case they are two real accidents subsisting in the act, whether a considerer considers them or not, so the subsistence of an accident in an accident follows. Thus in Sharh al-Saha'if. And the investigator al-Taftazani has verified in Sharh al-Usul that the legally good, upon investigation, is eternal, not an accident, and is connected to the act, not an attribute of it, so let it be examined.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the fourth argument of the Mu'tazila that ugliness is a real attribute of the act, not the essence or a part of it, and that it is existent because it is the opposite of non-ugliness which subsists in a non-existent. The response argues that the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent, and that the impossibility of two opposites is only in truth, not in existence. It also rejects the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident, as no proof has been established for this, and the same logic would apply to possibility and origination, which are mental considerations. The commentary then addresses subsidiary points: the meaning of 'on condition of the other's conjunction' and the possibility that a concept like 'impossible' can apply to both existent and non-existent individuals. The response further distinguishes between real ugliness (based on essence) and legal ugliness (based on divine command), arguing that legal goodness and ugliness are not real accidents but mental considerations, thus avoiding the problem of accident-on-accident. Finally, al-Taftazani is cited to note that legal goodness is actually eternal and not an accident.

القبح
نقيض
معدوم
العرض بالعرض
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opposite of a non-existent must be existent.
Correction: The opposite of a non-existent can also be non-existent; the impossibility of two opposites is only in truth, not in existence.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the ugliness not have to be an existent attribute?
A) Because ugliness is not a real attribute.
B) Because the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent.
C) Because ugliness is a mental concept.
D) Because the act is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that ugliness is an existent attribute fails because the opposite of a non-existent is not necessarily existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your moral judgments (like 'this is wrong') as if they are fixed, existent realities? When was the last time you considered that such a judgment might be a mental consideration, not an objective entity? Will you examine one such judgment today and ask yourself whether it is a logical truth or an existential reality – and how that distinction might change your response?
?
The lesson shows that opposites in logic do not necessarily imply opposites in existence. Where in your life are you confusing logical certainty with existential truth? For example, do you assume that because you believe something is bad, it must be 'out there' as a real quality? How would your actions change if you recognized that some of your moral certainty is merely a mental consideration?
?
If accidents can subsist in accidents, then your own character traits may be interdependent, not independent. Consider a time you felt proud of a virtue (e.g., patience). Is that virtue attached to your essence or to another attribute (e.g., self-control)? Does that change how you cultivate or rely on it? What attribute will you consciously build upon another attribute this week?

Condition of Conjunction and the Negation Objection
Subsidiary points about condition of conjunction, whole-part, and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subsidiary discussions about the condition of conjunction and the response to the objection that a negation particle's meaning cannot be a real attribute.

Core Idea
A universal concept can apply to both existent and non-existent individuals.

After the initial response, the discussion delves into finer points: the condition under which an act is described as ugly, and an objection that a meaning containing a negation particle cannot be an existent attribute. This lesson explains these nuances and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on condition and negation
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the objection and response about the condition of conjunction and the negation particle.
The condition of conjunction is about the act being in a state to be conjoined with another.
2
Understand the whole-part argument
Notice how the ugliness of a whole is explained as the sum of ugliness of parts.
3
Analyze the negation objection
The objection that a meaning with a negation particle cannot be existent is countered by the example of 'impossible' which applies to both existent and non-existent.
This shows that a concept can be universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كونه أي كون الفعل قبيحاً ليس نفس ذاته ولا جزءاً منها لتعلقها دونه بل زائد عليها وأنه موجود لأنه نقيض اللاقبيح القائم بالمعدوم فيلزم حينئذ قيام المعنى الذي هو القبح بالمعنى الذي هو الفعل، قلنا : قد سبق الكلام على مقدماته فإن نقيض العدمي لا يجب أن يكون موجوداً وارتفاع النقيضين إنما يستحيل في الصدق دون الوجود وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض إذ لم يقم عليه دليل كما عرفت مع انتقاضه بالإمكان والحدوث فإن هذا الدليل الذي أوردتموه على كون القبح أمراً موجوداً جارٍ فيهما مع كونهما اعتباريين. قوله : (بشرط انضمام الآخر إليه فإن قلت : عندما وجد الأول لانضمام، وعندما وجد الآخر الأول لقبح قلت : المراد بشرط كونه بحيث ينضم إليه الآخر، فإن قلت : الكلام في الكل فكيف يقوم بكل حرف بالشرط المذكور . قلت : قبح الكل جملة قبحات الأجزاء فلما وجد الموصوف على التقضي وجد الصفة كذلك. قوله : ( وأيضاً لا نسلم امتناع إلخ) وأيضاً ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من قيامه بالمعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي وبعضها عدمي كاللاممكن الصادق على الواجب والممتنع، وأيضاً لا نسلم إمكان تعقل ذات الفعل بالكنه، وأما التعقل بالوجه فلا يجد فإن قلت: لم ينقض الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض . قلت : لأن الحسن والقبح إذا لم يكونا لذات الفعل أو لصفة من صفاته بل بالشرع لا يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين به بل يكونان بمجرد اعتبار الشرع، فلا يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض بخلاف ما إذا كانا لذاته أو لصفته فإنهما حينئذ يكونان عرضين حقيقيين قائمين بالفعل سواء اعتبرهما معتبر أو لا، فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، كذا في شرح الصحائف، هذا وقد حقق المحقق التفتازاني في شرح الأصول أن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له فلينظر فيه.
The fourth: that the act's being ugly is neither its essence nor a part of it, because [the act] is attached to [the ugliness] without it, rather it is additional to it, and that it is existent because it is the opposite of non-ugliness which subsists in a non-existent, so there follows the subsistence of the meaning which is ugliness in the meaning which is the act. We said: The discussion of its premises has already passed, for the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent, and the removal of two opposites is only impossible in truth, not in existence. Additionally, we do not accept the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident, as no proof has been established for it, as you know, and it is refuted by possibility and origination, for this proof which you have brought that ugliness is an existent thing applies to them both while they are both mental considerations. He said: (on condition of the other's conjunction to it) — if you say: when the first exists for conjunction, and when the other exists the first is ugly, I say: the meaning is on condition that it is such that the other is conjoined to it. If you say: the discourse is about the whole, so how does it subsist in each letter with the mentioned condition? I say: the ugliness of the whole is the sum of the uglinesses of the parts, so when the described is found upon the condition, the attribute is found likewise. He said: (And also we do not accept the impossibility, etc.) — and also what contains a negation particle: from its subsistence in the non-existent it does not follow that it is a pure negation, because it may be a universal concept that applies to individuals some of which are existent and some non-existent, like the impossible which is true of the Necessary and the impossible. Also, we do not accept the possibility of conceiving the essence of the act in depth; as for conceiving it in a certain respect, it is not sufficient. If you say: the proof is not refuted, because it requires that the act not be described with legally good, due to the necessity of accident subsisting in accident. I say: because goodness and ugliness, if they are not for the essence of the act or for an attribute of it, but rather by the Law, they are not two real accidents subsisting in it, rather they are merely by the consideration of the Law, so the subsistence of an accident in an accident does not follow. Unlike if they are for its essence or for its attribute, then in that case they are two real accidents subsisting in the act, whether a considerer considers them or not, so the subsistence of an accident in an accident follows. Thus in Sharh al-Saha'if. And the investigator al-Taftazani has verified in Sharh al-Usul that the legally good, upon investigation, is eternal, not an accident, and is connected to the act, not an attribute of it, so let it be examined.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the fourth argument of the Mu'tazila that ugliness is a real attribute of the act, not the essence or a part of it, and that it is existent because it is the opposite of non-ugliness which subsists in a non-existent. The response argues that the opposite of a non-existent need not be existent, and that the impossibility of two opposites is only in truth, not in existence. It also rejects the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident, as no proof has been established for this, and the same logic would apply to possibility and origination, which are mental considerations. The commentary then addresses subsidiary points: the meaning of 'on condition of the other's conjunction' and the possibility that a concept like 'impossible' can apply to both existent and non-existent individuals. The response further distinguishes between real ugliness (based on essence) and legal ugliness (based on divine command), arguing that legal goodness and ugliness are not real accidents but mental considerations, thus avoiding the problem of accident-on-accident. Finally, al-Taftazani is cited to note that legal goodness is actually eternal and not an accident.

Key Terms
بشرط انضمام
الكل
جزء
حرف النفي
مفهوم كلي
لاممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example is given to show that a concept can apply to both existent and non-existent?
A) Ugliness
B) The impossible (al-mumtani‘)
C) Origination
D) Non-ugliness

Key Takeaway
A concept can be universal, applying to both existent and non-existent, so a negation particle does not entail pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often judge actions as right or wrong without considering the conditions that make them so. After this lesson, will you pause to examine whether your judgment depends on a hidden condition of conjunction? How will you change your habit of rushing to moral condemnation?
?
The lesson shows that concepts like 'justice' apply even to non-existent situations. When you claim to believe in justice, do you treat it as a universal concept that binds you even when no one is watching? What one area of your life have you been excusing from that standard?
?
If the impossible can be predicated of God (as a universal), then even your understanding of what is impossible for God must be rooted in a real concept, not mere negation. How does this challenge your lazy reliance on saying 'God cannot do evil' as a mere negation? Will you now strive to see that as a positive description of His perfection?

The Distinction Between Real and Legal Ugliness
Objection on legal ugliness and the real vs legal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real ugliness (based on essence) and legal ugliness (based on divine command), and how this resolves the accident-on-accident problem.

Core Idea
Legal goodness and ugliness are mere considerations of the Law, not real accidents.

The final part of the response addresses the objection that if ugliness is a real accident, then an act cannot be legally good or bad without leading to accident-on-accident. The reply distinguishes between real ugliness (intrinsic) and legal ugliness (by divine command), showing that the latter does not involve real accidents. The lesson also includes al-Taftazani's note that legal goodness is eternal and not an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response about legal ugliness
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the objection and the distinction made.
Notice the conditional: 'if they are for the essence... then they are real accidents'.
2
Understand the resolution
Legal ugliness is not a real accident but a mere consideration of the Law, so no accident-on-accident arises.
3
Consider Taftazani's refinement
Taftazani adds that legal goodness is eternal and not an accident, which further removes the problem.
This is an advanced point.

According to the response, why does legal ugliness not entail accident-on-accident?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Taftazani says legal goodness is eternal, not an accident. How does this truth reshape your view of the Qur’an’s commands? Are they timeless realities that define your very purpose, or temporary labels? What will you change in your attitude toward a specific command you struggle with?

The Fifth Argument: Cause of Ugliness Before the Act
Fifth argument and response on the cause of ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the cause of an act's ugliness must precede the act, and the rebuttal that the preventer of the act is the intellect's judgment, not a pre-existing quality.

Core Idea
Ugliness is a judgment of the intellect, not a pre-existing attribute.

This lesson examines a pivotal debate: does the ugliness of an act require a cause that exists before the act itself? The Mu'tazilites argue yes, but this leads to the problem of attributes subsisting in non-existence. The response clarifies that ugliness is not an existential attribute but a rational judgment that arises when the act occurs, and it is this judgment that prevents the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the argument and response unfold in their own terms.
Notice the two parts: the Mu'tazilite claim and the rebuttal starting with 'قلنا'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite argument
Identify why the Mu'tazilites say the cause of ugliness must exist before the act. What follows if the cause is an existential attribute?
The argument leads to the problem of a real quality inhering in the non-existent.
3
Examine the alternative formulation
The passage also presents an alternative: if ugliness were essential, it would mean the effect (ugliness) precedes its cause (the act). Understand this logical problem.
This is a separate attempt to force the opponent's position into contradiction.
4
Study the response
The response denies that ugliness or its cause is present before the act. Instead, the intellect judges the act as ugly when it occurs, and this judgment prevents the act.
This shifts the focus from an existential attribute to a rational assessment.
5
Reflect on the ancient Mu'tazilite view
The passage mentions that some ancient Mu'tazilites held essences to be fixed in pre-eternity, which allows attributes to be ascribed even in non-existence. Consider how this relates to the main argument.
This is a side point that mitigates the problem for that specific school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : علة القبح حاصلة قبل الفعل ولذلك ليس له أن يفعله فلولا أن ما يقتضي قبحه حاصل قبل وجوده لم يكن كذلك، ويلزم حينئذ قيام الصفة الحقيقية بالمعدوم لأن مقتضى القبح صفة وجودية وقد يقال : لو كان القبح ذاتياً لزم تقدم المعلول على علته لأن قبيح الفعل حاصل قبله لما عرفت وعلته إما ذات الفعل وصفته وليس شيء منهما حاصلا قبله . قلنا : لا نسلم أن القبح أو علته حاصل قبل الفعل بل يحكم العقل باتصافه بالقبح وبما يقتضيه إذا حصل وهذا الحكم هو المانع من فعله والإقدام عليه لاتصافه بالقبح أو بما يقتضيه على أن القدماء منهم زعموا أن الذوات ثابتة متقررة في الأزل فيصح عندهم اتصافها بالصفات الثبوتية.
The fifth: the cause of ugliness is obtained before the act, and therefore he is not allowed to do it. For if that which necessitates its ugliness were not obtained before its existence, it would not be so. And there follows then the subsistence of the real attribute in the non-existent, because the requirement of ugliness is an existential attribute. And it may be said: if ugliness were essential, then the effect would precede its cause, because the ugliness of the act is obtained before it, as you know, and its cause is either the essence of the act or its attribute, and nothing of them is obtained before it. We said: we do not accept that ugliness or its cause is obtained before the act; rather, the intellect judges its being described with ugliness and with what it requires when it occurs, and this judgment is what prevents him from doing it and proceeding to it due to its being described with ugliness or with what it requires. Moreover, the ancients among them held that essences are fixed and established in pre-eternity, so according to them it is correct that they be described with positive attributes.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth Mu'tazilite argument that the cause of an act's ugliness must exist before the act itself; otherwise, the act would not be ugly. This leads to the problem of a real attribute subsisting in a non-existent entity, since the cause is a real quality. Alternatively, if ugliness were essential, it would mean the effect (ugliness) precedes its cause (the act). The response denies that ugliness or its cause is present before the act; instead, the intellect makes a judgment that the act is ugly once it occurs, and this judgment is what prevents the agent from performing it. It also notes that some ancient Mu'tazilites believed essences are eternally fixed, allowing them to be described with attributes even in non-existence, which might mitigate the problem.

Key Terms
علة القبح
صفة وجودية
ذاتي
حكم العقل
القدماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the response admits that ugliness is a real quality that exists before the act.
Correction: The response clarifies that ugliness is not an existential attribute; it is a rational judgment of the intellect that arises when the act occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what actually prevents the agent from performing an ugly act?
A) The ugliness itself existing before the act.
B) The intellect's judgment that the act is ugly when it occurs.
C) The divine command forbidding the act.
D) The nature of the agent being good.

Key Takeaway
The preventer of an ugly act is not a pre-existing attribute but the intellect's judgment of ugliness when the act occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If ugliness is simply a judgment of your intellect at the moment an act occurs, then when you decide not to do something you consider ugly, is it because you truly believe it is ugly, or because you fear consequences? How can you train your intellect to judge morally clearly, especially when your desires pull you otherwise?
?
The passage shows that the intellect's judgment is what prevents the agent from acting. Think of a time you knew something was ugly (sinful) but did it anyway. What does that reveal about your intellect's judgment? Are you willing to submit your actions to the verdict of your intellect, or do you silence it with excuses?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that ugliness must have a cause before the act. The author responds that it is a judgment at the time of the act. How does this affect your understanding of God's actions? Do you ever question the wisdom of God's commands because they seem 'ugly' to you? How does this lesson challenge you to trust that God's intellect is perfect, even when yours cannot grasp the full picture?

The Mu'tazila's First Real Argument: Universal Agreement
First real way of the Mu'tazila: universal agreement on qubh, and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument from universal agreement for the objectivity of good and evil, and the response that distinguishes between rational and nominal senses.

Core Idea
Universal moral agreement does not prove objective qubh in the contested sense.

The Mu'tazila claimed that all people, regardless of religion or custom, agree on certain moral judgments like the ugliness of injustice. This lesson examines that argument and the response that such agreement is only in the sense of natural compatibility, not in the disputed sense of real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's argument
Read the passage describing the universal agreement on moral judgments and how the Mu'tazila use this to claim objective qubh.
Notice they exclude Christians on trinitarianism and the reasoning of non-religious people.
2
Identify the response's distinction
The response introduces two senses of 'good' and 'evil': one based on natural compatibility (fitra) and the other on real attributes. Identify which sense is acknowledged and which is denied.
3
Consider the alternative explanation
The response suggests that a universal custom could be the origin of the shared certainty. Reflect on how this undermines the Mu'tazila's argument.
